Suppose that $f$ is bounded, and consider these three conditions:
(i) There is an $r$ with the property that for each $\epsilon$ there is a $\delta$ such that (17.1) holds if $\left(I_{i}\right)$ partitions $[0,1]$ into subintervals with $\lambda\left(I_{i}\right)<\delta$ and if $x_{i} \in I_{i}$.
(ii) $R_{*} f=R^{*} f$.
(iii) If $D_{f}$ is the set of points of discontinuity of $f$, then $\lambda\left(D_{f}\right)=0$.

The conditions are equivalent.
17.1. Prove:
(a) $D_{f}$ is a Borel set.
(b) (i) implies (ii).
(c) (ii) implies (iii).
(d) (iii) implies (i).
(e) The $r$ of (i) must coincide with the $R_{*} f=R^{*} f$ of (ii).

A note on terminology. An $f$ on the general ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, \mu$ ) is defined to be integrable not if (15.12) holds, but if (16.1) does. And an $f$ on [ 0,1 ] is defined to be integrable with respect to Lebesgue measure not if $L_{*} f=L^{*} f$ holds, but, rather, if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{0}^{1}|f(x)| d x<\infty \tag{17.24}
\end{equation*}
$$

does. The condition $L_{*} f=L^{*} f$ is not at issue, since for bounded $f$ it always holds if $f$ is a Borel function, and in this book $f$ is always assumed to be a Borel function unless the contrary is explicitly stated. For the Lebesgue integral, the question is whether $f$ is small enough that (17.24) holds, not whether it is sufficiently regular that $L_{*} f=L^{*} f$. For the Riemann integral, the terminology is different because $R_{*} f<R^{*} f$ holds for all sorts of important Borel functions, and one way to define Riemann integrability is to require $R_{*} f=R^{*} f$. In the context of general integration theory, one occasionally looks at the Riemann integral, but mostly for illustration and comparison.
17.2. 3.15 $17.1 \uparrow$ (a) Show that an indicator $I_{A}$ for $A \subset[0,1]$ is Riemann integrable if and only if $A$ is Jordan measurable.
(b) Find a Riemann integrable function that is not a Borel function.
17.3. Extend Theorem 17.1 to $R^{k}$.
17.4. Show that if $f$ is integrable, then

$$
\lim _{t \rightarrow 0} \int|f(x+t)-f(x)| d x=0
$$

Use Theorem 17.1.
17.5. Suppose that $\mu$ is a finite measure on $\mathscr{R}^{k}$ and $A$ is closed. Show that $\mu(x+A)$ is upper semicontinuous in $x$ and hence measurable.
17.6. Suppose that $\int_{0}^{\infty}|f(x)| d x<\infty$. Show that for each $\epsilon, \lambda[x: x>\alpha,|f(x)|>\epsilon] \rightarrow 0$ as $\alpha \rightarrow \infty$. Show by example that $f(x)$ need not go to 0 as $x \rightarrow \infty$ (even if $f$ is continuous).
17.7. Let $f_{n}(x)=x^{n-1}-2 x^{2 n-1}$. Calculate and compare $\int_{0}^{1} \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} f_{n}(x) d x$ and $\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \int_{0}^{1} f_{n}(x) d x$. Relate this to Theorem 16.6 and to the corollary to Theorem 16.7.
17.8. Show that $\left(1+y^{2}\right)^{-1}$ has equal integrals over $(-\infty,-1),(-1,0),(0,1),(1, \infty)$. Conclude from (17.9) that $\int_{0}^{1}\left(1+y^{2}\right)^{-1} d y=\pi / 4$. Expand the integrand in a geometric series and deduce Leibniz's formula

$$
\frac{\pi}{4}=1-\frac{1}{3}+\frac{1}{5}-\frac{1}{7}+\cdots
$$

by Theorem 16.7 (note that its corollary does not apply).
17.9. Show that if $f$ is integrable, there exist continuous, integrable functions $g_{n}$ such that $g_{n}(x) \rightarrow f(x)$ except on a set of Lebesgue measure 0 . (Use Theorem 17.1(ii) with $\epsilon=n^{-2}$.)
17.10. 13.9 17.9 ↑ Let $f$ be a finite-valued Borel function over $[0,1]$ By the following steps, prove Lusin's theorem: For each $\epsilon$ there exists a continuous function $g$ such that $\lambda[x \in(0,1): f(x) \neq g(x)]<\epsilon$.
(a) Show that $f$ may be assumed integrable, or even bounded.
(b) Let $g_{n}$ be continuous functions converging to $f$ almost everywhere. Combine Egoroff's theorem and Theorem 12.3 to show that convergence is uniform on a compact set $K$ such that $\lambda((0,1)-K)<\epsilon$. The limit $\lim _{n} g_{n}(x)=f(x)$ must be continuous when rcstricted to $K$.
(c) Exhibit $(0,1)-K$ as a disjoint union of open intervals $I_{k}$ [A12], define $g$ as $f$ on $K$, and define it by linear interpolation on each $I_{k}$.
17.11. Suppose in (17.7) that $T^{\prime}$ exists and is continuous and $f$ is a Borel function, and suppose that $\int_{a}^{b}\left|f(T x) T^{\prime}(x)\right| d x<\infty$. Show in steps that $\int_{T(a, b)}|f(y)| d y<\infty$ and (17.8) holds. Prove this for (a) $f$ continuous, (b) $f=I_{[s, l]}$, (c) $f=I_{B}$, (d) $f$ simple, (e) $f \geq 0$, (f) $f$ general.
17.12. 16.12 ↑ Let $\mathscr{L}$ consist of the continuous functions on $R^{1}$ with compact support. Show that $\mathscr{L}$ is a vector lattice in the sense of Problem 11.4 and has the property that $f \in \mathscr{L}$ implies $f \wedge 1 \in \mathscr{L}$ (note that $1 \notin \mathscr{L}$ ). Show that the $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{F}$ generated by $\mathscr{L}$ is $\mathscr{R}^{1}$. Suppose $\Lambda$ is a positive linear functional on $\mathscr{L}$; show that $\Lambda$ has the required continuity property if and only if $f_{n}(x) \downarrow 0$ uniformly in $x$ implies $\Lambda\left(f_{n}\right) \rightarrow 0$. Show under this assumption on $\Lambda$ that there is a measure $\mu$ on $\mathscr{R}^{1}$ such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\Lambda(f)=\int f d \mu, \quad f \in \mathscr{L} \tag{17.25}
\end{equation*}
$$

Show that $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite and unique. This is a version of the Riesz representation theorem.
17.13. ↑ Let $\Lambda(f)$ be the Riemann integral of $f$, which does exist for $f$ in $\mathscr{L}$. Using the most elementary facts about Riemann integration, show that the $\mu$ determined by (17.25) is Lebesgue measure. This gives still another way of constructing Lebesgue measure.
17.14. $\uparrow$ Extend the ideas in the preceding two problems to $R^{k}$.

## SECTION 18. PRODUCT MEASURE AND FUBINI'S THEOREM

Let $(X, \mathscr{X})$ and $(Y, \mathscr{Y})$ be measurable spaces. For given measures $\mu$ and $\nu$ on these spaces, the problem is to construct on the Cartesian product $X \times Y$ a product measure $\pi$ such that $\pi(A \times B)=\mu(A) \nu(B)$ for $A \subset X$ and $B \subset Y$. In the case where $\mu$ and $\nu$ are Lebesgue measure on the line, $\pi$ will be Lebesgue measure in the plane. The main result is Fubini's theorem, according to which double integrals can be calculated as iterated integrals.

## Product Spaces

It is notationally convenient in this section to change from ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}$ ) to ( $X, \mathscr{X}$ ) and ( $Y, \mathscr{Y}$ ). In the product space $X \times Y$ a measurable rectangle is a product $A \times B$ for which $A \in \mathscr{X}$ and $B \in \mathscr{Y}$. The natural class of sets in $X \times Y$ to consider is the $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ generated by the measurable rectangles. (Of course, $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ is not a Cartesian product in the usual sense.)

Example 18.1. Suppose that $X=Y=R^{1}$ and $\mathscr{X}=\mathscr{Y}=\mathscr{R}^{1}$. Then a measurable rectangle is a Cartesian product $A \times B$ in which $A$ and $B$ are linear Borel sets. The term rectangle has up to this point been reserved for Cartesian products of intervals, and so a measurable rectangle is more general. As the measurable rectangles do include the ordinary ones and the latter generate $\mathscr{R}^{2}$, it follows that $\mathscr{R}^{2} \subset \mathscr{R}^{1} \times \mathscr{R}^{1}$. On the other hand, if $A$ is an interval, [ $B: A \times B \in \mathscr{R}^{2}$ ] contains $R^{1}\left(A \times R^{1}=\bigcup_{n}(A \times(-n, n]) \in\right. \mathscr{R}^{2}$ ) and is closed under the formation of proper differences and countable unions; thus it is a $\sigma$-field containing the intervals and hence the Borel sets. Therefore, if $B$ is a Borel set, $\left[A: A \times B \in \mathscr{R}^{2}\right]$ contains the intervals and hence, being a $\sigma$-field, contains the Borel sets. Thus all the measurable rectangles are in $\mathscr{R}^{2}$, and so $\mathscr{R}^{1} \times \mathscr{R}^{1}=\mathscr{R}^{2}$ consists exactly of the twodimensional Borel sets. $\square$

As this example shows, $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ is in general much larger than the class of measurable rectangles.

Theorem 18.1. (i) If $E \in \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$, then for each $x$ the set $[y:(x, y) \in E]$ lies in $\mathscr{Y}$ and for each $y$ the set $[x:(x, y) \in E]$ lies in $\mathscr{X}$.
(ii) If $f$ is measurable $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$, then for each fixed $x$ the function $f(x, \cdot)$ is measurable $\mathscr{Y}$, and for each fixed $y$ the function $f(\cdot, y)$ is measurable $\mathscr{X}$.

The set $[y:(x, y) \in E]$ is the section of $E$ determined by $x$, and $f(x, \cdot)$ is the section of $f$ determined by $x$.

Proof. Fix $x$, and consider the mapping $T_{x}: Y \rightarrow X \times Y$ defined by $T_{x} y=(x, y)$. If $E=A \times B$ is a measurable rectangle, $T_{x}^{-1} E$ is $B$ or $\varnothing$
according as $A$ contains $x$ or not, and in either case $T_{x}^{-1} E \in \mathscr{Y}$. By Theorem 13.1(i), $T_{x}$ is measurable $\mathscr{Y} / \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$. Hence $[y:(x, y) \in E]=T_{x}^{-1} E \in \mathscr{Y}$ for $E \in \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$. By Theorem 13.1(ii), if $f$ is measurable $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y} / \mathscr{R}^{1}$, then $f T_{x}$ is measurable $\mathscr{Y} / \mathscr{R}^{1}$. Hence $f(x, \cdot)=f T_{x}(\cdot)$ is measurable $\mathscr{Y}$. The symmetric statements for fixed $y$ are proved the same way. $\square$

## Product Measure

Now suppose that $(X, \mathscr{X}, \mu)$ and $(Y, \mathscr{Y}, \nu)$ are measure spaces, and suppose for the moment that $\mu$ and $\nu$ are finite. By the theorem just proved $\nu[y$ : $(x, y) \in E]$ is a well-defined function of $x$. If $\mathscr{L}$ is the class of $E$ in $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ for which this function is measurable $\mathscr{X}$, it is not hard to show that $\mathscr{L}$ is a $\lambda$-system. Since the function is $I_{A}(x) \nu(B)$ for $E=A \times B, \mathscr{L}$ contains the $\pi$-system consisting of the measurable rectangles. Hence $\mathscr{L}$ coincides with $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ by the $\pi-\lambda$ theorem. It follows without difficulty that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\pi^{\prime}(E)=\int_{X} \nu[y:(x, y) \in E] \mu(a x), \quad E \in \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}, \tag{18.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

is a finite measure on $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$, and similarly for

$$
\begin{equation*}
\pi^{n}(E)=\int_{Y} \mu[x:(x, y) \in E] \nu(d y), \quad E \in \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y} \tag{18.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

For measurable rectangles,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\pi^{\prime}(A \times B)=\pi^{\prime \prime}(A \times B)=\mu(A) \cdot \nu(B) . \tag{18.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

The class of $E$ in $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ for which $\pi^{\prime}(E)=\pi^{\prime \prime}(E)$ thus contains the measurable rectangles; since this class is a $\lambda$-system, it contains $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$. The common value $\pi^{\prime}(E)=\pi^{\prime \prime}(E)$ is the product measure sought.

To show that (18.1) and (18.2) also agree for $\sigma$-finite $\mu$ and $\nu$, let $\left\{A_{m}\right\}$ and $\left\{B_{n}\right\}$ be decompositions of $X$ and $Y$ into sets of finite measure, and put $\mu_{m}(A)=\mu\left(A \cap A_{m}\right)$ and $\nu_{n}(B)=\nu\left(B \cap B_{n}\right)$. Since $\nu(B)=\Sigma_{m} \nu_{m}(B)$, the integrand in (18.1) is measurable $\mathscr{X}$ in the $\sigma$-finite as well as in the finite case; hence $\pi^{\prime}$ is a well-defined measure on $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ and so is $\pi^{\prime \prime}$. If $\pi_{m n}^{\prime}$ and $\pi_{m n}^{\prime \prime}$ are (18.1) and (18.2) for $\mu_{m}$ and $\nu_{n}$, then by the finite case, already treated (see Example 16.5), $\pi^{\prime}(E)=\sum_{m n} \pi_{m n}^{\prime}(E)=\sum_{m n} \pi_{m n}^{\prime \prime}(E)=\pi^{\prime \prime}(E)$. Thus (18.1) and (18.2) coincide in the $\sigma$-finite case as well. Moreover, $\pi^{\prime}(A \times B)= \sum_{m n} \mu_{m}(A) \nu_{n}(B)=\mu(A) \nu(B)$.

Theorem 18.2. If $(X, \mathscr{X}, \mu)$ and $(Y, \mathscr{Y}, \nu)$ are $\sigma$-finite measure spaces, $\pi(E)=\pi^{\prime}(E)=\pi^{\prime \prime}(E)$ defines a $\sigma$-finite measure on $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$; it is the only measure such that $\pi(A \times B)=\mu(A) \cdot \nu(B)$ for measurable rectangles.

Proof. Only $\sigma$-finiteness and uniqueness remain to be proved. The products $A_{m} \times B_{n}$ for $\left\{A_{m}\right\}$ and $\left\{B_{n}\right\}$ as above decompose $X \times Y$ into measurable rectangles of finite $\pi$-measure. This proves both $\sigma$-finiteness and uniqueness, since the measurable rectangles form a $\pi$-system generating $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ (Theorem 10.3). $\square$

The $\pi$ thus defined is called product measure; it is usually denoted $\mu \times \nu$. Note that the integrands in (18.1) and (18.2) may be infinite for certain $x$ and $y$, which is one reason for introducing functions with infinite values. Note also that (18.3) in some cases requires the conventions (15.2).

## Fubini's Theorem

Integrals with respect to $\pi$ are usually computed via the formulas

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{X \times Y} f(x, y) \pi(d(x, y))=\int_{X}\left[\int_{Y} f(x, y) \nu(d y)\right] \mu(d x) \tag{18.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

and

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{X \times Y} f(x, y) \pi(d(x, y))=\int_{Y}\left[\int_{X} f(x, y) \mu(d x)\right] \nu(d y) . \tag{18.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

The left side here is a double integral, and the right sides are iterated integrals. The formulas hold very generally, as the following argument shows.

Consider (18.4). The inner integral on the right is

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{Y} f(x, y) \nu(d y) \tag{18.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

Because of Theorem 18.1(ii), for $f$ measurable $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ the integrand here is measurable $\mathscr{Y}$; the question is whether the integral exists, whether (18.6) is measurable $\mathscr{X}$ as a function of $x$, and whether it integrates to the left side of (18.4).

First consider nonnegative $f$. If $f=I_{E}$, everything follows from Theorem 18.2: (18.6) is $\nu[y:(x, y) \in E]$, and (18.4) reduces to $\pi(E)=\pi^{\prime}(E)$. Because of linearity (Theorem 15.1(iv)), if $f$ is a nonnegative simple function, then (18.6) is a linear combination of functions measurable $\mathscr{X}$ and hence is itself measurable $\mathscr{X}$; further application of linearity to the two sides of (18.4) shows that (18.4) again holds. The general nonnegative $f$ is the monotone limit of nonnegative simple functions; applying the monotone convergence theorem to (18.6) and then to each side of (18.4) shows that again $f$ has the properties required.

Thus for nonnegative $f$, (18.6) is a well-defined function of $x$ (the value $\infty$ is not excluded), measurable $\mathscr{X}$, whose integral satisfies (18.4). If one side of
(18.4) is infinite, so is the other; if both are finite, they have the same finite value.

Now suppose that $f$, not necessarily nonnegative, is integrable with respect to $\pi$. Then the two sides of (18.4) are finite if $f$ is replaced by $|f|$. Now make the further assumption that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{Y}|f(x, y)| \nu(d y)<\infty \tag{18.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all $x$. Then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{Y} f(x, y) \nu(d y)=\int_{Y} f^{+}(x, y) \nu(d y)-\int_{Y} f^{-}(x, y) \nu(d y) \tag{18.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

The functions on the right here are measurable $\mathscr{X}$ and (since $f^{+}, f^{-} \leq|f|$ ) integrable with respect to $\mu$, and so the same is true of the function on the left. Integrating out the $x$ and applying (18.4) to $f^{+}$and to $f^{-}$gives (18.4) for $f$ itself.

The set $A_{0}$ of $x$ satisfying (18.7) need not coincide with $X$, but $\mu\left(X--A_{0}\right)=0$ if $f$ is integrable with respect to $\pi$, because the function in (18.7) integrates to $\int|f| d \pi$ (Theorem 15.2(iii)). Now (18.8) holds on $A_{0}$, (18.6) is measurable $\mathscr{X}$ on $A_{0}$, and (18.4) again follows if the inner integral on the right is given some arbitrary constant value on $X-A_{0}$.

The same analysis applies to (18.5):
Theorem 18.3. Under the hypotheses of Theorem 18.2, for nonnegative $f$ the functions

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{Y} f(x, y) \nu(d y), \int_{X} f(x, y) \mu(d x) \tag{18.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

are measurable $\mathscr{X}$ and $\mathscr{Y}$, respectively, and (18.4) and (18.5) hold. If $f$ (not necessarily nonnegative) is integrable with respect to $\pi$, then the two functions (18.9) are finite and measurable on $A_{0}$ and on $B_{0}$, respectively, where $\mu(X- \left.A_{0}\right)=\nu\left(Y-B_{0}\right)=0$, and again (18.4) and (18.5) hold.

It is understood here that the inner integrals on the right in (18.4) and (18.5) are set equal to 0 (say) outside $A_{0}$ and $B_{0} .^{\dagger}$

This is Fubini's theorem; the part concerning nonnegative $f$ is sometimes called Tonelli's theorem. Application of the theorem usually follows a two-step procedure that parallels its proof. First, one of the iterated integrals is computed (or estimated above) with $|f|$ in place of $f$. If the result is finite,
${ }^{\dagger}$ Since two functions that are equal almost everywhere have the same integral, the theory of integration could be extended to functions that are only defined almost everywhere; then $A_{0}$ and $B_{0}$ would disappear from Theorem 18.3.
then the double integral (integral with respect to $\pi$ ) of $|f|$ must be finite, so that $f$ is integrable with respect to $\pi$; then the value of the double integral of $f$ is found by computing one of the iterated integrals of $f$. If the iterated integral of $|f|$ is infinite, $f$ is not integrable $\pi$.

Example 18.2. Let $D_{r}$ be the closed disk in the plane with center at the origin and radius $r$. By (17.12),

$$
\lambda_{2}\left(D_{r}\right)=\iint_{D_{r}} d x d y=\iint_{\substack{0<p \leq r \\ 0<0<2 \pi}} \rho d \rho d \theta
$$

The last integral can be evaluated by Fubini's theorem:

$$
\lambda_{2}\left(D_{r}\right)=2 \pi \int_{0}^{r} \rho d \rho=\pi r^{2}
$$

Example 18.3. Let $I=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-x^{2}} d x$. By Fubini's theorem applied in the plane and by the polar-coordinate formula,

$$
I^{2}=\iint_{R^{2}} e^{-\left(x^{2}+y^{2}\right)} d x d y=\iint_{\substack{\rho>0 \\ 0<\theta<2 \pi}} e^{-\rho^{2}} \rho d \rho d \theta
$$

The double integral on the right can be evaluated as an iterated integral by another application of Fubini's theorem, which leads to the famous formula

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-x^{2}} d x=\sqrt{\pi} \tag{18.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

As the integrand in this example is nonnegative, the question of integrability does not arise.

Example 18.4. It is possible by means of Fubini's theorem to identify the limit in (17.4). First,

$$
\int_{0}^{I} e^{-u x} \sin x d x=\frac{1}{1+u^{2}}\left[1-e^{-u t}(u \sin t+\cos t)\right]
$$

as follows by differentiation with respect to $t$. Since

$$
\int_{0}^{t}\left[\int_{0}^{\infty}\left|e^{-u x} \sin x\right| d u\right] d x=\int_{0}^{t}|\sin x| \cdot x^{-1} d x \leq t<\infty
$$

Fubini's theorem applies to the integration of $e^{-u x} \sin x$ over $(0, t) \times(0, \infty)$ :

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{0}^{t} \frac{\sin x}{x} d x & =\int_{0}^{t} \sin x\left[\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-u x} d u\right] d x \\
& =\int_{0}^{\infty}\left[\int_{0}^{t} e^{-u x} \sin x d x\right] d u \\
& =\int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{d u}{1+u^{2}}-\int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{e^{-u t}}{1+u^{2}}(u \sin t+\cos t) d u
\end{aligned}
$$

The next-to-last integral is $\pi / 2$ (see (17.9)), and a change of variable $u t=s$ shows that the final integral goes to 0 as $t \rightarrow \infty$. Therefore,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{\imath \rightarrow \infty} \int_{0}^{\imath} \frac{\sin x}{x} d x=\frac{\pi}{2} \tag{18.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

## Integration by Parts

Let $F$ and $G$ be two nondecreasing, right-continuous functions on an interval $[a, b]$, and let $\mu$ and $\nu$ be the corresponding measures:

$$
\mu(x, y]=F(y)-F(x), \quad \nu(x, y]=G(y)-G(x), \quad a \leq x \leq y \leq b .
$$

In accordance with the convention (17.22) write $d F(x)$ and $d G(x)$ in place of $\mu(d x)$ and $\nu(d x)$.

Theorem 18.4. If $F$ and $G$ have no common points of discontinuity in ( $a, b$ ], then

$$
\begin{align*}
& \int_{(a, b]} G(x) d F(x)  \tag{18.12}\\
& \quad=F(b) G(b)-F(a) G(a)-\int_{(a, b]} F(x) d G(x)
\end{align*}
$$

In brief: $\int G d F=\Delta F G-\int F d G$. This is one version of the partial integration formula.

Proof. Note first that replacing $F(x)$ by $F(x)-C$ leaves (18.12) un-changed-it merely adds and subtracts $C \nu(a, b]$ on the right. Hence (take $C=F(a)$ ) it is no restriction to assume that $F(x)=\mu(a, x]$ and no restriction to assume that $G(x)=\nu(a, x]$. If $\pi=\mu \times \nu$ is product measure in the plane,
then by Fubini's theorem,

$$
\begin{align*}
& \pi[(x, y): a<y \leq x \leq b]  \tag{18.13}\\
& \quad=\int_{(a, b]} \nu(a, x] \mu(d x)=\int_{(a, b]} G(x) d F(x)
\end{align*}
$$

and

$$
\begin{align*}
& \pi[(x, y): a<x \leq y \leq b]  \tag{18.14}\\
& \quad=\int_{(a, b]} \mu(a, y] \nu(d y)=\int_{(a, b]} F(y) d G(y)
\end{align*}
$$

The two sets on the left have as their union the square $S=(a, b] \times(a, b]$. The diagonal of $S$ has $\pi$-measure

$$
\pi[(x, y): a<x=y \leq b]=\int_{(a, b]} \nu\{x\} \mu(d x)=0
$$

because of the assumption that $\mu$ and $\nu$ share no points of positive measure. Thus the left sides of (18.13) and (18.14) add to $\pi(S)=\mu(a, b] \nu(a, b]= F(b) G(b)$. $\square$

Suppose that $\nu$ has a density $g$ with respect to Lebesgue measure and let $G(x)=c+\int_{a}^{x} g(t) d t$. Transform the right side of (18.12) by the formula (16.13) for integration with respect to a density; the result is

$$
\begin{align*}
& \int_{(a, \dot{b}]} G(x) d F(x)  \tag{18.15}\\
& \quad=F(b) G(b)-F(a) G(a)-\int_{a}^{b} F(x) g(x) d x
\end{align*}
$$

A consideration of positive and negative parts shows that this holds for any $g$ integrable over ( $a, b$ ].

Suppose further that $\mu$ has a density $f$ with respect to Lebesgue measure, and let $F(x)=c^{\prime}+\int_{a}^{x} f(t) d t$. Then (18.15) further reduces to

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{a}^{b} G(x) f(x) d x=F(b) G(b)-F(a) G(a)-\int_{a}^{b} F(x) g(x) d x \tag{18.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

Again, $f$ can be any integrable function. This is the classical formula for integration by parts.

Under the appropriate integrability conditions, ( $a, b$ ] can be replaced by an unbounded interval.

## Products of Higher Order

Suppose that $(X, \mathscr{X}, \mu),(Y, \mathscr{Y}, \nu)$, and $(Z, \mathscr{Y}, \eta)$ are three $\sigma$-finite measure spaces. In the usual way, identify the products $X \times Y \times Z$ and $(X \times Y) \times Z$. Let $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y} \times \mathscr{Z}$ be the $\sigma$-field in $X \times Y \times Z$ generated by the $A \times B \times C$ with $A, B, C$ in $\mathscr{X}, \mathscr{Y}, \mathscr{Z}$, respectively. For $C$ in $Z$, let $\mathscr{A}_{C}$ be the class of $E \in \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ for which $E \times C \in \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y} \times \mathscr{Z}$. Then $\mathscr{I}_{C}$ is a $\sigma$-field containing the measurable rectangles in $X \times Y$, and so $\mathscr{A}_{C}=\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$. Therefore, $(\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}) \times \mathscr{Z} \subset \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y} \times \mathscr{Z}$. But the reverse relation is obvious, and so $(\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}) \times \mathscr{Z}=\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y} \times \mathscr{Z}$.

Define the product $\mu \times \nu \times \eta$ on $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y} \times \mathscr{Z}$ as $(\mu \times \nu) \times \eta$. It gives to $A \times B \times C$ the value $(\mu \times \nu)(A \times B) \cdot \eta(C)=\mu(A) \nu(B) \eta(C)$, and it is the only measure that does. The formulas (18.4) and (18.5) extend in the obvious way.

Products of four or more components can clearly be treated in the same way. This leads in particular to another construction of Lebesgue measure in $R^{k}=R^{1} \times \cdots \times R^{1}$ (see Example 18.1) as the product $\lambda \times \cdots \times \lambda$ ( $k$ factors) on $\mathscr{R}^{k}=\mathscr{R}^{1} \times \cdots \times \mathscr{R}^{1}$. Fubini's theorem of course gives a practical way to calculate volumes:

Example 18.5. Let $V_{k}$ be the volume of the sphere of radius 1 in $R^{k}$; by Theorem 12.2, a sphere in $R^{k}$ with radius $r$ has volume $r^{k} V_{k}$. Let $A$ be the unit sphere in $R^{k}$, let $B=\left[\left(x_{1}, x_{2}\right): x_{1}^{2}+x_{2}^{2} \leq 1\right]$, and let $C\left(x_{1}, x_{2}\right)= \left[\left(x_{3}, \ldots, x_{k}\right): \sum_{i=3}^{k} \ldots x_{i}^{2} \leq 1-x_{1}^{2}-x_{2}^{2}\right]$. By Fubini's theorem,

$$
\begin{aligned}
V_{k} & =\int_{A} d x_{1} \cdots d x_{k}=\int_{B} d x_{1} d x_{2} \int_{C\left(x_{1}, x_{2}\right)} d x_{3} \cdots d x_{k} \\
& =\int_{B} d x_{1} d x_{2} V_{k-2}\left(1-x_{1}^{2}-x_{2}^{2}\right)^{(k-2) / 2} \\
& =V_{k-2} \int_{\substack{0<\theta<2 \pi \\
0<\rho<1}}\left(1-\rho^{2}\right)^{(k-2) / 2} \rho d \rho d \theta \\
& =\pi V_{k-2} \int_{0}^{1} t^{(k-2) / 2} d t=\frac{2 \pi V_{k-2}}{k}
\end{aligned}
$$

If $V_{0}$ is taken as 1 , this holds for $k=2$ as well as for $k \geq 3$. Since $V_{1}=2$, it follows by induction that

$$
V_{2 i-1}=\frac{2(2 \pi)^{i-1}}{1 \times 3 \times 5 \times \cdots \times(2 i-1)}, \quad V_{2 i}=\frac{(2 \pi)^{i}}{2 \times 4 \times \cdots \times(2 i)}
$$

for $i=1,2, \ldots$. Example 18.2 is a special case. $\square$

## PROBLEMS

18.1. Show by Theorem 18.1 that if $A \times B$ is nonempty and lies in $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$, then $A \in \mathscr{X}$ and $B \in \mathscr{Y}$.
18.2. $2.9 \uparrow$ Suppose that $X=Y$ is uncountable and $\mathscr{X}=\mathscr{Y}$ consists of the countable and the cocountable sets. Show that the diagonal $E=[(x, y): x=y]$ does not lie in $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$, even though $[y:(x, y) \in E] \in \mathscr{Y}$ and $[x:(x, y) \in E] \in \mathscr{X}$ for all $x$ and $y$.
18.3. $10.518 .1 \uparrow$ Let $(X, \mathscr{X}, \mu)=(Y, \mathscr{Y}, \nu)$ be the completion of $\left(R^{!}, \mathscr{P}^{!}, \lambda\right)$. Show that $(X \times Y, \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}, \mu \times \nu)$ is not complete.
18.4. The assumption of $\sigma$-finiteness in Theorem 18.2 is essential: Let $\mu$ be Lebesgue measure on the line, let $\nu$ be counting measure on the line, and take $E=[(x, y): x=y]$. Then (18.1) and (18.2) do not agree.
18.5. Example 18.2 in effect connects $\pi$ as the area of the unit disk $D_{1}$ with the $\pi$ of trigonometry.
(a) A second way: Calculate $\lambda_{2}\left(D_{1}\right)$ directly by Fubini's theorem: $\lambda_{2}\left(D_{1}\right)= \int_{-1}^{1} 2\left(1-x^{2}\right)^{1 / 2} d x$. Evaluate the integral by trigonometric substitution.
(b) A third way: Inscribe in the unit circle a regular polygon of $n$ sides. Its interior consists of $n$ congruent isosceles triangles with angle $2 \pi / n$ at the apex; the area is $n \sin (\pi / n) \cos (\pi / n)$, which goes to $\pi$.
18.6. Suppose that $f$ is nonnegative on a $\sigma$-finite measure space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, \mu$ ). Show that

$$
\int_{\Omega} f d \mu=(\mu \times \lambda)\left[(\omega, y) \in \Omega \times R^{\prime}: 0 \leq y \leq f(\omega)\right] .
$$

Prove that the set on the right is measurable. This gives the "area under the curve." Given the existence of $\mu \times \lambda$ on $\Omega \times R^{l}$, one can use the right side of this equation as an alternative definition of the integral.
18.7. Reconsider Problem 12.12.
18.8. Suppose that $\nu[y:(x, y) \in E]=\nu[y:(x, y) \in F]$ for all $x$, and show that $(\mu \times \nu)(E)=(\mu \times \nu)(F)$. This is a general version of Cavalieri's principle.
18.9. (a) Suppose that $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite, and prove the corollary to Theorem 16.7 by Fubini's theorem in the product of $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, \mu)$ and $(1,2, \ldots)$ with counting measure.
(b) Relate the series in Problem 17.7 to Fubini's theorem.
18.10. (a) Let $\mu=\nu$ be counting measure on $X=Y=\{1,2, \ldots\}$. If

$$
f(x, y)= \begin{cases}2-2^{-x} & \text { if } x=y \\ -2+2^{-x} & \text { if } x=y+1 \\ 0 & \text { otherwise }\end{cases}
$$

then the iterated integrals exist but are unequal. Why does this not contradict Fubini's theorem?
(b) Show that $x y /\left(x^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{2}$ is not integrable over the square $[(x, y)|x|,|y| \leq$ 1] even though the iterated integrals exist and are equal
18.11. Exhibit a case in which (18.12) fails because $F$ and $G$ have a common point of discontinuity.
18.12. Prove (18.16) for the case in which all the functions are continuous by differentiating with respect to the upper limit of integration.
18.13. Prove for distribution functions $F$ that $\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}(F(x+c)-F(x)) d x=c$.
18.14. Prove for continuous distribution functions that $\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} F(x) d F(x)=\frac{1}{2}$.
18.15. Suppose that a number $f_{n}$ is defined for each $n \geq n_{0}$ and put $F(x)= \Sigma_{n_{0} \leq n \leq x} f_{n}$. Deduce from (18.15) that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum_{n_{0} \leq n \leq x} G(n) f_{n}=F(x) G(x)-\int_{n_{0}}^{x} F(t) g(t) d t \tag{18.17}
\end{equation*}
$$

if $G(y)-G(x)=\int_{x}^{y} g(t) d t$, which will hold if $G$ has continuous derivative $g$. First assume that the $f_{n}$ are nonnegative.
18.16. $\uparrow$ Take $n_{0}=1, f_{7}=1$, and $G(x)=1 / x$, and derive $\sum_{n \leq x} n^{-1}=\log x+\gamma+ O(1 / x)$, where $\gamma=1-\int_{1}^{\infty}(t-|t|) t^{-2} d t$ is Euler's constant.
18.17. $5.2018 .15 \uparrow$ Use (18.17) and (5.51) to prove that there exists a constant $c$ such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum_{p \leq x} \frac{1}{p}=\log \log x+c+O\left(\frac{1}{\log x}\right) . \tag{18.18}
\end{equation*}
$$

18.18. Euler's gamma function is defined for positive $t$ by $\Gamma(t)=\int_{0}^{\infty} x^{t-1} e^{-x} d x$.
(a) Prove that $\Gamma^{(k)}(t)=\int_{0}^{\infty} x^{t-1}(\log x)^{k} e^{-x} d x$.
(b) Show by partial integration that $\Gamma(t+1)=t \Gamma(t)$ and hence that $\Gamma(n+1) =n!$ for integral $n$.
(c) From (18.10) deduce $\Gamma\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)=\sqrt{\pi}$.
(d) Show that the unit sphere in $R^{k}$ has volume (see Example 18.5)

$$
\begin{equation*}
V_{k}=\frac{\pi^{k / 2}}{\Gamma((k / 2)+1)} \tag{18.19}
\end{equation*}
$$

18.19. By partial integration prove that $\left.\int_{0}^{\infty}(\sin x) / x\right)^{2} d x=\pi / 2$ and $\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}(1- \cos x) x^{-2} d x=\pi$.
18.20. Suppose that $\mu$ is a probability measure on ( $X, \mathscr{X}$ ) and that, for each $x$ in $X$, $\nu_{x}$ is a probability measure on ( $Y, \mathscr{Y}$ ). Suppose further that, for each $B$ in $\mathscr{Y}$, $\nu_{x}(B)$ is, as a function of $x$, measurable $\mathscr{X}$. Regard the $\mu(A)$ as initial probabilities and the $\nu_{x}(B)$ as transition probabilities.
(a) Show that, if $E \in \mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$, then $\nu_{x}[y:(x, y) \in E]$ is measurable $\mathscr{X}$.
(b) Show that $\pi(E)=\int_{X} \nu_{x}[y:(x, y) \in E] \mu(d x)$ defines a probability measure on $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$. If $\nu_{x}=\nu$ does not depend on $x$, this is just (18.1).
(c) Show that if $f$ is measurable $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{Y}$ and nonnegative, then $\int_{Y} f(x, y) \nu_{x}(d y)$ is measurable $\mathscr{X}$. Show further that

$$
\int_{X \times Y} f(x, y) \pi(d(x, y))=\int_{X}\left[\int_{Y} f(x, y) \nu_{x}(d y)\right] \mu(d x)
$$

which extends Fubini's theorem (in the probability case). Consider also $f$ 's that may be negative.
(d) Let $\nu(B)=\int_{X} \nu_{x}(B) \mu(d x)$. Show that $\pi(X \times B)=\nu(B)$ and

$$
\int_{Y} f(y) \nu(d y)=\int_{X}\left[\int_{Y} f(y) \nu_{x}(d y)\right] \mu(d x)
$$

## SECTION 19. THE $L^{p}$ SPACES*

## Definitions

Fix a measure space $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, \mu)$. For $1 \leq p<\infty$, let $L^{p}=L^{p}(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, \mu)$ be the class of (measurable) real functions $f$ for which $|f|^{p}$ is integrable, and for $f$ in $L^{p}$, write

$$
\begin{equation*}
\|f\|_{p}=\left[\int|f|^{p} d \mu\right]^{1 / p} \tag{19.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

There is a special definition for the case $p=\infty$ : The essential supremum of $f$ is

$$
\begin{equation*}
\|f\|_{\infty}=\inf [\alpha: \mu[\omega:|f(\omega)|>\alpha]=0] \tag{19.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

take $L^{\infty}$ to consist of those $f$ for which this is finite. The spaces $L^{p}$ have a geometric structure that can usefully guide the intuition. The basic facts are laid out in this section, together with two applications to theoretical statistics.

[^0]For $1<p, q<\infty, p$ and $q$ are conjugate indices if $p^{-1}+q^{-1}=1 ; p$ and $q$ are also conjugate if one is 1 and the other is $\infty$ (formally, $1^{-1}+\infty^{-1}=1$ ). Hölder's inequality says that if $p$ and $q$ are conjugate and if $f \in L^{p}$ and $g \in L^{q}$, then $f g$ is integrable and

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left|\int f g d \mu\right| \leq \int|f g| d \mu \leq\|f\|_{p}\|g\|_{q} . \tag{19.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

This is obvious if $p=1$ and $q=\infty$. If $1<p, q<\infty$ and $\mu$ is a probability measure, and if $f$ and $g$ are simple functions, (19.3) is (5.35). But the proof in Section 5 goes over without change to the general case.

Minkowski's inequality says that if $f, g \in L^{p}(1 \leq p \leq \infty)$, then $f+g \in L^{p}$ and

$$
\begin{equation*}
\|f+g\|_{p} \leq\|f\|_{p}+\|g\|_{p} . \tag{19.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

This is clear if $p=1$ or $p=\infty$. Suppose that $1<p<\infty$. Since $|f+g| \leq 2\left(|f|^{p}\right. \left.+|g|^{p}\right)^{1 / p}, f+g$ does lie in $L^{p}$. Let $q$ be conjugate to $p$. Since $p-1=p / q$, Hölder's inequality gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
\|f+g\|_{p}^{p} & =\int|f+g|^{p} d \mu \\
& \leq \int|f| \cdot|f+g|^{p / q} d \mu+\int|g| \cdot|f+g|^{p / q} d \mu \\
& \leq\|f\|_{p} \cdot\left\||f+g|^{p / q}\right\|_{q}+\|g\|_{p} \cdot\left\||f+g|^{p / q}\right\|_{q} \\
& =\left(\|f\|_{p}+\|g\|_{p}\right)\left[\int|f+g|^{p} d \mu\right]^{1 / q} \\
& =\left(\|f\|_{p}+\|g\|_{p}\right)\|f+g\|_{p}^{p / q}
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $p-p / q=1$, (19.4) follows. ${ }^{\dagger}$
If $\alpha$ is real and $f \in L^{p}$, then obviously $\alpha f \in L^{p}$ and

$$
\begin{equation*}
\|\alpha f\|_{p}=|\alpha| \cdot\|f\|_{p} . \tag{19.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

Define a metric on $L^{p}$ by $d_{p}(f, g)=\|f-g\|_{p}$. Minkowski's inequality gives the triangle inequality for $d_{p}$, and $d_{p}$ is certainly symmetric. Further, $d_{p}(f, g)=0$ if and only if $|f-g|^{p}$ integrates to 0 , that is, $f=g$ almost everywhere. To make $L^{p}$ a metric space, identify functions that are equal almost everywhere.

[^1]If $\left\|f-f_{n}\right\|_{p} \rightarrow 0$ and $p<\infty$, so that $\int\left|f-f_{n}\right|^{p} d \mu \rightarrow 0$, then $f_{n}$ is said to converge to $f$ in the mean of order $p$.

If $f=f^{\prime}$ and $g=g^{\prime}$ almost everywhere, then $f+g=f^{\prime}+g^{\prime}$ almost everywhere, and for real $\alpha, \alpha f=\alpha f^{\prime}$ almost everywhere. In $L^{p}, f$ and $f^{\prime}$ become the same function, and similarly for the pairs $g$ and $g^{\prime}, f+g$ and $f^{\prime}+g^{\prime}$, and $\alpha f$ and $\alpha f^{\prime}$. This means that addition and scalar multiplication are unambiguously defined in $L^{p}$, which is thus a real vector space. It is a normed vector space in the sense that it is equipped with a norm $\|\cdot\|_{p}$ satisfying (19.4) and (19.5).

## Completeness and Separability

A normed vector space is a Banach space if it is complete under the corresponding metric. According to the Riesz-Fischer theorem, this is true of $L^{p}$ :

Theorem 19.1. The space $L^{p}$ is complete.

Proof. It is enough to show that every fundamental sequence contains a convergent subsequence. Suppose first that $p<\infty$. Assume that $\left\|f_{m}-f_{n}\right\|_{p} \rightarrow$ 0 as $m, n \rightarrow \infty$, and choose an increasing sequence $\left\{n_{k}\right\}$ so that $\left\|f_{m}-f_{n}\right\|_{p}^{p} \leq 2^{-(p+1) k}$ for $m, n \geq n_{k}$. Since $\int\left|f_{m}-f_{n}\right|^{p} d \mu \geq \alpha^{p} \mu\left[\left|f_{m}-f_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]$ (this is just a general version of Markov's inequality (5.31)), $\mu\left[\left|f_{n}-f_{m}\right| \geq 2^{-k}\right] \leq 2^{p k}\left\|f_{m}-f_{n}\right\|_{p}^{p} \leq 2^{-k}$ for $m, n \geq n_{k}$. Therefore, $\sum_{k} \mu\left[\left|f_{n_{k+1}}-f_{n_{k}}\right| \geq 2^{-k}\right]<\infty$, and it follows by the first Borel-Cantelli lemma (which works for arbitrary measures) that, outside a set of $\mu$-measure $0, \Sigma_{k}\left|f_{n_{k+1}}-f_{n_{k}}\right|$ converges. But then $f_{n_{k}}$ converges to some $f$ almost everywhere, and by Fatou's lemma, $\int\left|f-f_{n_{k}}\right|^{p} d \mu \leq \liminf _{i} \int\left|f_{n_{i}}-f_{n_{k}}\right|^{p} d \mu \leq 2^{-k}$. Therefore, $f \in L^{p}$ and $\left\|f-f_{n_{k}}\right\|_{p} \rightarrow 0$, as required.

If $p=\infty$, choose $\left\{n_{k}\right\}$ so that $\| f_{m}-\left.f_{n}\right|_{\infty} \leq 2^{-k}$ for $m, n \geq n_{k}$. Since $\left|f_{n_{k+1}}-f_{n_{k}}\right| \leq 2^{-k}$ almost everywhere, $f_{n_{k}}$ converges to some $f$, and $\left|f-f_{n_{k}}\right| \leq 2^{-k}$ almost everywhere. Again, $\left\|f-f_{n_{k}}\right\|_{p} \rightarrow 0$.

The next theorem has to do with separability.
Theorem 19.2. (i) Let $U$ be the set of simple functions $\sum_{i=1}^{m} \alpha_{i} I_{B_{i}}$ for $\alpha_{i}$ and $\mu\left(B_{i}\right)$ finite. For $1 \leq p \leq \infty, U$ is dense in $L^{p}$.
(ii) If $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite and $\mathscr{F}$ is countably generated, and if $p<\infty$, then $L^{p}$ is separable.

Proof. Proof of (i). Suppose first that $p<\infty$. For $f \in L^{p}$, choose (Theorem 13.5 ) simple functions $f_{n}$ such that $f_{n} \rightarrow f$ and $\left|f_{n}\right| \leq|f|$. Then $f_{n} \in L^{p}$, and by the dominated convergence theorem, $\int\left|f-f_{n}\right|^{p} d \mu \rightarrow 0$. Therefore,
$\left\|f-f_{n}\right\|_{p}<\epsilon$ for some $n$; but each $f_{n}$ is in $U$. As for the case $p=\infty$, if $n 2^{n}>\|f\|_{\infty}$, then the $f_{n}$ defined by (13.6) satisfies $\left\|f-f_{n}\right\|_{\infty} \leq 2^{-n}$ ( $<\epsilon$ for large $n$ ).

Proof of (ii). Suppose that $\mathscr{F}$ is generated by a countable class $\mathscr{b}$ and that $\Omega$ is covered by a countable class $\mathscr{D}$ of $\mathscr{F}$-sets of finite measure. Let $E_{1}, E_{2}, \ldots$ be an enumeration of $\mathscr{C} \cup \mathscr{D}$; let $\mathscr{P}_{n}(n \geq 1)$ be the partition consisting of the sets of the form $F_{1} \cap \cdots \cap F_{n}$, where $F_{i}$ is $E_{i}$ or $E_{i}^{c}$; and let $\mathscr{F}_{n}$ be the field of unions of the sets in $\mathscr{P}_{n}$ Then $\mathscr{F}_{0}=\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} \mathscr{F}_{n}$ is a countable field that generates $\mathscr{F}$, and $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite on $\mathscr{F}_{0}$. Let $V$ be the set of simple functions $\sum_{i=1}^{m} \alpha_{i} I_{A_{i}}$ for $\alpha_{i}$ rational, $A_{i} \in \mathscr{F}_{0}$, and $\mu\left(A_{i}\right)<\infty$.

Let $g=\sum_{i=1}^{m} \alpha_{i} I_{B_{i}}$ be the element of $U$ constructed in the proof of part (i). Then $\|f-g\|_{p}<\epsilon$, the $\alpha_{i}$ are rational by (13.6), and any $\alpha_{i}$ that vanish can be suppressed. By Theorem 11.4(ii), there exist sets $A_{i}$ in $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ such that $\mu\left(B_{i} \Delta A_{i}\right)<\left(\epsilon / m\left|\alpha_{i}\right|\right)^{p}$, provided $p<\infty$, and then $h=\sum_{i=1}^{m} \alpha_{i} I_{A_{i}}$ lies in $V$ and $\|f-h\|_{p}<2 \epsilon$. But $V$ is countable. ${ }^{\dagger}$ $\square$

## Conjugate Spaces

A linear functional on $L^{p}$ is a real-valued function $\gamma$ such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\gamma\left(\alpha f+\alpha^{\prime} f^{\prime}\right)=\alpha \gamma(f)+\alpha^{\prime} \gamma\left(f^{\prime}\right) . \tag{19.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

The functional is bounded if there is a finite $M$ such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
|\gamma(f)| \leq M \cdot\|f\|_{p} \tag{19.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all $f$ in $L^{p}$. A bounded linear functional is uniformly continuous on $L^{p}$ because $\left\|f-f^{\prime}\right\|_{p}<\epsilon / M$ implies $\left|\gamma(f)-\gamma\left(f^{\prime}\right)\right|<\epsilon$ (if $M>0$; and $M=0$ implies $\gamma(f) \equiv 0$ ). The norm $\|\gamma\|$ of $\gamma$ is the smallest $M$ that works in (19.7): $\|\gamma\|=\sup \left[|\gamma(f)| /\|f\|_{p}: f \neq 0\right]$.

Suppose $p$ and $q$ are conjugate indices and $g \in L^{q}$. By Hölder's inequality,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\gamma_{g}(\hat{f})=\int f g d \mu \tag{19.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

is defined for $f \in L^{p}$ and satisfies (19.7) if $M \geq\|g\|_{q}$; and $\gamma_{g}$ is obviously linear. According to the Riesz representation theorem, this is the most general bounded linear functional in the case $p<\infty$ :

Theorem 19.3. Suppose that $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite, that $1 \leq p<\infty$, and that $q$ is conjugate to $p$. Every bounded linear functional on $L^{p}$ has the form (19.8) for some $g \in L^{q}$; further,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left\|\gamma_{g}\right\|=\|g\|_{q}, \tag{19.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

and $g$ is unique up to a set of $\mu$-measure 0 .

[^2]The space of bounded linear functionals on $L^{p}$ is called the dual space, or the conjugate space, and the theorem identifies $L^{q}$ as the dual of $L^{p}$. Note that the theorem does not cover the case $p=\infty .^{\dagger}$

Proof. Case I: $\mu$ finite. For $A$ in $\mathscr{F}$, define $\varphi(A)=\gamma\left(I_{A}\right)$. The linearity of $\gamma$ implies that $\varphi$ is finitely additive. For the $M$ of (19.7), $|\varphi(A)| \leq M \cdot\left\|I_{A}\right\|_{p} =M \cdot|\mu(A)|^{1 / p}$. If $A=\cup_{n} A_{n}$, where the $A_{n}$ are disjoint, then $\varphi(A)= \sum_{n=1}^{N} \varphi\left(A_{n}\right)+\varphi\left(\bigcup_{n>N} A_{n}\right)$, and since $\left|\varphi\left(\bigcup_{n>N} A_{n}\right)\right| \leq M \mu^{1 / p}\left(\bigcup_{n>N} A_{n}\right) \rightarrow$ 0 , it follows that $\varphi$ is an additive set function in the sense of (32.1).

The Jordan decomposition (32.2) represents $\varphi$ as the difference of two finite measures $\varphi^{+}$and $\varphi^{-}$with disjoint supports $A^{+}$and $A^{-}$. If $\mu(A)=0$, then $\varphi^{+}(A)=\varphi\left(A \cap A^{+}\right) \leq M \mu^{1 / p}(A)=0$. Thus $\varphi^{+}$is absolutely continuous with respect to $\mu$ and by the Radon-Nikodym theorem (p. 422) has an integrable density $g^{+}$: $\varphi^{+}(A)=\int_{A} g^{+} d \mu$. Together with the same result for $\varphi^{-}$, this shows that there is an integrable $g$ such that $\gamma\left(I_{A}\right)=\varphi(A)= \int_{A} g d \mu=\int I_{A} g d \mu$. Thus $\gamma(f)=\int f g d \mu$ for simple functions $f$ in $L^{p}$.

Assume that this $g$ lies in $L^{q}$, and define $\gamma_{g}$ by the equation (19.8). Then $\gamma$ and $\gamma_{g}$ are bounded linear functionals that agree for simple functions; since the latter are dense (Theorem 19.2(i)), it follows by the continuity of $\gamma$ and $\gamma_{g}$ that they agree on all of $L^{p}$. It is therefore enough (in the case of finite $\mu$ ) to prove $g \in L^{q}$. It will also be shown that $\|g\|_{q}$ is at most the $M$ of (19.7); since $\|g\|_{q}$ does work as a bound in (19.7), (19.9) will follow. If $\gamma_{g}(f) \equiv 0$, (19.9) will imply that $g=0$ almost everywhere, and for the general $\gamma$ it will follow further that two functions $g$ satisfying $\gamma_{g}(f) \equiv \gamma(f)$ must agree almost everywhere.

Assume that $1<p, q<\infty$. Let $g_{n}$ be simple functions such that $0 \leq g_{n} \uparrow|g|^{q}$, and take $h_{n}=g_{n}^{1 / p}$ sgn $g$. Then $h_{n} g=g_{n}^{1 / p}|g| \geq g_{n}^{1 / p} g_{n}^{1 / q}=g_{n}$, and since $h_{n}$ is simple, it follows that $\int g_{n} d \mu \leq \int h_{n} g d \mu=\gamma_{g}\left(h_{n}\right)=\gamma\left(h_{n}\right) \leq M \cdot\left\|h_{n}\right\|_{p}= M\left[\int g_{n} d \mu\right]^{1 / p}$. Since $1-1 / p=1 / q$, this gives $\left[\int g_{n} d \mu\right]^{1 / q} \leq M$. Now the monotone convergence theorem gives $g \in L^{p}$ and even $\|g\|_{q} \leq M$.

Assume that $p=1$ and $q=\infty$. In this case, $|f g d \mu|=\left|\gamma_{g}(f)\right|=|\gamma(f)| \leq M$. $\|f\|_{1}$ for simple functions $f$ in $L^{1}$. Take $f=\operatorname{sgn} g \cdot I_{[|g| \geq \alpha]}$. Then $\alpha \mu[|g| \geq \alpha] \leq \int I_{[|g| \geq \alpha]} \cdot|g| d \mu=\int f g d \mu \leq M \cdot\|f\|_{1}=M \mu[|g| \geq \alpha]$. If $\alpha>M$, this inequality gives $\mu[|g| \geq \alpha]=0$; therefore $\|g\|_{\infty}=\|g\|_{q} \leq M$ and $g \in L^{\infty}=L^{q}$.

Case II: $\mu \sigma$-finite. Let $A_{n}$ be sets such that $A_{n} \uparrow \Omega$ and $\mu\left(A_{n}\right)<\infty$. If $\mu_{n}(A)=\mu\left(A \cap A_{n}\right)$, then $\left|\gamma\left(f I_{A_{n}}\right)\right| \leq M \cdot\left\|f I_{A_{n}}\right\|_{p}=M \cdot\left[\int|f|^{p} d \mu_{n}\right]^{1 / p}$ for $f \in L^{p}\left(f I_{A_{n}} \in L^{p}(\mu) \subset L^{p}\left(\mu_{n}\right)\right.$ ). By the finite case, $A_{n}$ supports a $g_{n}$ in $L^{q}$ such that $\gamma\left(f I_{A_{n}}\right)=\int f I_{A_{n}} g_{n} d \mu$ for $f \in L^{p}$, and $\left\|g_{n}\right\|_{q} \leq M$. Because of uniqueness, $g_{n+1}$ can be taken to agree with $g_{n}$ on $A_{n}\left(L^{p}\left(\mu_{n+1}\right) \subset L^{p}\left(\mu_{n}\right)\right)$. There is therefore a function $g$ on $\Omega$ such that $g=g_{n}$ on $A_{n}$ and $\left\|I_{A_{n}} g\right\|_{q} \leq M$. It follows that $\|g\|_{q} \leq M$ and $g \in L^{q}$. By the dominated convergence theorem and the continuity of $\gamma, f \in L^{p}$ implies $\int f g d \mu=\lim _{n} \int f I_{A_{n}} g d \mu= \lim _{n} \gamma\left(f I_{A_{n}}\right)=\gamma(f)$. Uniqueness follows as before. $\square$

[^3]
## Weak Compactness

For $f \in L^{p}$. and $g \in L^{q}$, where $p$ and $q$ are conjugate, write

$$
\begin{equation*}
(f, g)=\int f g d \mu \tag{19.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

For fixed $f$ in $L^{p}$, this defines a bounded linear functional on $L^{q}$; for fixed $g$ in $L^{q}$, it defines a bounded linear functional on $L^{p}$. By Hölder's inequality,

$$
\begin{equation*}
|(f, g)| \leq\|f\|_{p}\|g\|_{q} . \tag{19.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

Suppose that $f$ and $f_{n}$ are elements of $L^{p}$. If $(f, g)=\lim _{n}\left(f_{n}, g\right)$ for each $g$ in $L^{q}$, then $f_{n}$ converges weakly to $f$. If $\left\|f-f_{n}\right\|_{p} \rightarrow 0$, then certainly $f_{n}$ converges weakly to $f$, although the converse is false. ${ }^{\dagger}$

The next theorem says in effect that if $p>1$, then the unit ball $B_{1}^{p}= \left[f \in L^{p}:\|f\|_{p} \leq 1\right]$ is compact in the topology of weak convergence.

Theorem 19.4. Suppose that $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite and $\mathscr{F}$ is countably generated. If $1<p \leq \infty$, then every sequence in $B_{1}^{p}$ contains a subsequence converging weakly to an element of $B_{1}^{p}$.

Suppose of elements $f_{n}, f$, and $f^{\prime}$ of $L^{p}$ that $f_{n}$ converges weakly both to $f$ and to $f^{\prime}$. Since, by hypothesis, $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite and $p>1$, Theorem 19.3 applies if the $p$ and $q$ there are interchanged. And now, since $(f, g)=\left(f^{\prime}, g\right)$ for all $g$ in $L^{q}$, it follows by uniqueness that $f=f^{\prime}$. Therefore, weak limits are unique under the present hypothesis. The assumption $p>1$ is essential. ${ }^{\ddagger}$

Proor. Let $q$ be conjugate to $p(1 \leq q<\infty)$. By Theorem 19.2(ii), $L^{q}$ contains a countable, dense set $G$. Add to $G$ all finite, rational linear combinations of its elements; it is still countable. Suppose that $\left\{f_{n}\right\} \subset B_{1}^{p}$.

By (19.11), $\left|\left(f_{n}, g\right)\right| \leq \|\left. g\right|_{q}$ for $g \in L^{q}$. Since $\left\{\left(f_{n}, g\right)\right\}$ is bounded, it is possible by the diagonal method [A14] to pass to a subsequence of $\left\{f_{n}\right\}$ along which, for each of the countably many $g$ in $G$, the limit $\lim _{n}\left(f_{n}, g\right)=\gamma(g)$ exists and $|\gamma(g)| \leq\|g\|_{q}$. For $g, g^{\prime} \in G,\left|\gamma(g)-\gamma\left(g^{\prime}\right)\right|=\lim _{n}\left|\left(f_{n}, g-g^{\prime}\right)\right| \leq \| g -g^{\prime} \|_{g}$. Therefore, $\gamma$ is uniformly continuous on $G$ and so has a unique continuous extension to all of $L^{q}$. For $g, g^{\prime} \in G, \gamma\left(g+g^{\prime}\right)=\lim _{n}\left(f_{n}, g+g^{\prime}\right) =\gamma(g)+\gamma\left(g^{\prime}\right)$; by continuity, this extends to all of $L^{q}$. For $g \in G$ and $\alpha$ rational, $\gamma(\alpha g)=\alpha \lim _{n}\left(f_{n}, g\right)=\alpha \gamma(g)$; by continuity, this extends to all real $\alpha$ and all $g$ in $L^{q}: \gamma$ is a linear functional on $L^{q}$. Finally, $|\gamma(g)| \leq\|g\|_{q}$ extends from $G$ to $L^{q}$ by continuity, and $\gamma$ is bounded in the sense of (19.7).

[^4]By the Riesz representation theorem ( $1 \leq q<\infty$ ), there is an $f$ in $L^{p}$ (the space adjoint to $L^{q}$ ) such that $\gamma(g)=(f, g)$ for all $g$. Since $\gamma$ has norm at most 1 , (19.9) implies that $\|f\|_{p} \leq 1: f$ lies in $B_{1}^{p}$.

Now $(f, g)=\lim _{n}\left(f_{n}, g\right)$ for $g$ in $G$. Suppose that $g^{\prime}$ is an arbitrary element of $L^{q}$, and choose $g$ in $G$ so that $\left\|g^{\prime}-g\right\|_{q}<\epsilon$. Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \left|\left(f, g^{\prime}\right)-\left(f_{n}, g^{\prime}\right)\right| \\
& \quad \leq\left|\left(f, g^{\prime}\right)-(f, g)\right|+\left|(f, g)-\left(f_{n}, g\right)\right|+\left|\left(f_{n}, g\right)-\left(f_{n}, g^{\prime}\right)\right| \\
& \quad \leq\|f\|_{p}\left\|g^{\prime}-g\right\|_{q}+\left|(f, g)-\left(f_{n}, g\right)\right|+\left\|f_{n}\right\|_{p}\left\|g-g^{\prime}\right\|_{q} \\
& \quad \leq 2 \epsilon+\left|(f, g)-\left(f_{n}, g\right)\right|
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $g \in G$, the last term here goes to 0 , and hence $\lim _{n}\left(f_{n} . g^{\prime}\right)=\left(f, g^{\prime}\right)$ for all $g^{\prime}$ in $L^{q}$. Therefore, $f_{n}$ converges weakly to $f$.

## Some Decision Theory

The weak compactness of the unit ball in $L^{\infty}$ has interesting implications for statistical decision theory. Suppose that $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite and $\mathscr{F}$ is countably generated. Let $f_{1}, \ldots, f_{k}$ be probability densities with respect to $\mu$-nonnegative and integrating to 1. Imagine that, for some $i, \omega$ is drawn from $\Omega$ according to the probability measure $P_{i}(A)=\int_{A} f_{i} d \mu$. The statistical problem is to decide, on the basis of an observed $\omega$, which $f_{i}$ is the right one.

Assume that if the right density is $f_{i}$, then a statistician choosing $f_{1}$ incurs a nonnegative loss $L(j \mid i)$. A decision rule is a vector function $\delta(\omega)=\left(\delta_{1}(\omega), \ldots, \delta_{k}(\omega)\right)$, where the $\delta_{i}(\omega)$ are nonnegative and add to 1 : the statistician, observing $\omega$, selects $f_{i}$ with probability $\delta_{i}(\omega)$. If, for each $\omega, \delta_{i}(\omega)$ is 1 for one $i$ and 0 for the others, $\delta$ is a nonrandomized rule; otherwise, it is a randomized rule. Let $D$ be the set of all rules. The problem is to choose, in some more or less rational way that connects up with the losses $L(j \mid i)$, a rule $\delta$ from $D$.

The risk corresponding to $\delta$ and $f_{i}$ is

$$
R_{i}(\delta)=\int\left[\sum_{j} \delta_{i}(\omega) L(j \mid i)\right] f_{i}(\omega) \mu(d \omega)
$$

which can be interpreted as the loss a statistician using $\delta$ can expect if $f_{i}$ is the right density. The risk point for $\delta$ is $R(\delta)=\left(R_{1}(\delta), \ldots, R_{k}(\delta)\right)$. If $R_{i}\left(\delta^{\prime}\right) \leq R_{i}(\delta)$ for all $i$ and $R_{i}\left(\delta^{\prime}\right)<R_{i}(\delta)$ for some $i$-that is, if the point $R\left(\delta^{\prime}\right)$ is "southwest" of $R(\delta)$-then of course $\delta^{\prime}$ is taken as being better than $\delta$. There is in general no rule better than all the others. (Different rules can have the same risk point, but they are then indistinguishable as regards the decision problem.)

The risk set is the collection $S$ of all the risk points; it is a bounded set in the first orthant of $R^{k}$. To avoid trivialities, assume that $S$ does not contain the origin (as would happen for example if the $L(j \mid i)$ were all 0 ).

Suppose that $\delta$ and $\delta^{\prime}$ are elements of $D$, and $\lambda$ and $\lambda^{\prime}$ are nonnegative and add to 1. If $\delta_{i}^{\prime \prime}(\omega)=\lambda \delta_{i}(\omega)+\lambda^{\prime} \delta_{i}^{\prime}(\omega)$, then $\delta^{\prime \prime}$ is in $D$ and $R\left(\delta^{\prime \prime}\right)=\lambda R(\delta)+\lambda^{\prime} R\left(\delta^{\prime}\right)$. Therefore, $S$ is a convex set.

Lying much deeper is the fact that $S$ is compact. Given points $x^{(n)}$ in $S$, choose rules $\delta^{(n)}$ such that $R\left(\delta^{(n)}\right)=x^{(n)}$. Now $\delta_{j}^{(n)}(\cdot)$ is an element of $L^{\infty}$, in fact of $B_{1}^{\infty}$, and so by Theorem 19.4 there is a subsequence along which, for each $j=1, \ldots, k, \delta_{j}^{(n)}$
converges weakly to a function $\delta_{1}$ in $B_{1}^{\infty}$. If $\mu(A)<\infty$, then $\int \delta_{j} I_{A} d \mu= \lim _{n} \int \delta_{j}^{(n)} I_{A} d \mu \geq 0$ and $\int\left(1-\sum_{j} \delta_{j}\right) I_{A} d \mu=\lim _{n} \int\left(1-\sum_{j} \delta_{j}^{(n)}\right) I_{A} d \mu=0$, so that $\delta_{j} \geq 0$ and $\sum_{j} \delta_{j}=1$ almost everywhere on $A$. Since $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite, the $\delta_{j}$ can be altered on a set of $\mu$-measure 0 in such a way as to ensure that $\delta=\left(\delta_{1}, \ldots, \delta_{k}\right)$ is an element of $D$. But, along the subsequence, $x^{(n)} \rightarrow R(\delta)$. Therefore: The risk set is compact and convex.

The rest is geometry. For $x$ in $R^{k}$, let $Q_{x}$ be the set of $x^{\prime}$ such that $0 \leq x_{i}^{\prime} \leq x_{i}$ for all $i$. If $x=R(\delta)$ and $x^{\prime}=R\left(\delta^{\prime}\right)$, then $\delta^{\prime}$ is better than $\delta$ if and only if $x^{\prime} \in Q_{x}$ and $x^{\prime} \neq x$. A rule $\delta$ is admissible if there exists no $\delta^{\prime}$ better than $\delta$; it makes no sense to use a rule that is not admissible. Geometrically, admissibility means that, for $x=R(\delta)$, $S \cap Q_{x}$ consists of $x$ alone.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/6289d1e6-4ff9-4bb6-b3da-068bafdb2b1b-020.jpg?height=606&width=672&top_left_y=871&top_left_x=200)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/6289d1e6-4ff9-4bb6-b3da-068bafdb2b1b-020.jpg?height=581&width=667&top_left_y=896&top_left_x=931)

Let $x=R(\delta)$ be given, and suppose that $\delta$ is not admissible. Since $S \cap Q_{x}$ is compact, it contains a point $x^{\prime}$ nearest the origin ( $x^{\prime}$ unique, since $S \cap Q_{x}$ is convex as well as compact); let $\delta^{\prime}$ be a corresponding rule: $x^{\prime}=R\left(\delta^{\prime}\right)$. Since $\delta$ is not admissible, $x^{\prime} \neq x$, and $\delta^{\prime}$ is better than $\delta$. If $S \cap Q_{x^{\prime}}$ contained a point distinct from $x^{\prime}$, it would be a point of $S \cap Q_{x}$ nearer the origin than $x^{\prime}$, which is impossible. This means that $Q_{x^{\prime}}$ contains no point of $S$ other than $x^{\prime}$ itself, which means in turn that $\delta^{\prime}$ is admissible. Therefore, if $\delta$ is not itself admissible, there is a $\delta^{\prime}$ that is admissible and is better than $\delta$. This is expressed by saying that the class of admissible rules is complete.

Let $p=\left(p_{1}, \ldots, p_{k}\right)$ be a probability vector, and view $p_{i}$ as an a priori probability that $f_{i}$ is the correct density. A rule $\delta$ has Bayes risk $R(p, \delta)=\sum_{i} p_{i} R_{i}(\delta)$ with respect to $p$. This is a kind of compound risk: $f_{i}$ is correct with probability $p_{i}$, and the statistician crooses $f_{j}$ with probability $\delta_{j}(\omega)$. A Bayes rule is one that minimizes the Bayes risk for a given $p$. In this case, take $\alpha=R(p, \delta)$ and consider the hyperplane

$$
\begin{equation*}
H=\left[z: \sum_{i} p_{i} z_{i}=\alpha\right] \tag{19.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

and the half space

$$
\begin{equation*}
H^{+}=\left[z: \sum_{i} p_{i} z_{i} \geq \alpha\right] . \tag{19.13}
\end{equation*}
$$

Then $x=R(\delta)$ lies on $H$, and $S$ is contained in $H^{+}: x$ is on the boundary of $S$, and
$H$ is a supporting hyperplane. If $p_{i}>0$ for all $i$, then $Q_{x}$ meets $S$ only at $x$, and so $\delta$ is admissible.

Suppose now that $\delta$ is admissible, so that $x=R(\delta)$ is the only point in $S \cap Q_{x}$ and $x$ lies on the boundary of $S$. The problem is to show that $\delta$ is a Bayes rule, which means finding a supporting hyperplane (19.12) corresponding to a probability vector $p$. Let $T$ consist of those $y$ for which $Q_{y}$ meets $S$. Then $T$ is convex: given a convex combination $y^{\prime \prime}=\lambda y+\lambda^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ of points in $T$, choose in $S$ points $z$ and $z^{\prime}$ southwest of $y$ and $y^{\prime}$, respectively, and note that $z^{\prime \prime}=\lambda z+\lambda^{\prime} z^{\prime}$ lies in $S$ and is southwest of $y^{\prime \prime}$. Since $S$ meets $Q_{x}$ only in the point $x$, the same is true of $T$, so that $x$ is a boundary point of $T$ as well as of $S$. Let (19.12) ( $p \neq 0$ ) be a supporting hyperplane through $x$. $x \in H$ and $T \subset H^{+}$. If $p_{i_{4}}<0$, take $z_{i_{4}}=x_{i_{4}}+1$ and take $z_{i}=x_{i}$ for the other $i$; then $z$ lies in $T$ but not in $H^{+}$, a contradiction. (The right-hand figure shows the role of $T$ : the planes $H_{1}$ and $H_{2}$ both support $S$, but only $H_{2}$ supports $T$ and only $H_{2}$ corresponds to a probability vector.) Thus $p_{i} \geq 0$ for all $i$, and since $\Sigma_{i} p_{i}=1$ can be arranged by normalization, $\delta$ is indeed a Bayes rule. Therefore The admissible rules are Bayes rules, and they form a complete class.

## The Space $\boldsymbol{L}^{\mathbf{2}}$

The space $L^{2}$ is special because $p=2$ is its own conjugate index. If $f, g \in L^{2}$, the inner product $(f, g)=\int f g d \mu$ is well defined, and by (19.11)-write $\|f\|$ in place of $\|f\|_{2}-(f, g) \mid \leq\|f\| \cdot\|g\|_{\text {. This is the Schwarz (or Cauchy-Schwarz) }}$ inequality. If one of $f$ and $g$ is fixed, ( $f, g$ ) is a bounded (hence continuous) linear functional in the other. Further, $(f, g)=(g, f)$, the norm is given by $\|f\|^{2}=(f, f)$, and $L^{2}$ is complete under the metric $d(f, g)=\|f-g\|$. A Hilbert space is a vector space on which is defined an inner product having all these properties.

The Hilbert space $L^{2}$ is quite like Euclidean space. If $(f, g)=0$, then $f$ and $g$ are orthogonal, and orthogonality is like perpendicularity. If $f_{1}, \ldots, f_{n}$ are orthogonal (in pairs), then by linearity, $\left(\sum_{i} f_{i}, \sum_{j} f_{j}\right)=\sum_{i} \sum_{j}\left(f_{i}, f_{j}\right)= \sum_{i}\left(f_{i}, f_{i}\right):\left\|\sum_{i} f_{i}\right\|^{2}=\sum_{i}\left\|f_{i}\right\|^{2}$. This is a version of the Pythagorean theorem. If $f$ and $g$ are orthogonal, write $f \perp g$. For every $f, f \perp 0$.

Suppose now that $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite and $\mathscr{F}$ is countably generated, so that $L^{2}$ is separable as a metric space. The construction that follows gives a sequence (finite or infinite) $\varphi_{1}, \varphi_{2}, \ldots$ that is orthonormal in the sense that $\left\|\varphi_{n}\right\|=1$ for all $n$ and $\left(\varphi_{m}, \varphi_{n}\right)=0$ for $m \neq n$, and is complete in the sense that $\left(f, \varphi_{n}\right)=0$ for all $n$ implies $f=0$-so that the orthonormal system cannot be enlarged. Start with a sequence $f_{1}, f_{2}, \ldots$ that is dense in $L^{2}$. Define $g_{1}, g_{2}, \ldots$ inductively: Let $g_{1}=f_{1}$. Suppose that $g_{1}, \ldots, g_{n}$ have been defined and are orthogonal. Define $g_{n+1}=f_{n+1}-\sum_{i=1}^{n} \alpha_{n i} g_{i}$, where $\alpha_{n i}$ is $\left(f_{n+1}, g_{i}\right) /\left\|g_{i}\right\|^{2}$ if $g_{i} \neq 0$ and is arbitrary if $g_{i}=0$. Then $g_{n+1}$ is orthogonal to $g_{1}, \ldots, g_{n}$, and $f_{n+1}$ is a linear combination of $g_{1}, \ldots, g_{n+1}$. This, the Gram-Schmidt method, gives an orthogonal sequence $g_{1}, g_{2}, \ldots$ with the property that the finite linear combinations of the $g_{n}$ include all the $f_{n}$ and are therefore dense in $L^{2}$. If $g_{n} \neq 0$, take $\varphi_{n}=g_{n} /\left\|g_{n}\right\|$; if $g_{n}=0$, discard it from the sequence. Then $\varphi_{1}, \varphi_{2}, \ldots$ is orthonormal, and the finite linear combinations of the $\varphi_{n}$ are still dense. It can happen that all but finitely many of the $g_{n}$ are 0 , in which
case there are only finitely many of the $\varphi_{n}$. In what follows it is assumed that $\varphi_{1}, \varphi_{2}, \ldots$ is an infinite sequence; the finite case is analogous and somewhat simpler.

Suppose that $f$ is orthogonal to all the $\varphi_{n}$. If $a_{i}$ are arbitrary scalars, then $f, a_{1} \varphi_{1}, \ldots, a_{n} \varphi_{n}$ is an orthogonal set, and by the Pythagorean property, $\left\|f-\sum_{i=1}^{n} a_{i} \varphi_{i}\right\|^{2}=\|f\|^{2}+\sum_{i=1}^{n} a_{i}^{2} \geq\|f\|^{2}$. If $\|f\|>0$, then $f$ cannot be approximated by finite linear combinations of the $\varphi_{n}$, a contradiction: $\varphi_{1}, \varphi_{2}, \ldots$ is a complete orthonormal system.

Consider now a sequence $a_{1}, a_{2}, \ldots$ of scalars for which $\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} a_{i}^{2}$ converges. If $s_{n}=\sum_{i=1}^{n} a_{i} \varphi_{i}$, then the Pythagorean theorem gives $\left\|s_{n}-s_{m}\right\|^{2}=\sum_{m<i \leq n} a_{i}^{2}$. Since the scalar series converges, $\left\{s_{n}\right\}$ is fundamental and therefore by Theorem 19.1 converges to some $g$ in $L^{2}$. Thus $g=\lim _{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n} a_{i} \varphi_{i}$, which it is natural to express as $g=\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} a_{i} \varphi_{i}$. The series (that is to say, the sequence of partial sums) converges to $g$ in the mean of order 2 (not almost everywhere). By the following argument, every element of $L^{2}$ has a unique representation in this form.

The Fourier coefficients of $f$ with respect to $\left\{\varphi_{i}\right\}$ are the inner products $a_{i}=\left(f, \varphi_{i}\right)$. For each $n, 0 \leq\left\|f-\sum_{i=1}^{n} a_{i} \varphi_{i}\right\|^{2}=\|f\|^{2}-2 \sum_{i} a_{i}\left(f, \varphi_{i}\right)+ \sum_{i j} a_{i} a_{j}\left(\varphi_{i}, \varphi_{j}\right)=\|f\|^{2}-\sum_{i=1}^{n} a_{i}^{2}$, and hence, $n$ being arbitrary, $\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} a_{i}^{2} \leq\|f\|^{2}$. By the argument above, the series $\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} a_{i} \varphi_{i}$ therefore converges. By linearity, $\left(f-\sum_{i=1}^{n} a_{i} \varphi_{i}, \varphi_{j}\right)=0$ for $n \geq j$, and by continuity, $\left(f-\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} a_{i} \varphi_{i}, \varphi_{j}\right)=0$. Therefore, $f-\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} a_{i} \varphi_{i}$ is orthogonal to each $\varphi_{j}$ and by completeness must be 0 :

$$
\begin{equation*}
f=\sum_{i=1}^{\infty}\left(f, \varphi_{i}\right) \varphi_{i} \tag{19.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

This is the Fourier representation of $f$. It is unique because if $\hat{J}=\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} a_{i} \varphi_{i}$ is $0\left(\sum a_{i}^{2}<\infty\right)$, then $a_{j}=\left(f, \varphi_{j}\right)=0$. Because of (19.14), $\left\{\varphi_{n}\right\}$ is also called an orthonormal basis for $L^{2}$.

A subset $M$ of $L^{2}$ is a subspace if it is closed both algebraically $\left(f, f^{\prime} \in M\right.$ implies $\alpha f+\alpha^{\prime} f^{\prime} \in M$ ) and topologically ( $f_{n} \in M, f_{n} \rightarrow f$ implies $f \in M$ ). If $L^{2}$ is separable, then so is the subspace $M$, and the construction above carries over: $M$ contains an orthonormal system $\left\{\varphi_{n}\right\}$ that is complete in $M$, in the sense that $f=0$ if $\left(f, \varphi_{n}\right)=0$ for all $n$ and if $f \in M$. And each $f$ in $M$ has the unique Fourier representation (19.14). Even if $f$ does not lie in $M$, $\sum_{i=1}^{\infty}\left(f, \varphi_{i}\right)^{2}$ converges, so that $\sum_{i=1}^{\infty}\left(f, \varphi_{i}\right) \varphi_{i}$ is well defined.

This leads to a powerful idea, that of orthogonal projection onto $M$. For an orthonormal basis $\left\{\varphi_{i}\right\}$ of $M$, define $P_{M} f=\sum_{i=1}^{\infty}\left(f, \varphi_{i}\right) \varphi_{i}$ for all $f$ in $L^{2}$ (not just for $f$ in $M$ ). Clearly, $P_{M} f \in M$. Further, $f-\sum_{i=1}^{n}\left(f, \varphi_{i}\right) \varphi_{i} \perp \varphi_{j}$ for $n \geq j$ by linearity, so that $f-P_{M} f \perp \varphi_{j}$ by continuity. But if $f-P_{M} f$ is orthogonal to each $\varphi_{j}$, then, again by linearity and continuity, it is orthogonal to the general element $\sum_{j=1}^{\infty} b_{j} \varphi_{j}$ of $M$. Therefore, $P_{M} f \in M$ and $f-P_{M} f \perp M$. The map $f \rightarrow P_{M} f$ is the orthogonal projection on $M$.

The fundamental properties of $P_{M}$ are these:
(i) $g \in M$ and $f-g \perp M$ together imply $g=P_{M} f$;
(ii) $f \in M$ implies $P_{M} f=f$;
(iii) $g \in M$ implies $\|f-g\| \geq\left\|f-P_{M} f\right\|$;
(iv) $P_{M}\left(\alpha f+\alpha^{\prime} f^{\prime}\right)=\alpha P_{M} f+\alpha^{\prime} P_{M} f^{\prime}$.

Property (i) says that $P_{M} f$ is uniquely determined by the two conditions $P_{M} f \in M$ and $f-P_{M} \perp M$. To prove it, suppose that $g, g^{\prime} \in M, f-g \perp M$, and $f-g^{\prime} \perp M$. Then $g-g^{\prime} \in M$ and $g-g^{\prime} \perp M$, so that $g-g^{\prime}$ is orthogonal to itself and hence $\left\|g-g^{\prime}\right\|^{2}=0: g=g^{\prime}$. Thus the mapping $P_{M}$ is independent of the particular basis $\left\{\varphi_{i}\right\}$; it is determined by $M$ alone.

Clearly, (ii) follows from (i); it implies that $P_{M}$ is idempotent in the sense that $P_{M}^{2} f=P_{M} f$. As for (iii), if $g$ lies in $M$, so does $P_{M} f-g$, so that, by the Pythagorean relation, $\|f-g\|^{2}=\left\|f-P_{M} f\right\|^{2}+\left\|P_{M} f-g\right\|^{2} \geq\left\|f-P_{M} f\right\|^{2}$; the inequality is strict if $g \neq P_{M} f$. Thus $P_{M} f$ is the unique point of $M$ lying nearest to $f$. Property (iv), linearity, follows from (i).

## An Estimation Problem

First, the technical setting: Let ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}^{*}, \mu$ ) and ( $\Theta, \mathscr{E}, \pi$ ) be a $\sigma$-finite space and a probability space, and assume that $\mathscr{F}$ and $\mathscr{E}$ are countably generated. Let $f_{\theta}(\omega)$ be a nonnegative function on $\Theta \times \Omega$, measurable $\mathscr{E} \times \mathscr{F}$, and assume that $\int_{\Omega} f_{\theta}(\omega) \mu(d \omega) =1$ for each $\theta \in \Theta$. For some unknown value of $\theta, \omega$ is drawn from $\Omega$ according to the probabilities $P_{\theta}(A)=\int_{A} f_{\theta}(\omega) \mu(d \omega)$, and the statistical problem is to estimate the value of $g(\theta)$, where $g$ is a real function on $\Theta$. The statistician knows the functions $f(\cdot)$ and $g(\cdot)$, as well as the value of $\omega$; it is the value of $\theta$ that is unknown.

For an exampte, take $\Omega$ to be the line, $f(\omega)$ a function known to the statistician, and $f_{\theta}(\omega)=\alpha f(\alpha \omega+\beta)$, where $\theta=(\alpha, \beta)$ specifies unknown scale and location parameters; the problem is to estimate $g(\theta)=\alpha$, say. Or, more simply, as in the exponential case (14.7), take $f_{\theta}(\omega)=\alpha f(\alpha \omega)$, where $\theta=g(\theta)=\alpha$.

An estimator of $g(\theta)$ is a function $t(\omega)$. It is unbiased if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{\Omega} t(\omega) f_{\theta}(\omega) \mu(d \omega)=g(\theta) \tag{19.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all $\theta$ in $\Theta$ (assume the integral exists); this condition means that the estimate is on target in an average sense. A natural loss function is $(t(\omega)-g(\theta))^{2}$, and if $f_{\theta}$ is the correct density, the risk is taken to be $\int_{\Omega}(t(\omega)-g(\theta))^{2} f_{\theta}(\omega) \mu(d \theta)$.

If the probability measure $\pi$ is regarded as an a priori distribution for the unknown $\theta$, the Bayes risk of $t$ is

$$
\begin{equation*}
R(\pi, t)=\int_{\Theta} \int_{\Omega}(t(\omega)-g(\theta))^{2} f_{\theta}(\omega) \mu(d \omega) \pi(d \theta) \tag{19.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

this integral, assumed finite, can be viewed as a joint integral or as an iterated integral (Fubini's theorem). And now $t_{0}$ is a Bayes estimator of $g$ with respect to $\pi$ if it minimizes $R(\pi, t)$ over $t$. This is analogous to the Bayes rules discussed earlier. The
following simple projection argument shows that, except in trivial cases, no Bayes estimator is unbaised ${ }^{\dagger}$

Let $Q$ be the probability measure on $\mathscr{E} \times \mathscr{F}$ having density $f_{\theta}(\omega)$ with respect to $\pi \times \mu$, and let $L^{2}$ be the space of square-integrable functions on $(\Theta \times \Omega, \mathscr{E} \times \mathscr{F}, Q)$. Then $Q$ is finite and $\mathscr{E} \times \mathscr{F}$ is countably generated. Recall that an element of $L^{2}$ is an equivalence class of functions that are equal almost everywhere with respect to $Q$. Let $G$ be the class of elements of $L^{2}$ containing a function of the form $\bar{g}(\theta, \omega)=g(\omega)$ -functions of $\theta$ alone Then $G$ is a subspace. (That $G$ is algebraically closed is clear; if $f_{n} \in G$ and $\left\|f_{n}-f\right\| \rightarrow 0$, then-see the proof of Theorem 19.1-some subsequence converges to $f$ outside a set of $Q$-measure 0 , and it follows easily that $f \in G$.) Similarly, let $T$ be the subspace of functions of $\omega$ alone: $\bar{t}(\theta, \omega)=t(\omega)$. Consider only functions $g$ and their estimators $t$ for which the corresponding $\bar{g}$ and $\bar{t}$ are in $L^{2}$.

Suppose now that $t_{0}$ is both an unbiased estimator of $g_{0}$ and a Bayes estimator of $g_{0}$ with respect to $\pi$. By (1916) for $g_{0}, R(\pi, t)=\left\|t-\bar{g}_{0}\right\|^{1}$, and since $t_{0}$ is a Bayes estimator of $g_{0}$, it follows that $\left\|t_{0}-\bar{g}_{0}\right\|^{2} \leq\left\|\bar{t}-\bar{g}_{0}\right\|^{2}$ for all $\bar{t}$ in $T$. This means that $\bar{t}_{0}$ is the orthogonal projection of $\bar{g}_{0}$ on the subspace $T$ and hence that $\bar{g}_{0}-\bar{t}_{0} \perp \bar{t}_{0}$. On the other hand, from the assumption that $t_{0}$ is an unbiased estimator of $g_{0}$, it follows that, for every $\bar{g}(\theta, \omega)=g(\theta)$ in $G$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left(\bar{t}_{0}-\bar{g}_{0}, \bar{g}\right) & =\int_{\Theta} \int_{\Omega}\left(t_{0}(\omega)-g_{0}(\theta)\right) g(\theta) f_{\theta}(\omega) \mu(d \omega) \pi(d \theta) \\
& =\int_{\Theta} g(\theta)\left[\int_{\Omega}\left(t_{0}(\omega)-g_{0}(\theta)\right) f_{\theta}(\omega) \mu(d \omega)\right] \pi(d \theta)=0
\end{aligned}
$$

This means that $\bar{t}_{0}-\bar{g}_{0} \perp G: \bar{g}_{0}$ is the orthogonal projection of $\bar{t}_{0}$ on the subspace $G$ But $\bar{g}_{0}-\bar{t}_{0} \perp \bar{t}_{0}$ and $\bar{t}_{0}-\bar{g}_{0} \perp \bar{g}_{0}$ together imply that $\bar{t}_{0}-\bar{g}_{0}$ is orthogonal to itself: $\bar{t}_{0}=\bar{g}_{0}$. Therefore, $t_{0}(\omega)=\bar{t}_{0}(\theta, \omega)=\bar{g}_{0}(\theta, \omega)=g_{0}(\theta)$ for $(\theta, \omega)$ outside a set of $Q$ measure 0 .

This implies that $t_{0}$ and $g_{0}$ are essentially constant. Suppose for simplicity that $f_{\theta}(\omega)>0$ for all $(\theta, \omega)$, so that (Theorem 15.2) $(\pi \times \mu)\left[(\theta, \omega): t_{0}(\omega) \neq g_{0}(\theta)\right]=0$. By Fubini's theorem, there is a $\theta$ such that, if $a=g_{0}(\theta)$, then $\mu\left[\omega: t_{0}(\omega) \neq a\right]=0$; and there is an $\omega$ such that, if $b=t_{0}(\omega)$, then $\pi\left[\theta: g_{0}(\theta) \neq b\right]=0$. It follows that, for $(\theta, \omega)$ outside a set of ( $\pi \times \mu$ )-measure $0, t_{0}(\omega)$ and $g_{0}(\theta)$ have the common value $a=b: \pi\left[\theta: g_{0}(\theta)=a\right]=1$ and $P_{\theta}\left[\omega: t_{0}(\omega)=a\right]=1$ for all $\theta$ in $\Theta$.

## PROBLEMS

19.1. Suppose that $\mu(\Omega)<\infty$ and $f \in L^{\infty}$. Show that $\|f\|_{p} \uparrow\|f\|_{\infty}$.
19.2. (a) Show that $L^{\infty}((0,1], \mathscr{B}, \lambda)$ is not separable.
(b) Show that $L^{\rho}((0,1], \mathscr{B}, \mu)$ is not separable if $\mu$ is counting measure ( $\mu$ is not $\sigma$-finite).
(c) Show that $L^{p}(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ is not separable if (Theorem 36.2) there is on the space an independent stochastic process $\left[X_{t}: 0 \leq t \leq 1\right]$ such that $X_{t}$ takes the values $\pm 1$ with probability $\frac{1}{2}$ each ( $\mathscr{F}$ is not countably generated).
${ }^{\dagger}$ This is interesting because of the close connection between Bayes rules and admissibility; see Berger, pp. 546 ff.
19.3. Show that Theorem 19.3 fails for $L^{\infty}((0,1], \mathscr{B}, \lambda)$. Hint: Take $\gamma(f)$ to be a Banach limit of $n \int_{0}^{1 / n} f(x) d x$.
19.4. Consider weak convergence in $L^{p}((0,1], \mathscr{B}, \lambda)$.
(a) For the case $p=\infty$, find functions $f_{n}$ and $f$ such that $f_{n}$ goes weakly to $f$ but $\left\|f-f_{n}\right\|_{p}$ does not go to 0 .
(b) Do the same for $p=2$.
19.5. Show that the unit ball in $\left.L^{1}(0,1], \mathscr{B}, \lambda\right)$ is not weakly compact.
19.6. Show that a Bayes rule corresponding to $p=\left(p_{1},, p_{k}\right)$ may not be admissible if $p_{i}=0$ for some $i$ But there will be a better Bayes rule that is admissible
19.7. The Neyman-Pearson lemma. Suppose $f_{1}$ and $f_{2}$ are rival densities and $L(J \mid i)$ is 0 or 1 as $j=i$ or $j \neq i$, so that $R_{i}(\delta)$ is the probability of choosing the opposite density when $f_{i}$ is the right one. Suppose of $\delta$ that $\delta_{2}(\omega)=1$ if $f_{2}(\omega)>t f_{1}(\omega)$ and $\delta_{2}(\omega)=0$ if $f_{2}(\omega)<t f_{1}(\omega)$, where $t>0$ Show that $\delta$ is admissible: For any rule $\delta^{\prime}$, $\int \delta_{2}^{\prime} f_{1} d \mu<\int \delta_{2} f_{1} d \mu$ implies $\int \delta_{1}^{\prime} f_{2} d \mu>\int \delta_{1} f_{2} d \mu$. Hint. $\quad \int\left(\delta_{2}-\delta_{2}^{\prime}\right) \left(f_{2}-t f_{1}\right) d \mu \geq 0$, since the integrand is nonnegative.
19.8. The classical orthonormal basis for $L^{2}[0,2 \pi]$ with Lebesgue measure is the trigonometric system
(19.17) $(2 \pi)^{-1}, \quad \pi^{-1 / 2} \sin n x, \quad \pi^{-1 / 2} \cos n x, \quad n=1,2, \ldots$

Prove orthonormality. Hint: Express the sines and cosines in terms of $e^{i n x} \pm e^{-i n x}$, multiply out the products, and use the fact that $\int_{0}^{2 \pi} e^{i m x} d x$ is $2 \pi$ or 0 as $m=0$ or $m \neq 0$. (For the completeness of the trigonometric system, see Problem 26.26.)
19.9. Drop the assumption that $L^{2}$ is separable. Order by inclusion the orthonormal systems in $L^{2}$, and let (Zorn's lemma) $\Phi=\left[\varphi_{\gamma}: \gamma \in \Gamma\right]$ be maximal.
(a) Show that $\Gamma_{f}=\left[\gamma:\left(f, \varphi_{\gamma}\right) \neq 0\right]$ is countable. Hint. Use $\sum_{i=1}^{n}\left(f, \varphi_{\gamma}\right)^{2} \leq\|f\|^{2}$ and the argument for Theorem 10.2(iv).
(b) Let $P f=\sum_{\gamma \in \mathbb{I}_{f}}\left(f, \varphi_{\gamma}\right) \varphi_{\gamma}$. Show that $f-P f \perp \Phi$ and hence (maximality) $f=$ Pf. Thus $\Phi$ is an orthonormal basis.
(c) Show that $\Phi$ is countable if and only if $L^{2}$ is separable.
(d) Now take $\Phi$ to be a maximal orthonormal system in a subspace $M$, and define $P_{M} f=\sum_{\gamma \in r_{f}}\left(f, \varphi_{\gamma}\right) \varphi_{\gamma}$. Show that $P_{M} f \in M$ and $f-P_{M} f \perp \Phi$, that $g= P_{M} g$ if $g \in M$, and that $f-P_{M} f \perp M$. This defines the general orthogonal projection.

## CHAPTER4

## Random Variables and Expected Values

## SECTION 20. RANDOM VARIABLES AND DISTRIBUTIONS

This section and the next cover random variables and the machinery for dealing with them-expected values, distributions, moment generating functions, independence, convolution.

## Random Variables and Vectors

A random variable on a probability space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) is a real-valued function $X=X(\omega)$ measurable $\mathscr{F}$. Sections 5 through 9 dealt with random variables of a special kind, namely simple random variables, those with finite range. All concepts and facts concerning real measurable functions carry over to random variables; any changes are matters of viewpoint, notation, and terminology only.

The positive and negative parts $X^{+}$and $X^{-}$of $X$ are defined as in (15.4) and (15.5). Theorem 13.5 also applies: Define

$$
\psi_{n}(x)= \begin{cases}(k-1) 2^{-n} & \text { if }(k-1) 2^{-n} \leq x<k 2^{-n}  \tag{20.1}\\ & 1 \leq k \leq n 2^{n} \\ n & \text { if } x \geq n .\end{cases}
$$

If $X$ is nonnegative and $X_{n}=\psi_{n}(X)$, then $0 \leq X_{n} \uparrow X$. If $X$ is not necessarily nonnegative, define

$$
X_{n}=\left\{\begin{array}{cc}
\psi_{n}(X) & \text { if } X \geq 0  \tag{20.2}\\
-\psi_{n}(-X) & \text { if } X \leq 0
\end{array}\right.
$$

(This is the same as (13.6).) Then $0 \leq X_{n}(\omega) \uparrow X(\omega)$ if $X(\omega) \geq 0$ and $0 \geq$ 254
$X_{n}(\omega) \downarrow X(\omega)$ if $X(\omega) \leq 0$; and $\left|X_{n}(\omega)\right| \uparrow|X(\omega)|$ for every $\omega$. The random variable $X_{n}$ is in each case simple.

A random vector is a mapping from $\Omega$ to $R^{k}$ that is measurable $\mathscr{F}$. Any mapping from $\Omega$ to $R^{k}$ must have the form $\omega \rightarrow X(\omega)=\left(X_{1}(\omega), \ldots, X_{k}(\omega)\right)$, where each $X_{i}(\omega)$ is real; as shown in Section 13 (see (13.2)), $X$ is measurable if and only if each $X_{i}$ is. Thus a random vector is simply a $k$-tuple $X=\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$ of random variables.

## Subfields

If $\mathscr{A}$ is a $\sigma$-field for which $\mathscr{A} \subset \mathscr{F}$, a $k$-dimensional random vector $X$ is of course measurabie $\mathscr{A}$ if $[\omega: X(\omega) \in H] \in \mathscr{A}$ for every $H$ in $\mathscr{R}^{k}$. The $\sigma$-field $\sigma(X)$ generated by $X$ is the smallest $\sigma$-field with respect to which it is measurable. The $o$-field generated by a collection of random vectors is the smallest $\sigma$-field with respect to which each one is measurable.

As explained in Sections 4 and 5, a sub- $\sigma$-field corresponds to partial information about $\omega$. The information contained in $\sigma(X)=\sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$ consists of the $k$ numbers $X_{1}(\omega), \ldots, X_{k}(\omega) .^{\mathrm{i}}$ The following theorem is the analogue of Theorem 5.1, but there are technical complications in its proof.

Theorem 20.1. Let $X=\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$ be a random vector.
(i) The $\sigma$-field $\sigma(X)=\sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$ consists exactly of the sets $[X \in H]$ for $H \in \mathscr{R}^{k}$.
(ii) In order that a random variable $Y$ be measurable $\sigma(X)=\sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$ it is necessary and sufficient that there exist a measurable map $f: R^{k} \rightarrow R^{1}$ such that $Y(\omega)=f\left(X_{1}(\omega), \ldots, X_{k}(\omega)\right)$ for all $\omega$.

Proof. The class $\mathscr{G}$ of sets of the form $[X \in H]$ for $H \in \mathscr{R}^{k}$ is a $\sigma$-field. Since $X$ is measurable $\sigma(X), \mathscr{G} \subset \sigma(X)$. Since $X$ is measurable $\mathscr{G}$, $\sigma(X) \subset \mathscr{A}$. Hence part (i).
