Measurability of $f$ in part (ii) refers of course to measurability $\mathscr{R}^{k} / \mathscr{R}^{1}$. The sufficiency is easy: if such an $f$ exists, Theorem 13.1(ii) implies that $Y$ is measurable $\sigma(X)$.

To prove necessity, ${ }^{\ddagger}$ suppose at first that $Y$ is a simple random variable, and let $y_{1}, \ldots, y_{m}$ be its different possible values. Since $A_{i}=\left[\omega: Y(\omega)=y_{i}\right]$ lies in $\sigma(X)$, it must by part (i) have the form $\left[\omega: X(\omega) \in H_{i}\right]$ for some $H_{i}$ in $\mathscr{R}^{k}$. Put $f=\sum_{i} y_{i} I_{H_{i}}$; certainly $f$ is measurable. Since the $A_{i}$ are disjoint, no $X(\omega)$ can lie in more than one $H_{i}$ (even though the latter need not be disjoint), and hence $f(X(\omega))=Y(\omega)$.

[^5]To treat the general case, consider simple random variables $Y_{n}$ such that $Y_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow Y(\omega)$ for each $\omega$. For each $n$, there is a measurable function $f_{n}$ : $R^{k} \rightarrow R^{1}$ such that $Y_{n}(\omega)=f_{n}(X(\omega))$ for all $\omega$. Let $M$ be the set of $x$ in $R^{k}$ for which $\left\{f_{n}(x)\right\}$ converges; by Theorem 13.4(iii), $M$ lies in $\mathscr{R}^{k}$. Let $f(x)=\lim _{n} f_{n}(x)$ for $x$ in $M$, and let $f(x)=0$ for $x$ in $R^{k}-M$. Since $f=\lim _{n} f_{n} I_{M}$ and $f_{n} I_{M}$ is measurable, $f$ is measurable by Theorem 13.4(ii). For each $\omega, Y(\omega)=\lim _{n} f_{n}(X(\omega))$; this implies in the first place that $X(\omega)$ lies in $M$ and in the second place that $Y(\omega)=\lim _{n} f_{n}(X(\omega))=f(X(\omega))$.

## Distributions

The distribution or law of a random variable $X$ was in Section 14 defined as the probability measure on the line given by $\mu=P X^{-1}$ (see (13.7)), or

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu(A)=P[X \in A], \quad A \in \mathscr{R}^{1} \tag{20.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

The distribution function of $X$ was defined by

$$
\begin{equation*}
F(x)=\mu(-\infty, x]=P[X \leq x] \tag{20.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

for real $x$. The left-hand limit of $F$ satisfies

$$
\begin{align*}
F(x-)=\mu(-\infty, x) & =P[X<x]  \tag{20.5}\\
F(x)-F(x-)=\mu\{x\} & =P[X=x]
\end{align*}
$$

and $F$ has at most countably many discontinuities. Further, $F$ is nondecreasing and right-continuous, and $\lim _{x \rightarrow-\infty} F(x)=0, \lim _{x \rightarrow \infty} F(x)=1$. By Theorem 14.1, for each $F$ with these properties there exists on some probability space a random variable having $F$ as its distribution function.

A support for $\mu$ is a Borel set $S$ for which $\mu(S)=1$. A random variable, its distribution, and its distribution function are discrete if $\mu$ has a countable support $S=\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots\right\}$. In this case $\mu$ is completely determined by the values $\mu\left\{x_{1}\right\}, \mu\left\{x_{2}\right\}, \ldots$.

A familiar discrete distribution is the binomial:

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[X=r]=\mu\{r\}=\binom{n}{r} p^{r}(1-p)^{n-r}, \quad r=0,1, \ldots, n \tag{20.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

There are many random variables, on many spaces, with this distribution: If $\left\{X_{k}\right\}$ is an independent sequence such that $P\left[X_{k}=1\right]=p$ and $P\left[X_{k}=0\right]=1 -p$ (see Theorem 5.3), then $X$ could be $\sum_{i=1}^{n} X_{i}$, or $\sum_{i=9}^{8+n} X_{i}$, or the sum of any $n$ of the $X_{i}$, Or $\Omega$ could be $\{0,1, \ldots, n\}$ if $\mathscr{F}$ consists of all subsets, $P\{r\}=\mu\{r\}, r=0,1, \ldots, n$, and $X(r) \equiv r$. Or again the space and random variable could be those given by the construction in either of the two proofs of Theorem 14.1. These examples show that, although the distribution of a
random variable $X$ contains all the information about the probabilistic behavior of $X$ itself, it contains beyond this no further information about the underlying probability space $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ or about the interaction of $X$ with other random variables on the space.

Another common discrete distribution is the Poisson distribution with parameter $\lambda>0$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[X=r]=\mu\{r\}=e^{-\lambda} \frac{\lambda^{r}}{r!}, \quad-\quad r=0,1, \ldots . \tag{20.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

A constant $c$ can be regarded as a discrete random variable with $X(\omega) \equiv c$. In this case $P[X=c]=\mu\{c\}=1$. For an artificial discrete example, let $\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots\right\}$ be an enumeration of the rationals, and put

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu\left\{x_{r}\right\}=2^{-r} ; \tag{20.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

the point of the example is that the support need not be contained in a lattice.

A random variable and its distribution have density $f$ with respect to Lebesgue measure if $f$ is a nonnegative Borel function on $R^{1}$ and

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[X \in A]=\mu(A)=\int_{A} f(x) d x, \quad A \in \mathscr{R}^{1} \tag{20.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

In other words, the requirement is that $\mu$ have density $f$ with respect to Lebesgue measure $\lambda$ in the sense of (16.11). The density is assumed to be with respect to $\lambda$ if no other measure is specified.

Taking $A=R^{1}$ in (20.9) shows that $f$ must integrate to 1 . Note that $f$ is determined only to within a set of Lebesgue measure 0 : if $f=g$ except on a set of Lebesgue measure 0 , then $g$ can also serve as a density for $X$ and $\mu$.

It follows by Theorem 3.3 that (20.9) holds for every Borel set $A$ if it holds for every interval-that is, if

$$
F(b)-F(a)=\int_{a}^{b} f(x) d x
$$

holds for every $a$ and $b$. Note that $F$ need not differentiate to $f$ everywhere (see (20.13), for example); all that is required is that $f$ integrate properly-that (20.9) hold. On the other hand, if $F$ does differentiate to $f$ and $f$ is continuous, it follows by the fundamental theorem of calculus that $f$ is indeed a density for $F .^{\dagger}$

[^6]For the exponential distribution with parameter $\alpha>0$, the density is

$$
f(x)= \begin{cases}0 & \text { if } x<0  \tag{20.10}\\ \alpha e^{-\alpha x} & \text { if } x \geq 0\end{cases}
$$

The corresponding distribution function

$$
F(x)= \begin{cases}0 & \text { if } x \leq 0  \tag{20.11}\\ 1-e^{-\alpha x} & \text { if } \geq 0\end{cases}
$$

was studied in Section 14.
For the nornal distribution with parameters $m$ and $\sigma, \sigma>0$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
f(x)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi} \sigma} \exp \left[-\frac{(x-m)^{2}}{2 \sigma^{2}}\right], \quad-\infty<x<\infty ; \tag{20.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

a change of variable together with (18.10) shows that $f$ does integrate to 1 . For the standard normal distribution, $m=0$ and $\sigma=1$.

For the uniform distribution over an interval $(a, b]$,

$$
f(x)= \begin{cases}\frac{1}{b-a} & \text { if } a<x \leq b  \tag{20.13}\\ 0 & \text { otherwise }\end{cases}
$$

The distribution function $F$ is useful if it has a simple expression, as in (20.11). It is ordinarily simpler to describe $\mu$ by means of a density $f(x)$ or discrete probabilities $\mu\left\{x_{r}\right\}$.

If $F$ comes from a density, it is continuous. In the discrete case, $F$ increases in jumps; the example (20.8), in which the points of discontinuity are dense, shows that it may nonetheless be very irregular. There exist distributions that are not discrete but are not continuous either. An example is $\mu(A)=\frac{1}{2} \mu_{1}(A)+\frac{1}{2} \mu_{2}(A)$ for $\mu_{1}$ discrete and $\mu_{2}$ coming from a density; such mixed cases arise, but they are few. Section 31 has examples of a more interesting kind, namely functions $F$ that are continuous but do not come from any density. These are the functions singular in the sense of Lebesgue; the $Q(x)$ describing bold play in gambling (see (7.33)) turns out to be one of them. See Example 31.1.

If $X$ has distribution $\mu$ and $g$ is a real function of a real variable,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[g(X) \in A]=P\left[X \in g^{-1} A\right]=\mu\left(g^{-1} A\right) . \tag{20.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

Thus the distribution of $g(X)$ is $\mu g^{-1}$ in the notation (13.7).

In the case where there is a density, $f$ and $F$ are related by

$$
\begin{equation*}
F(x)=\int_{-\infty}^{x} f(t) d t \tag{20.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

Hence $f$ at its continuity points must be the derivative of $F$. As noted above, if $F$ has a continuous derivative, this derivative can serve as the density $f$. Suppose that $f$ is continuous and $g$ is increasing, and let $T=g^{-1}$. The distribution function of $g(X)$ is $P[g(X) \leq x]=P[X \leq T(x)]=F(T(x))$. If $T$ is differentiable, this differentiates to $f(T(x)) T^{\prime}(x)$, which is therefore the density for $g(X)$. If $g$ is decreasing, on the other hand, then $P[g(X)<x]= P[X>T(x)]=1-F(T(x))$, and the derivative is equal to $-f(T(x)) T^{\prime}(x)= f(T(x))\left|T^{\prime}(x)\right|$. In either case, $g(X)$ has density

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{d}{d x} P[g(X) \leq x]=f(T(x))\left|T^{\prime}(x)\right| . \tag{20.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $X$ has the normal density (20.12) and $a>0$, (20.16) shows that $a X+b$ has the normal density with parameters $a m+b$ and $a \sigma$. Finding the density of $g(X)$ from first principles, as in the argument leading to (20.16), often works even if $g$ is many-to-one:

Example 20.1. If $X$ has the standard normal distribution, then

$$
P\left[X^{2} \leq x\right]=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{-\sqrt{x}}^{\sqrt{x}} e^{-t^{2} / 2} d t=\frac{2}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{0}^{\sqrt{x}} e^{-t^{2} / 2} d t
$$

for $x>0$. Hence $X^{2}$ has density

$$
f(x)= \begin{cases}0 & \text { if } x \leq 0, \\ \frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} x^{-1 / 2} e^{-x / 2} & \text { if } x>0 .\end{cases}
$$

## Multidimensional Distributions

For a $k$-dimensional random vector $X=\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$, the distribution $\mu$ (a probability measure on $\mathscr{R}^{k}$ ) and the distribution function $F$ (a real function on $R^{k}$ ) are defined by

$$
\begin{align*}
\mu(A) & =P\left[\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right) \in A\right], \quad A \in \mathscr{R}^{k},  \tag{20.17}\\
F\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right) & =P\left[X_{1} \leq x_{1}, \ldots, X_{k} \leq x_{k}\right]=\mu\left(S_{x}\right),
\end{align*}
$$

where $S_{x}=\left[y: y_{i} \leq x_{i}, i=1, \ldots, k\right]$ consists of the points "southwest" of $x$.

Often $\mu$ and $F$ are called the joint distribution and joint distribution function of $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$.

Now $F$ is nondecreasing in each variable, and $\Delta_{A} F \geq 0$ for bounded rectangles $A$ (see (12.12)). As $h$ decreases to 0 , the set

$$
S_{x, h}=\left[y: y_{i} \leq x_{i}+h, i=1, \ldots, k\right]
$$

decreases to $S_{x}$, and therefore (Theorem 2.1(ii)) $F$ is continuous from above in the sense that $\lim _{h \downarrow 0} F\left(x_{1}+h, \ldots, x_{k}+h\right)=F\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right)$. Further, $F\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right) \rightarrow 0$ if $x_{i} \rightarrow-\infty$ for some $i$ (the other coordinates held fixed), and $F\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right) \rightarrow 1$ if $x_{i} \rightarrow \infty$ for each $i$. For any $F$ with these properties there is by Theorem 12.5 a unique probability measure $\mu$ on $\mathscr{R}^{k}$ such that $\mu(A)=\Delta_{A}{ }^{F}$ for bounded rectangles $A$, and $\mu\left(S_{x}\right)=F(x)$ for all $x$.

As $h$ decreases to $0, S_{x,-h}$ increases to the interior $S_{x}^{\circ}=\left[y: y_{i}<x_{i}\right.$, $i=1, \ldots, k]$ of $S_{x}$, and so

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{h \downarrow 0} F\left(x_{1}-h, \ldots, x_{k}-h\right)=\mu\left(S_{x}^{\circ}\right) . \tag{20.18}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $F$ is nondecreasing in each variable, it is continuous at $x$ if and only if it is continuous from below there in the sense that this last limit coincides with $F(x)$. Thus $F$ is continuous at $x$ if and only if $F(x)=\mu\left(S_{x}\right)=\mu\left(S_{x}^{\circ}\right)$, which holds if and only if the boundary $\partial S_{x}=S_{x}-S_{x}^{\circ}$ (the $y$-set where $y_{i} \leq x_{i}$ for all $i$ and $y_{i}=x_{i}$ for some $i$ ) satisfies $\mu\left(\partial S_{x}\right)=0$. If $k>1, F$ can have discontinuity points even if $\mu$ has no point masses: if $\mu$ corresponds to a uniform distribution of mass over the segment $B=[(x, 0): 0<x<1]$ in the plane $(\mu(A)=\lambda[x: 0<x<1,(x, 0) \in A]$ ), then $F$ is discontinuous at each point of $B$. This also shows that $F$ can be discontinuous at uncountably many points. On the other hand, for fixed $x$ the boundaries $\partial S_{x, h}$ are disjoint for different values of $h$, and so (Theorem 10.2(iv)) only countably many of them can have positive $\mu$-measure. Thus $x$ is the limit of points $\left(x_{1}+h, \ldots, x_{k}+h\right)$ at which $F$ is continuous: the continuity points of $F$ are dense.

There is always a random vector having a given distribution and distribution function: Take $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)=\left(R^{k}, \mathscr{R}^{k}, \mu\right)$ and $X(\omega) \equiv \omega$. This is the obvious extension of the construction in the first proof of Theorem 14.1.

The distribution may as for the line be discrete in the sense of having countable support. It may have density $f$ with respect to $k$-dimensional Lebesgue measure: $\mu(A)=\int_{A} f(x) d x$. As in the case $k=1$, the distribution $\mu$ is more fundamental than the distribution function $F$, and usually $\mu$ is described not by $F$ but by a density or by discrete probabilities.

If $X$ is a $k$-dimensional random vector and $g: R^{k} \rightarrow R^{i}$ is measurable, then $g(X)$ is an $i$-dimensional random vector; if the distribution of $X$ is $\mu$, the distribution of $g(X)$ is $\mu g^{-1}$, just as in the case $k=1$-see (20.14). If $g_{j}$ : $R^{k} \rightarrow R^{1}$ is defined by $g_{j}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right)=x_{j}$, then $g_{j}(X)$ is $X_{j}$, and its distribution $\mu_{j}=\mu g_{j}^{-1}$ is given by $\mu_{j}(A)=\mu\left[\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right): x_{j} \in A\right]=P\left[X_{j} \in A\right]$ for
$A \in \mathscr{R}^{1}$. The $\mu_{j}$ are the marginal distributions of $\mu$. If $\mu$ has a density $f$ in $R^{k}$, then $\mu_{j}$ has over the line the density

$$
\begin{equation*}
f_{j}(x)=\int_{R^{k-1}} f\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{j-1}, x, x_{j+1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right) d x_{1} \cdots d x_{j-1} d x_{j+1} \cdots d x_{k} \tag{20.19}
\end{equation*}
$$

since by Fubini's theorem the right side integrated over $A$ comes to $\mu\left[\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right): x_{j} \in A\right]$.

Now suppose that $g$ is a one-to-one, continuously differentiable map of $V$ onto $U$, where $U$ and $V$ are open sets in $R^{k}$. Let $T$ be the inverse, and suppose its Jacobian $J(x)$ never vanishes. If $X$ has a density $f$ supported by $V$, then for $A \subset U, P[g(X) \in A]=P[X \in T A]=\int_{T A} f(y) d y$, and by (17.10), this equals $\int_{A} f(T x)|J(x)| d x$. Therefore, $g(X)$ has density

$$
d(x)= \begin{cases}f(T x)|J(x)| & \text { for } x \in U,  \tag{20.20}\\ 0 & \text { for } x \notin U .\end{cases}
$$

This is the analogue of (20,16).
Example 20.2. Suppose that ( $X_{1}, X_{2}$ ) has density

$$
f\left(x_{1}, x_{2}\right)=(2 \pi)^{-1} \exp \left[-\frac{1}{2}\left(x_{1}^{2}+x_{2}^{2}\right)\right],
$$

and let $g$ be the transformation to polar coordinates. Then $U, V$, and $T$ are as in Example 17.7. If $R$ and $\Theta$ are the polar coordinates of ( $X_{1}, X_{2}$ ), then $(R, \Theta)=g\left(X_{1}, X_{2}\right)$ has density $(2 \pi)^{-1} \rho e^{-\rho^{2} / 2}$ in $V$. By (20.19), $R$ has density $\rho e^{-\rho^{2} / 2}$ on $(0, \infty)$, and $\Theta$ is uniformly distributed over $(0,2 \pi)$. $\square$

For the normal distribution in $R^{k}$, see Section 29.

## Independence

Random variables $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are defined to be independent if the $\sigma$-fields $\sigma\left(X_{1}\right), \ldots, \sigma\left(X_{k}\right)$ they generate are independent in the sense of Section 4. This concept for simple random variables was studied extensively in Chapter 1; the general case was touched on in Section 14. Since $\sigma\left(X_{i}\right)$ consists of the sets [ $X_{i} \in H$ ] for $H \in \mathscr{R}^{1}, X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are independent if and only if

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[X_{1} \in H_{1}, \ldots, X_{k} \in H_{k}\right]=P\left[X_{1} \in H_{1}\right] \cdots P\left[X_{k} \in H_{k}\right] \tag{20.21}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all linear Borel sets $H_{1}, \ldots, H_{k}$. The definition (4.10) of independence requires that (20.21) hold also if some of the events [ $X_{i} \in H_{i}$ ] are suppressed on each side, but this only means taking $H_{i}=R^{1}$.

Suppose that

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[X_{1} \leq x_{1}, \ldots, X_{k} \leq x_{k}\right]=P\left[X_{1} \leq x_{1}\right] \cdots P\left[X_{k} \leq x_{k}\right] \tag{20.22}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all real $x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}$; it then also holds if some of the events [ $X_{i} \leq x_{i}$ ] are suppressed on each side (let $x_{i} \rightarrow \infty$ ). Since the intervals ( $-\infty, x$ ] form a $\pi$-system generating $\mathscr{R}^{1}$, the sets $\left[X_{i} \leq x\right]$ form a $\pi$-system generating $\sigma\left(X_{i}\right)$. Therefore, by Theorem 4.2, (20.22) implies that $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are independent. If, for example, the $X_{i}$ are integer-vaiued, it is enough that $P\left[X_{1}=\right. \left.n_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}=n_{k}\right]=P\left[X_{1}=n_{1}\right] \cdots P\left[X_{k}=n_{k}\right]$ for integral $n_{1}, \ldots, n_{k}$ (see (5.9)).

Let ( $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ ) have distribution $\mu$ and distribution function $F$, and let the $X_{i}$ have distributions $\mu_{i}$ and distribution functions $F_{i}$ (the marginals). By (20.21), $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are independent if and only if $\mu$ is product measure in the sense of Section 18:

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu=\mu_{1} \times \cdots \times \mu_{k} . \tag{20.23}
\end{equation*}
$$

By (20.22), $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are independent if and only if

$$
\begin{equation*}
F\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right)=F_{1}\left(x_{1}\right) \cdots F_{k}\left(x_{k}\right) . \tag{20.24}
\end{equation*}
$$

Suppose that each $\mu_{i}$, has density $f_{i}$; by Fubini's theorem, $f_{1}\left(y_{1}\right) \cdots f_{k}\left(y_{k}\right)$ integrated over $\left(-\infty, x_{1}\right] \times \cdots \times\left(-\infty, x_{k}\right]$ is just $F_{1}\left(x_{1}\right) \cdots F_{k}\left(x_{k}\right)$, so that $\mu$ has density

$$
\begin{equation*}
f(x)=f_{1}\left(x_{1}\right) \cdots f_{k}\left(x_{k}\right) \tag{20.25}
\end{equation*}
$$

in the case of independence.
If $\mathscr{H}_{1}, \ldots, \mathscr{H}_{k}$ are independent $\sigma$-fields and $X_{i}$ is measurable $\mathscr{H}_{i}, i= 1, \ldots, k$, then certainly $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are independent.

If $X_{i}$ is a $d_{i}$-dimensional random vector, $i=1, \ldots, k$, then $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are by definition independent if the $\sigma$-fields $\sigma\left(X_{1}\right), \ldots, \sigma\left(X_{k}\right)$ are independent. The theory is just as for random variables: $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are independent if and only if (20.21) holds for $H_{1} \in \mathscr{R}^{d_{1}}, \ldots, H_{k} \in \mathscr{R}^{d_{k}}$. Now ( $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ ) can be regarded as a random vector of dimension $d=\sum_{i=1}^{k} d_{i}$; if $\mu$ is its distribution in $R^{d}=R^{d_{i}} \times \cdots \times R^{d_{k}}$ and $\mu_{i}$ is the distribution of $X_{i}$ in $R^{d_{i}}$, then, just as before, $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are independent if and only if $\mu=\mu_{1} \times \cdots \times \mu_{k}$. In none of this need the $d_{i}$ components of a single $X_{i}$ be themselves independent random variables.

An infinite collection of random variables or random vectors is by definition independent if each finite subcollection is. The argument following (5.10)
extends from collections of simple random variables to collections of random vectors:

Theorem 20.2. Suppose that

$$
\begin{array}{ccc}
X_{11} & X_{12} & \cdots  \tag{20.26}\\
X_{21} & X_{22} & \cdots \\
\vdots & \vdots &
\end{array}
$$

is an independent collection of random vectors. If $\mathscr{F}_{i}$ is the $\sigma$-field generated by the $i$ th row, then $\mathscr{F}_{1}, \mathscr{F}_{2}, \ldots$ are independent.

Proof. Let $\mathscr{A}_{i}$ consist of the finite intersections of sets of the form [ $X_{i j} \in H$ ] with $H$ a Borel set in a space of the appropriate dimension, and apply Theorem 4.2. The $\sigma$-fields $\mathscr{F}_{i}=\sigma\left(\mathscr{A}_{i}\right), i=1, \ldots, n$, are independent for each $n$, and the result follows. $\square$

Each row of (20.26) may be finite or infinite, and there may be finitely or infinitely many rows. As a matter of fact, rows may be uncountable and there may be uncountably many of them.

Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ are independent random vectors with distributions $\mu$ and $\nu$ in $R^{j}$ and $R^{k}$. Then $(X, Y)$ has distribution $\mu \times \nu$ in $R^{j} \times R^{k}=R^{j+k}$. Let $x$ range over $R^{j}$ and $y$ over $R^{k}$. By Fubini's theorem,

$$
\begin{equation*}
(\mu \times \nu)(B)=\int_{R^{j}} \nu[y:(x, y) \in B] \mu(d x), \quad B \in \mathscr{R}^{j+k} . \tag{20.27}
\end{equation*}
$$

Replace $B$ by $\left(A \times R^{k}\right) \cap B$, where $A \in \mathscr{R}^{j}$ and $B \in \mathscr{R}^{j+k}$. Then (20.27) reduces to

$$
\begin{align*}
(\mu \times \nu)\left(\left(A \times R^{k}\right) \cap B\right)=\int_{A} \nu[y:(x, y) \in B] \mu(d x) &  \tag{20.28}\\
& A \in \mathscr{R}^{j}, \quad B \in \mathscr{R}^{j+k}
\end{align*}
$$

If $B_{x}=[y:(x, y) \in B]$ is the $x$-section of $B$, so that $B_{x} \in \mathscr{R}^{k}$ (Theorem 18.1), then $P[(x, Y) \in B]=P[\omega:(x, Y(\omega)) \in B]=P\left[\omega: Y(\omega) \in B_{x}\right]=\nu\left(B_{x}\right)$. Expressing the formulas in terms of the random vectors themselves gives this result:

Theorem 20.3. If $X$ and $Y$ are independent random vectors with distributions $\mu$ and $\nu$ in $R^{j}$ and $R^{k}$, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[(X, Y) \in B]=\int_{R^{j}} P[(x, Y) \in B] \mu(d x), \quad B \in \mathscr{R}^{j+k} \tag{20.29}
\end{equation*}
$$

and

$$
\begin{align*}
& P[X \in A,(X, Y) \in B]=\int_{A} P[(x, Y) \in B] \mu(d x),  \tag{20.30}\\
& A \in \mathscr{R}^{j}, \quad B \in \mathscr{R}^{j+k} .
\end{align*}
$$

Example 20.3. Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ are independent exponentially distributed random variables. By (20.29), $P[Y / X \geq z]=\int_{0}^{\infty} P[Y / x \geq z] \alpha e^{-\alpha x} d x=\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-\alpha x z} \alpha e^{-\alpha x} d x=(1+z)^{-1}$. Thus $Y / X$ has density $(1+z)^{-2}$ for $z \geq 0$. Since $P\left[X \geq z_{1}, Y / X \geq z_{2}\right]=\int_{z_{1}}^{\infty} P\left[Y / x \geq z_{2}\right] \alpha e^{-\alpha x} d x$ by (20.30), the joint distribution of $X$ and $Y / X$ can be calculated as well.

The formulas (20.29) and (20.30) are constantly applied as in this example. There is no virtue in making an issue of each case, however, and the appeal to Theorem 20.3 is usually silent.

Example 20.4. Here is a more complicated argument of the same sort. Let $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ be independent random variables, each uniformly distributed over $[0, t]$. Let $Y_{k}$ be the $k$ th smallest among the $X_{i}$, so that $0 \leq Y_{1} \leq \cdots \leq Y_{n} \leq t$. The $X_{i}$ divide $[0, t]$ into $n+1$ subintervals of lengths $Y_{1}, Y_{2}-Y_{1}, \ldots, Y_{n}-Y_{n-1}, t-Y_{n}$; let $M$ be the maximum of these lengths. Define $\psi_{n}(t, a)=P[M \leq a]$. The problem is to show that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\psi_{n}(t, a)=\sum_{k=0}^{n+1}(-1)^{k}\binom{n+1}{k}\left(1-k \frac{a}{t}\right)_{+}^{n}, \tag{20.31}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $x_{+}=(x+|x|) / 2$ denotes positive part.
Separate consideration of the possibilities $0 \leq a \leq t / 2, t / 2 \leq a \leq t$, and $t \leq a$ disposes of the case $n=1$. Suppose it is shown that the probability $\psi_{n}(t, a)$ satisfies the recursion

$$
\begin{equation*}
\psi_{n}(t, a)=n \int_{0}^{a} \psi_{n-1}(t-x, a)\left(\frac{t-x}{t}\right)^{n-1} \frac{d x}{t} \tag{20.32}
\end{equation*}
$$

Now (as follows by an integration together with Pascal's identity for binomial coefficients) the right side of (20.31) satisfies this same recursion, and so it will follow by induction that (20.31) holds for all $n$.

In intuitive form, the argument for (20.32) is this: If [ $M \leq a$ ] is to hold, the smallest of the $X_{i}$ must have some value $x$ in $[0, a]$. If $X_{1}$ is the smallest of the $X_{i}$, then $X_{2}, \ldots, X_{n}$ must all lie in $[x, t]$ and divide it into subintervals of length at most $a$; the probability of this is $(1-x / t)^{n-1} \psi_{n-1}(t-x, a)$, because $X_{2}, \ldots, X_{n}$ have probability $(1-x / t)^{n-1}$ of all lying in $[x, t]$, and if they do, they are independent and uniformly distributed there. Now (20.32) results from integrating with respect to the density for $X_{1}$ and multiplying by $n$ to allow for the fact that any of $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ may be the smallest.

To make this argument rigorous, apply (20.30) for $j=1$ and $k=n-1$. Let $A$ be the interval $[0, a]$, and let $B$ consist of the points $\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}\right)$ for which $0 \leq x_{i} \leq t, x_{1}$ is the minimum of $x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}$, and $x_{2}, \ldots, x_{n}$ divide $\left[x_{1}, t\right]$ into subintervals of length at most $a$. Then $P\left[X_{1}=\min X_{i}, M \leq a\right]=P\left[X_{1} \in A,\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right) \in B\right]$. Take $X_{1}$ for
$X$ and $\left(X_{2}, \ldots, X_{n}\right)$ for $Y$ in (20.30). Since $X_{1}$ has density $1 / t$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[X_{1}=\min X_{i}, M \leq a\right]=\int_{0}^{a} P\left[\left(x, X_{2}, \ldots, X_{n}\right) \in B\right] \frac{d x}{t} . \tag{20.33}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $C$ is the event that $x \leq X_{i} \leq t$ for $2 \leq i \leq n$, then $P(C)=(1-x / t)^{n-1}$. A simple calculation shows that $P\left[X_{i}-x \leq s_{i}, 2 \leq i \leq n \mid C\right]=\prod_{i=2}^{n}\left(s_{i} /(t-x)\right)$; in other words, given $C$, the random variables $X_{2}-x, \ldots, X_{n}-x$ are conditionally independent and uniformly distributed over $[0, t-x]$. Now $X_{2}, \ldots, X_{n}$ are random variables on some probability space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ); replacing $P$ by $P(\cdot \mid C)$ shows that the integrand in (20.33) is the same as that in (20.32). The same argument holds with the index 1 replaced by any $k(1 \leq k \leq n)$, which gives (20.32). (The events [ $X_{k}=\min X_{i}, Y \leq a$ ] are not disjoint, but any two intersect in a set of probability 0 .) $\square$

## Sequences of Random Variables

Theorem 5.3 extends to general distributions $\mu_{n}$.
Theorem 20.4. If $\left\{\mu_{n}\right\}$ is a finite or infinite sequence of probability measures on $\mathscr{R}^{1}$, there exists on some probability space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) an independent sequence $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ of random variables such that $X_{n}$ has distribution $\mu_{n}$.

Proof. By Theorem 5.3 there exists on some probability space an independent sequence $Z_{1}, Z_{2}, \ldots$ of random variables assuming the values 0 and 1 with probabilities $P\left[Z_{n}=0\right]=P\left[Z_{n}=1\right]=\frac{1}{2}$. As a matter of fact, Theorem 5.3 is not needed: take the space to be the unit interval and the $Z_{n}(\omega)$ to be the digits of the dyadic expansion of $\omega$-the functions $d_{n}(\omega)$ of Sections and 1 and 4.

Relabel the countably many random variables $Z_{n}$ so that they form a double array,

$$
\begin{array}{ccc}
Z_{11} & Z_{12} & \cdots \\
Z_{21} & Z_{22} & \cdots \\
\vdots & \vdots &
\end{array}
$$

All the $Z_{n k}$ are independent. Put $U_{n}=\Sigma_{k=1}^{\infty} Z_{n k} 2^{-k}$ The series certainly converges, and $U_{n}$ is a random variable by Theorem 13.4. Further, $U_{1}, U_{2}, \ldots$ is, by Theorem 20.2, an independent sequence.

Now $P\left[Z_{n i}=z_{i}, 1 \leq i \leq k\right]=2^{-k}$ for each sequence $z_{1}, \ldots, z_{k}$ of 0 's and 1 's; hence the $2^{k}$ possible values $j 2^{-k}, 0 \leq j<2^{k}$, of $S_{n k}=\sum_{i=1}^{k} Z_{n i} 2^{-i}$ all have probability $2^{-k}$. If $0 \leq x<1$, the number of the $j 2^{-k}$ that lie in $[0, x]$ is $\left\lfloor 2^{k} x\right\rfloor+1$, and therefore $P\left[S_{n k} \leq x\right]=\left(\left\lfloor 2^{k} x\right\rfloor+1\right) / 2^{k}$. Since $S_{n k}(\omega) \uparrow U_{n}(\omega)$ as $k \uparrow \infty$, it follows that $\left[S_{n k} \leq x\right] \downarrow\left[U_{n} \leq x\right]$ as $k \uparrow \infty$, and so $P\left[U_{n} \leq x\right]= \lim _{k} P\left[S_{n k} \leq x\right]=\lim _{k}\left(\left|2^{k} x\right|+1\right) / 2^{k}=x$ for $0 \leq x<1$. Thus $U_{n}$ is uniformly distributed over the unit interval.

The construction thus far establishes the existence of an independent sequence of random variables $U_{n}$ each uniformly distributed over $[0,1]$. Let $F_{n}$ be the distribution function corresponding to $\mu_{n}$, and put $\varphi_{n}(u)=\inf [x$ : $\left.u \leq F_{n}(x)\right]$ for $0<u<1$. This is the inverse used in Section 14-see (14.5). Set $\varphi_{n}(u)=0$, say, for $u$ outside ( 0,1 ), and put $X_{n}(\omega)=\varphi_{n}\left(U_{n}(\omega)\right)$. Since $\varphi_{n}(u) \leq x$ if and only if $u \leq F_{n}(x)$-see the argument following (14.5)- $P\left[X_{n}\right. \leq x]=P\left[U_{n} \leq F_{n}(x)\right]=F_{n}(x)$. Thus $X_{n}$ has distribution function $F_{n}$. And by Theorem 20.2, $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent.

This theorem of course includes Theorem 5.3 as a special case, and its proof does not depend on the earlier result. Theorem 20.4 is a special case of Kolmogorov's existence theorem in Section 36.

## Convolution

Let $X$ and $Y$ be independent random variables with distributions $\mu$ and $\nu$. Apply (20.27) and (20.29) to the planar set $B=[(x, y): x+y \in H]$ with $H \in \mathscr{R}^{1}$ :

$$
\begin{align*}
P[X+Y \in H] & =\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \nu(H-x) \mu(d x)  \tag{20.34}\\
& =\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} P[Y \in H-x] \mu(d x)
\end{align*}
$$

The convolution of $\mu$ and $\nu$ is the measure $\mu * \nu$ defined by

$$
\begin{equation*}
(\mu * \nu)(H)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \nu(H-x) \mu(d x), \quad H \in \mathscr{R}^{1} \tag{20.35}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $X$ and $Y$ are independent and have distributions $\mu$ and $\nu$, (20.34) shows that $X+Y$ has distribution $\mu * \nu$. Since addition of random variables is commutative and associative, the same is true of convolution: $\mu * \nu=\nu * \mu$ and $\mu *(\nu * \eta)=(\mu * \nu) * \eta$.

If $F$ and $G$ are the distribution functions corresponding to $\mu$ and $\nu$, the distribution function corresponding to $\mu * \nu$ is denoted $F * G$. Taking $H=$ ( $-\infty, y$ ] in (20.35) shows that

$$
\begin{equation*}
(F * G)(y)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} G(y-x) d F(x) \tag{20.36}
\end{equation*}
$$

(See (17.22) for the notation $d F(x)$.) If $G$ has density $g$, then $G(y-x)= \int_{-\infty}^{y-x} g(s) d s=\int_{-\infty}^{y} g(t-x) d t$, and so the right side of (20.36) is $\int_{-\infty}^{y}\left[\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} g(t\right. -x) d F(x)] d t$ by Fubini's theorem. Thus $F * G$ has density $F * g$, where

$$
\begin{equation*}
(F * g)(y)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} g(y-x) d F(x) \tag{20.37}
\end{equation*}
$$

this holds if $G$ has density $g$. If, in addition, $F$ has density $f$, (20.37) is denoted $f * g$ and reduces by (16.12) to

$$
\begin{equation*}
(f * g)(y)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} g(y-x) f(x) d x \tag{20.38}
\end{equation*}
$$

This defines convolution for densities, and $\mu * \nu$ has density $f * g$ if $\mu$ and $\nu$ have densities $f$ and $g$. The formula (20.38) can be used for many explicit calculations.

Example 20.5. Let $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ be independent random variables, each with the exponential density (20.10). Define $g_{k}$ by

$$
\begin{equation*}
g_{k}(x)=\alpha \frac{(\alpha x)^{k-1}}{(k-1)!} e^{-\alpha x}, \quad x \geq 0, \quad k=1,2, \ldots ; \tag{20.39}
\end{equation*}
$$

put $g_{k}(x)=0$ for $x \leq 0$. Now

$$
\left(g_{k-1} * g_{1}\right)(y)=\int_{0}^{y} g_{k-1}(y-x) g_{1}(x) d x,
$$

which reduces to $g_{k}(y)$. Thus $g_{k}=g_{k-1} * g_{1}$, and since $g_{1}$ coincides with (20.10), it follows by induction that the sum $X_{1}+\cdots+X_{k}$ has density $g_{k}$. The corresponding distribution function is

$$
\begin{equation*}
G_{k}(x)=1-e^{-\alpha x} \sum_{i=0}^{k-1} \frac{(\alpha x)^{i}}{i!}=\sum_{i=k}^{\infty} e^{-\alpha x} \frac{(\alpha x)^{i}}{i!}, \quad x \geq 0, \tag{20.40}
\end{equation*}
$$

as follows by differentiation. $\square$

Example 20.6. Suppose that $X$ has the normal density (20.12) with $m=0$ and that $Y$ has the same density with $\tau$ in place of $\sigma$. If $X$ and $Y$ are independent, then $X+Y$ has density

$$
\frac{1}{2 \pi \sigma \tau} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \exp \left[-\frac{(y-x)^{2}}{2 \sigma^{2}}-\frac{x^{2}}{2 \tau^{2}}\right] d x .
$$

A change of variable $u=x\left(\sigma^{2}+\tau^{2}\right)^{1 / 2} / \sigma \tau$ reduces this to

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi\left(\sigma^{2}+\tau^{2}\right)}} \frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \exp \left[-\frac{1}{2}\left(u-y \frac{\tau / \sigma}{\sqrt{\sigma^{2}+\tau^{2}}}\right)^{2}-\frac{y^{2}}{2\left(\sigma^{2}+\tau^{2}\right)}\right] d u \\
& \quad=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi\left(\sigma^{2}+\tau^{2}\right)}} e^{-y^{2} / 2\left(\sigma^{2}+\tau^{2}\right)}
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus $X+Y$ has the normal density with $m=0$ and with $\sigma^{2}+\tau^{2}$ in place of $\sigma^{2}$. $\square$

If $\mu$ and $\nu$ are arbitrary finite measures on the line, their convolution is defined by (20.35) even if they are not probability measures.

## Convergence in Probability

Random variables $X_{n}$ converge in probability to $X$, written $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} X$, if (20.41)

$$
\lim _{n} P\left[\left|X_{n}-X\right| \geq \epsilon\right]=0
$$

for each positive $\epsilon .^{\dagger}$ This is the same as (5.7), and the proof of Theorem 5.2 carries over without change (see also Example 5.4)

Theorem 20.5. (i) If $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ with probability I, then $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} X$.
(ii) A necessary and sufficient condition for $X_{n} \rightarrow_{P} X$ is that each subsequence $\left\{X_{n_{\mathrm{A}}}\right\}$ contain a further subsequence $\left\{X_{n_{k(i)}}\right\}$ such that $X_{n_{k(i)}} \rightarrow X$ with probability 1 as $i \rightarrow \infty$

Proof. Only part (ii) needs proof. If $X_{n} \rightarrow_{P} X$, then given $\left\{n_{k}\right\}$, choose a subsequence $\left\{n_{k(i)}\right\}$ so that $k \geq k(i)$ implies that $P\left[\left|X_{n_{k}}-X\right| \geq i^{-1}\right]<2^{-i}$. By the first Borel-Cantelli lemma there is probability 1 that $\left|X_{n_{A(i)}}-X\right|<i^{-1}$ for all but finitely many $i$. Therefore, $\lim _{i} X_{n_{k(i)}}=X$ with probability 1 .

If $X_{n}$ does not converge to $X$ in probability, there is some positive $\epsilon$ for which $P\left[\left|X_{n_{1}}-X\right| \geq \epsilon\right]>\epsilon$ holds along some sequence $\left(n_{k}\right)$. No subsequence of $\left\{X_{n_{1}}\right\}$ can converge to $X$ in probability, and hence none can converge to $X$ with probability 1. $\square$

It follows from (ii) that if $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} X$ and $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} Y$, then $X=Y$ with probability 1 . It follows further that if $f$ is continuous and $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} X$, then $f\left(X_{n}\right) \rightarrow_{p} f(X)$.

In nonprobabilistic contexts, convergence in probability becomes convergence in measure: If $f_{n}$ and $f$ are real measurable functions on a measure space $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, \mu)$, and if $\mu\left[\omega:\left|f(\omega)-f_{n}(\omega)\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \rightarrow 0$ for each $\epsilon>0$, then $f_{n}$ converges in measure to $f$.

## The Glivenko-Cantelli Theorem*

The empirical distribution function for random variables $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ is the distribution function $F_{n}(x, \omega)$ with a jump of $n^{-1}$ at each $X_{k}(\omega)$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
F_{n}(x, \omega)=\frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} I_{(-\infty, x]}\left(X_{k}(\omega)\right) . \tag{20.42}
\end{equation*}
$$

[^7]If the $X_{k}$ have a common unknown distribution function $F(x)$, then $F_{n}(x, \omega)$ is its natural estimate. The estimate has the right limiting behavior, according to the Glivenko-Cantelli theorem:

Theorem 20.6. Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and have a common distribution function $F$; put $D_{n}(\omega)=\sup _{x}\left|F_{n}(x, \omega)-F(x)\right|$. Then $D_{n} \rightarrow 0$ with probability 1.

For each $x, F_{n}(x, \omega)$ as a function of $\omega$ is a random variable. By right continuity, the supremum above is unchanged if $x$ is restricted to the rationals, and therefore $D_{n}$ is a random variable.

The summands in (20.42) are independent, identically distributed simple random variables, and so by the strong law of large numbers (Theorem 6.1), for each $x$ there is a set $A_{x}$ of probability 0 such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} F_{n}(x, \omega)=F(x) \tag{20.43}
\end{equation*}
$$

for $\omega \notin A_{x}$. But Theorem 20.6 says more, namely that (20.43) holds for $\omega$ outside some set $A$ of probability 0 , where $A$ does not depend on $x$-as there are uncountably many of the sets $A_{x}$, it is conceivable a priori that their union might necessarily have positive measure. Further, the convergence in (20.43) is uniform in $x$. Of course, the theorem implies that with probability 1 there is weak convergence $F_{n}(x, \omega) \Rightarrow F(x)$ in the sense of Section 14.

Proof of the Theorem. As already observed, the set $A_{x}$ where (20.43) fails has probability 0 . Another application of the strong law of large numbers, with $I_{(-\infty, x)}$ in place of $I_{(-\infty, x]}$ in (20.42), shows that (see (20.5)) $\lim _{n} F_{n}(x-, \omega)=F(x-)$ except on a set $B_{x}$ of probability 0 . Let $\varphi(u)= \inf [x: u \leq F(x)]$ for $0<u<1$ (see (14.5)), and put $x_{m, k}=\varphi(k / m), m \geq 1$, $1 \leq k \leq m$. It is not hard to see that $F(\varphi(u)-) \leq u \leq F(\varphi(u))$; hence $F\left(x_{m, k}\right.$ -) - $F\left(x_{m, k-1}\right) \leq m^{-1}, \quad F\left(x_{m, 1}-\right) \leq m^{-1}$, and $F\left(x_{m, m}\right) \geq 1-m^{-1}$. Let $D_{m, n}(\omega)$ be the maximum of the quantities $\left|F_{n}\left(x_{m, k}, \omega\right)-F\left(x_{m, k}\right)\right|$ and $\left|F_{n}\left(x_{m, k}-, \omega\right)-F\left(x_{m, k}-\right)\right|$ for $k=1, \ldots, m$.

If $x_{m, k-1} \leq x<x_{m, k}$, then $F_{n}(x, \omega) \leq F_{n}\left(x_{m, k}-, \omega\right) \leq F\left(x_{m, k}-\right)+ D_{m, n}(\omega) \leq F(x)+m^{-1}+D_{m, n}(\omega)$ and $F_{n}(x, \omega) \geq F_{n}\left(x_{m, k-1}, \omega\right) \geq F\left(x_{m, k-1}\right) -D_{m, n}(\omega) \geq F(x)-m^{-1}-D_{m, n}(\omega)$. Together with similar arguments for the cases $x<x_{m, 1}$ and $x \geq x_{m, m}$, this shows that

$$
\begin{equation*}
D_{n}(\omega) \leq D_{m, n}(\omega)+m^{-1} . \tag{20.44}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $\omega$ lies outside the union $A$ of all the $A_{x_{m k}}$ and $B_{x_{m k}}$, then $\lim _{n} D_{m, n}(\omega) =0$ and hence $\lim _{n} D_{n}(\omega)=0$ by (20.44). But $A$ has probability 0 . $\square$

## PROBLEMS

20.1. $2.11 \uparrow$ A necessary and sufficient condition for a $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{A}$ to be countably generated is that $\mathscr{G}=\sigma(X)$ for some random variable $X$. Hint: If $\mathscr{G}= \sigma\left(A_{1}, A_{2}, \ldots\right)$, consider $X=\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} f\left(I_{A_{k}}\right) / 10^{k}$, where $f(x)$ is 4 for $x=0$ and 5 for $x \neq 0$.
20.2. If $X$ is a positive random variable with density $f$, then $X^{-1}$ has density $f(1 / x) / x^{2}$. Prove this by (20.16) and by a direct argument.
20.3. Suppose that a two-dimensional distribution function $F$ has a continuous density $f$. Show that $f(x, y)=\partial^{2} F(x, y) / \partial x \partial y$.
20.4. The construction in Theorem 20.4 requires only Lebesgue measure on the unit interval. Use the theorem to prove the existence of Lebesgue measure on $R^{k}$. First construct $\lambda_{k}$ restricted to $(-n, n] \times \cdot \times(-n, n]$, and then pass to the limit $(n \rightarrow \infty)$. The idea is to argue from first principles, and not to use previous constructions, such as those in Theorems 12.5 and 18.2.
20.5. Suppose that $A, B$, and $C$ are positive, independent random variables with distribution function $F$. Show that the quadratic $A z^{2}+B z+C$ has real zeros with probability $\int_{0}^{\infty} \int_{0}^{\infty} F\left(x^{2} / 4 y\right) d F(x) d F(y)$.
20.6. Show that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent if $\sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}\right)$ and $\sigma\left(X_{n}\right)$ are independent for each $n$.
20.7. Let $X_{0}, X_{1}, \ldots$ be a persistent, irreducible Markov chain, and for a fixed state $j$ let $T_{1}, T_{2}, \ldots$ be the times of the successive passages through $j$. Let $Z_{1}=T_{1}$ and $Z_{n}=T_{n}-T_{n-1}, n \geq 2$. Show that $Z_{1}, Z_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and that $P\left[Z_{n}=k\right]=f_{j j}^{(k)}$ for $n \geq 2$.
20.8. Ranks and records. Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be independent random variables with a common continuous distribution function. Let $B$ be the $\omega$-set where $X_{m}(\omega)=X_{n}(\omega)$ for some pair $m, n$ of distinct integers, and show that $P(B)=0$. Remove $B$ from the space $\Omega$ on which the $X_{n}$ are defined. This leaves the joint distributions of the $X_{n}$ unchanged and makes ties impossible.

Let $T^{(n)}(\omega)=\left(T_{1}^{(n)}(\omega), \ldots, T_{n}^{(n)}(\omega)\right)$ be that permutation $\left(t_{1}, \ldots, t_{n}\right)$ of $(1, \ldots, n)$ for which $X_{t_{1}}(\omega)<X_{t_{2}}(\omega)<\cdots<X_{t_{n}}(\omega)$. Let $Y_{n}$ be the rank of $X_{n}$ among $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}: Y_{n}=r$ if and only if $X_{i}<X_{n}$ for exactly $r-1$ values of $i$ preceding $n$.
(a) Show that $T^{(n)}$ is uniformly distributed over the $n!$ permutations.
(b) Show that $P\left[Y_{n}=r\right]=1 / n, 1 \leq r \leq n$.
(c) Show that $Y_{k}$ is measurable $\sigma\left(T^{(n)}\right)$ for $k \leq n$.
(d) Show that $Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots$ are independent.
20.9. $\uparrow$ Record values. Let $A_{n}$ be the event that a record occurs at time $n$ : $\max _{k<n} X_{k}<X_{n}$.
(a) Show that $A_{1}, A_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and $P\left(A_{n}\right)=1 / n$.
(b) Show that no record stands forever.
(c) Let $N_{n}$ be the time of the first record after time $n$. Show that $P\left[N_{n}=n+\right. k]=n(n+k-1)^{-1}(n+k)^{-1}$.
20.10. Use Fubini's theorem to prove that convolution of finite measures is commutative and associative.
20.11. Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ are independent and have densities. Use (20.20) to find the joint density for $(X+Y, X)$ and then use (20.19) to find the density for $X+Y$. Check with (20.38).
20.12. If $F(x-\epsilon)<F(x+\epsilon)$ for all positive $\epsilon$, then $x$ is a point of increase of $F$ (see Problem 12.9). If $F(x-)<F(x)$, then $x$ is an atom of $F$.
(a) Show that, if $x$ and $y$ are points of increase of $F$ and $G$, then $x+y$ is a point of increase of $F * G$.
(b) Show that, if $x$ and $y$ are atoms of $F$ and $G$, then $x+y$ is an atom of $F * G$.
20.13. Suppose that $\mu$ and $\nu$ consist of masses $\alpha_{n}$ and $\beta_{n}$ at $n, n=0,1,2, \ldots$. Show that $\mu * \nu$ consists of a mass of $\sum_{k=0}^{n} \alpha_{k} \beta_{n-k}$ at $n, n=0,1,2, \ldots$. Show that two Poisson distributions (the parameters may differ) convolve to a Poisson distribution.
20.14. The Cauchy distribution has density

$$
\begin{equation*}
c_{n}(x)=\frac{1}{\pi} \frac{u}{u^{2}+x^{2}}, \quad-\infty<x<\infty, \tag{20.45}
\end{equation*}
$$

for $u>0$. (By (17.9), the density integrates to 1 .)
(a) Show that $c_{u} * c_{l}=c_{u+l}$. Hint: Expand the convolution integrand in partial fractions.
(b) Show that, if $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ are independent and have density $c_{u}$, then $\left(X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}\right) / n$ has density $c_{u}$ as well.
20.15. $\uparrow$ (a) Show that, if $X$ and $Y$ are independent and have the standard normal density, then $X / Y$ has the Cauchy density with $u=1$.
(b) Show that, if $X$ has the uniform distribution over ( $-\pi / 2, \pi / 2$ ), then $\tan X$ has the Cauchy distribution with $u=1$.
20.16. $18.18 \uparrow$ Let $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ be independent, each having the standard normal distribution Show that

$$
\chi_{n}^{2}=X_{1}^{2}+\cdots+X_{n}^{2}
$$

has density

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{1}{2^{n / 2} \Gamma(n / 2)} x^{(n / 2)-1} e^{-x / 2} \tag{20.46}
\end{equation*}
$$

over $(0, \infty)$. This is called the chi-squared distribution with $n$ degrees of freedom.
20.17. ↑ The gamma distribution has density

$$
\begin{equation*}
f(x ; \alpha, u)=\frac{\alpha^{u}}{\Gamma(u)} x^{u-1} e^{-\alpha x} \tag{20.47}
\end{equation*}
$$

over ( $0, \infty$ ) for positive parameters $\alpha$ and $u$. Check that (20.47) integrates to 1 Show that

$$
\begin{equation*}
f(, \alpha, u) * f(; \alpha, v)=f(\cdot ; \alpha, u+v) . \tag{20.48}
\end{equation*}
$$

Note that (20.46) is $f\left(x ; \frac{1}{2}, n / 2\right)$, and from (20.48) deduce again that (20.46) is the density of $\chi_{n}^{2}$. Note that the exponential density (2010) is $f(x ; \alpha, 1)$, and from (20 48) deduce (20 39) once again.
20.18. $\uparrow$ Let $N, X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be independent, where $P[N=n]=q^{n-1} p, n \geq 1$, and each $X_{k}$ has the exponential density $f(x ; \alpha, 1)$. Show that $X_{1}+\cdots+X_{N}$ has density $f(x ; \alpha p, 1)$.
20.19. Let $A_{n m}(\epsilon)=\left[\left|Z_{k}-Z\right|<\epsilon, n \leq k \leq m\right]$. Show that $Z_{n} \rightarrow Z$ with probability 1 if and only if $\lim _{n} \lim _{m} P\left(A_{n m}(\epsilon)\right)=1$ for all positive $\epsilon$, whereas $Z_{n} \rightarrow_{p} Z$ if and only if $\lim _{n} P\left(A_{n q}(\epsilon)\right)=1$ for all positive $\epsilon$.
20.20. (a) Suppose that $f: R^{2} \rightarrow R^{1}$ is continuous. Show that $X_{n} \rightarrow_{P} X$ and $Y_{n} \rightarrow_{P} Y$ imply $f\left(X_{n}, Y_{n}\right) \rightarrow_{p} f(X, Y)$.
(b) Show that addition and multiplication preserve convergence in probability.
20.21. Suppose that the sequence ( $X_{n}$ ) is fundamental in probability in the sense that for $\epsilon$ positive there exists an $N_{\epsilon}$ such that $P\left[\left|X_{m}-X_{n}\right|>\epsilon\right]<\epsilon$ for $m, n>N_{\epsilon}$.
(a) Prove there is a subsequence $\left\{X_{n_{k}}\right\}$ and a random variable $X$ such that $\lim _{k} X_{n_{k}}=X$ with probability 1. Hint: Choose increasing $n_{k}$ such that $P\left[\left|X_{m}-X_{n}\right|>2^{-k}\right]<2^{-k}$ for $m, n \geq n_{k}$. Analyze $P\left[\left|X_{n_{k+1}}-X_{n_{k}}\right|>2^{-k}\right]$.
(b) Show that $X_{n} \rightarrow_{P} X$.
20.22. (a) Suppose that $X_{1} \leq X_{2} \leq \cdots$ and that $X_{n} \rightarrow_{P} X$. Show that $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ with probability 1 .
(b) Show by example that in an infinite measure space functions can converge almost everywhere without converging in measure.
20.23. If $X_{n} \rightarrow 0$ with probability 1 , then $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k} \rightarrow 0$ with probability 1 by the standard theorem on Cesàro means [A30]. Show by example that this is not so if convergence with probability 1 is replaced by convergence in probability.
20.24. $2.19 \uparrow$ (a) Show that in a discrete probability space convergence in probability is equivalent to convergence with probability 1 .
(b) Show that discrete spaces are essentially the only ones where this equivalence holds: Suppose that $P$ has a nonatomic part in the sense that there is a set $A$ such that $P(A)>0$ and $P(\mid A)$ is nonatomic. Construct random variables $X_{n}$ such that $X_{n} \rightarrow_{P} 0$ but $X_{n}$ does not converge to 0 with probability 1.
0.25. $20.2120 .24 \uparrow$ Let $d(X, Y)$ be the infimum of those positive $\epsilon$ for which $P[|X-Y| \geq \epsilon] \leq \epsilon$.
(a) Show that $d(X, Y)=0$ if and only if $X=Y$ with probability 1 . Identify random variables that are equal with probability 1 , and show that $d$ is a metric on the resulting space.
(b) Show that $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} X$ if and only if $d\left(X_{n}, X\right) \rightarrow 0$.
(c) Show that the space is complete.
(d) Show that in general there is no metric $d_{0}$ on this space such that $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ with probability 1 if and only if $d_{0}\left(X_{n}, X\right) \rightarrow 0$.
20.26. Construct in $R^{k}$ a random variable $X$ that is uniformly distributed over the surface of the unit sphere in the sense that $|X|=1$ and $U X$ has the same distribution as $X$ for orthogonal transformations $U$. Hint Let $Z$ be uniformly distributed in the unit ball in $R^{k}$, define $\psi(x)=x /|x|(\psi(0)=(1,0, \quad, 0)$, say), and take $X=\psi(Z)$.
20.27. $\uparrow$ Let $\Theta$ and $\Phi$ be the longitude and latitude of a random point on the surface of the unit sphere in $R^{3}$. Show that $\Theta$ and $\Phi$ are independent, $\Theta$ is uniformly distributed over $[0,2 \pi)$, and $\Phi$ is distributed over $[-\pi / 2,+\pi / 2]$ with density $\frac{1}{2} \cos \phi$

## SECTION 21. EXPECTED VALUES

## Expected Value as Integral

The expected value of a random variable $X$ on ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) is the integral of $X$ with respect to the measure $P$ :

$$
E[X]=\int X d P=\int_{\Omega} X(\omega) P(d \omega)
$$

All the definitions, conventions, and theorems of Chapter 3 apply. For nonnegative $X, E[X]$ is always defined (it may be infinite); for the general $X, E[X]$ is defined, or $X$ has an expected value, if at least one of $E\left[X^{+}\right]$and $E\left[X^{-}\right]$is finite, in which case $E[X]=E\left[X^{+}\right]-E\left[X^{-}\right]$; and $X$ is integrable if and only if $E[|X|]<\infty$. The integral $\int_{A} X d P$ over a set $A$ is defined, as before, as $E\left[I_{A} X\right]$. In the case of simple random variables, the definition reduces to that used in Sections 5 through 9.

## Expected Values and Limits

The theorems on integration to the limit in Section 16 apply. A useful fact: If random variables $X_{n}$ are dominated by an integrable random variable, or if they are uniformly integrable, then $E\left[X_{n}\right] \rightarrow E[X]$ follows if $X_{n}$ converges to $X$ in probability-convergence with probability 1 is not necessary. This follows easily from Theorem 20.5.

## Expected Values and Distributions

Suppose that $X$ has distribution $\mu$. If $g$ is a real function of a real variable, then by the change-of-variable formula (16.17),

$$
\begin{equation*}
E[g(X)]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} g(x) \mu(d x) . \tag{21.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

(In applying (16.17), replace $T: \Omega \rightarrow \Omega^{\prime}$ by $X: \Omega \rightarrow R^{1}, \mu$ by $P, \mu T^{-1}$ by $\mu$, and $f$ by $g$.) This formula holds in the sense explained in Theorem 16.13: It holds in the nonnegative case, so that

$$
\begin{equation*}
E[|g(X)|]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}|g(x)| \mu(d x) ; \tag{212}
\end{equation*}
$$

if one side is infinite, then so is the other. And if the two sides of (21.2) are finite, then (21.1) holds.

If $\mu$ is disciete and $\mu\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots\right\}=1$, then (21.1) becomes (use Theorem 16.9)

$$
\begin{equation*}
E[g(X)]=\sum_{r} g\left(x_{r}\right) \mu\left\{x_{r}\right\} . \tag{21.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $X$ has density $f$, then (21.1) becomes (use Theorem 16.11)

$$
\begin{equation*}
E[g(X)]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} g(x) f(x) d x \tag{21.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $F$ is the distribution function of $X$ and $\mu$, (21.1) can be written $E[g(X)] =\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} g(x) d F(x)$ in the notation (17.22).

## Moments

By (21.2), $\mu$ and $F$ determine all the absolute moments of $X$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[|X|^{k}\right]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}|x|^{k} \mu(d x)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}|x|^{k} d F(x), \quad k=1,2, \ldots \tag{21.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $j \leq k$ implies that $|x|^{j} \leq 1+|x|^{k}$, if $X$ has a finite absolute moment of order $k$, then it has finite absolute moments of orders $1,2, \ldots, k-1$ as well. For each $k$ for which (2.15) is finite, $X$ has $k$ th moment

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[X^{k}\right]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x^{k} \mu(d x)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x^{k} d F(x) \tag{21.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

These quantities are also referred to as the moments of $\mu$ and of $F$. They can be computed by (21.3) and (21.4) in the appropriate circumstances.

Example 21.1. Consider the normal density (20.12) with $m=0$ and $\sigma=1$. For each $k, x^{k} e^{-x^{2} / 2}$ goes to 0 exponentially as $x \rightarrow \pm \infty$, and so finite moments of all orders exist. Integration by parts shows that

$$
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x^{k} e^{-x^{2} / 2} d x=\frac{k-1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x^{k-2} e^{-x^{2} / 2} d x, \quad k=2,3, \ldots .
$$

(Apply (18.16) to $g(x)=x^{k-2}$ and $f(x)=x e^{-x^{2} / 2}$, and let $a \rightarrow-\infty, b \rightarrow \infty$.) Of course, $E[X]=0$ by symmetry and $E\left[X^{0}\right]=1$. It follows by induction that

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[X^{2 k}\right]=1 \times 3 \times 5 \times \cdots \times(2 k-1), \quad k=1,2, \ldots, \tag{21.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

and that the odd moments all vanish. $\square$

If the first two moments of $X$ are finite and $E[X]=m$, then just as in Section 5, the variance is

$$
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{Var}[X] & =E\left[(X-m)^{2}\right]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}(x-m)^{2} \mu(d x)  \tag{21.8}\\
& =E\left[X^{2}\right]-m^{2} .
\end{align*}
$$

From Example 21.1 and a change of variable, it follows that a random variable with the normal density (20.12) has mean $m$ and variance $\sigma^{2}$.

Consider for nonnegative $X$ the relation

$$
\begin{equation*}
E[X]=\int_{0}^{\infty} P[X>t] d t=\int_{0}^{\infty} P[X \geq t] d t \tag{21.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $P[X=t]$ can be positive for at most countably many values of $t$, the two integrands differ only on a set of Lebesgue measure 0 and hence the integrals are the same. For $X$ simple and nonnegative, (21.9) was proved in Section 5; see (5.29). For the general nonnegative $X$, let $X_{n}$ be simple randem variables for which $0 \leq X_{n} \uparrow X$ (see (20.1)). By the monotone convergence theorem $E\left[X_{n}\right] \uparrow E[X]$; moreover, $P\left[X_{n}>t\right] \uparrow P[X>t]$, and therefore $\int_{0}^{\infty} P\left[X_{n}>t\right] d t \uparrow \int_{0}^{\infty} P[X>t] d t$, again by the monotone convergence theorem. Since (21.9) holds for each $X_{n}$, a passage to the limit establishes (21.9) for $X$ itself. Note that both sides of (21.9) may be infinite. If the integral on the right is finite, then $X$ is integrable.

Replacing $X$ by $X I_{[X>\alpha]}$ leads from (21.9) to

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{[X>\alpha]} X d P=\alpha P[X>\alpha]+\int_{\alpha}^{\infty} P[X>t] d t, \quad \alpha \geq 0 \tag{21.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

As long as $\alpha \geq 0$, this holds even if $X$ is not nonnegative.

## Inequalities

Since the final term in (21.10) is nonnegative, $\alpha P[X \geq \alpha] \leq f_{[X \geq \alpha]} X d P \leq E[X]$. Thus

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[X \geq \alpha] \leq \frac{1}{\alpha} \int_{[X \geq \alpha]} X d P \leq \frac{1}{\alpha} E[X], \quad \alpha>0, \tag{21.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

for nonnegative $X$. As in Section 5, there follow the inequalities

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[|X| \geq \alpha] \leq \frac{1}{\alpha^{k}} \int_{[|X| \geq \alpha]}|X|^{k} d P \leq \frac{1}{\alpha^{k}} E\left[|X|^{k}\right] \tag{21.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

It is the inequality between the two extreme terms here that usually goes under the name of Markov; but the left-hand inequality is often useful, too. As a special case there is Chebyshev's inequality,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[|X-m| \geq \alpha] \leq \frac{1}{\alpha^{2}} \operatorname{Var}[X] \tag{21.13}
\end{equation*}
$$

$(m=E[X])$.
Jensen's inequality

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi(E[X]) \leq E[\varphi(X)] \tag{21.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

holds if $\varphi$ is convex on an interval containing the range of $X$ and if $X$ and $\varphi(X)$ both have expected values. To prove it, let $l(x)=a x+b$ be a supporting line through $(E[X], \varphi(E[X]))$-a line lying entirely under the graph of $\varphi$ [A33]. Then $a X(\omega)+b \leq \varphi(X(\omega))$, so that $a E[X]+b \leq E[\varphi(X)]$. But the left side of this inequality is $\varphi(E[X])$.

Hölder's inequality is

$$
\begin{equation*}
E[|X Y|] \leq E^{1 / p}\left[|X|^{p}\right] E^{1 / q}\left[|Y|^{q}\right], \quad \frac{1}{p}+\frac{1}{q}=1 . \tag{21.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

For discrete random variables, this was proved in Section 5; see (5.35). For the general case, choose simple random variables $X_{n}$ and $Y_{n}$ satisfying $0 \leq\left|X_{n}\right| \uparrow|X|$ and $0 \leq\left|Y_{n}\right| \uparrow|Y|$; see (20.2). Then (5.35) and the monotone convergence theorem give (21.15). Notice that (21.15) implies that if $|X|^{p}$ and $|Y|^{q}$ are integrable, then so is $X Y$. Schwarz's inequality is the case $p=q=2$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
E[|X Y|] \leq E^{1 / 2}\left[X^{2}\right] E^{1 / 2}\left[Y^{2}\right] . \tag{21,16}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $X$ and $Y$ have second moments, then $X Y$ must have a first moment.

The same reasoning shows that Lyapounov's inequality (5.37) carries over from the simple to the general case.

## Joint Integrals

The relation (21.1) extends to 1 and vectors. Suppose that $\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$ has distribution $\mu$ in $k$-space and $g: R^{k} \rightarrow R^{1}$. By Theorem 16.13,

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[g\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)\right]=\int_{R^{k}} g(x) \mu(d x) \tag{21.17}
\end{equation*}
$$

with the usual provisos about infinite values. For example, $E\left[X_{i} X_{j}\right]= \int_{R^{k}} x_{i} x_{j} \mu(d x)$. If $E\left[X_{i}\right]=m_{i}$, the covariance of $X_{i}$ and $X_{j}$ is

$$
\operatorname{Cov}\left[X_{i}, X_{j}\right]=E\left[\left(X_{i}-m_{i}\right)\left(X_{j}-m_{j}\right)\right]=\int_{R^{k}}\left(x_{i}-m_{i}\right)\left(x_{j}-m_{j}\right) \mu(d x)
$$

Random variables are uncorrelated if they have covariance 0 .

## Independence and Expected Value

Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ are independent. If they are also simple, then $E[X Y]=E[X] E[Y]$, as proved in Section 5-see (5.25). Define $X_{n}$ by (20.2) and similarly define $Y_{n}=\psi_{n}\left(Y^{+}\right)-\psi_{n}\left(Y^{-}\right)$. Then $X_{n}$ and $Y_{n}$ are independent and simple, so that $E\left[\left|X_{n} Y_{n}\right|\right]=E\left[\left|X_{n}\right|\right] E\left[\left|Y_{n}\right|\right]$, and $0 \leq\left|X_{n}\right| \uparrow|X|, 0 \leq\left|Y_{n}\right| \uparrow|Y|$. If $X$ and $Y$ are integrable, then $E\left[\left|X_{n} Y_{n}\right|\right]=E\left[\left|X_{n}\right|\right] E\left[\left|Y_{n}\right|\right] \uparrow E[|X|] \cdot E[|Y|]$, and it follows by the monotone convergence theorem that $E[|X Y|]<\infty$; since $X_{n} Y_{n} \rightarrow X Y$ and $\left|X_{n} Y_{n}\right| \leq|X Y|$, it follows further by the dominated convergence theorem that $E[X Y]=\lim _{n} E\left[X_{n} Y_{n}\right]=\lim _{n} E\left[X_{n}\right] E\left[Y_{n}\right]=E[X] E[Y]$. Therefore, $X Y$ is integrable if $X$ and $Y$ are (which is by no means true for dependent random variables) and $E[X Y]=E[X] E[Y]$.

This argument obviously extends inductively: If $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are independent and integrable, then the product $X_{1} \cdots X_{k}$ is also integrable and

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[X_{1} \cdots X_{k}\right]=E\left[X_{1}\right] \cdots E\left[X_{k}\right] . \tag{21.18}
\end{equation*}
$$

Suppose that $\mathscr{G}_{1}$ and $\mathscr{G}_{2}$ are independent $\sigma$-fields, $A$ lies in $\mathscr{G}_{1}, X_{1}$ is measurable $\mathscr{H}_{1}$, and $X_{2}$ is measurable $\mathscr{L}_{2}$. Then $I_{A} X_{1}$ and $X_{2}$ are independent, so that (21.18) gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{A} X_{1} X_{2} d P=\int_{A} X_{1} d P \cdot E\left[X_{2}\right] \tag{21.19}
\end{equation*}
$$

if the random variables are integrable. In particular,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{A} X_{2} d P=P(A) E\left[X_{2}\right] \tag{21.20}
\end{equation*}
$$

From (21.18) it follows just as for simple random variables (see (5.28)) that variances add for sums of independent random variables. It is even enough that the random variables be independent in pairs.

## Moment Generating Functions

The moment generating function is defined as

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(s)=E\left[e^{s X}\right]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{s x} \mu(d x)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{s x} d F(x) \tag{21.21}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all $s$ for which this is finite (note that the integrand is nonnegative). Section 9 shows in the case of simple random variables the power of moment generating function methods. This function is also called the Laplace transform of $\mu$, especially in nonprobabilistic contexts.

Now $\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{s x} \mu(d x)$ is finite for $s \leq 0$, and if it is finite for a positive $s$, then it is finite for all smaller $s$. Together with the corresponding result for the left half-line, this shows that $M(s)$ is defined on some interval containing 0 . If $X$ is nonnegative, this interval contains $(-\infty, 0]$ and perhaps part of $(0, \infty)$; if $X$ is nonpositive, it contains $[0, \infty)$ and perhaps part of $(-\infty, 0)$. It is possible that the interval consists of 0 alone; this happens, for example, if $\mu$ is concentrated on the integers and $\mu\{n\}=\mu\{-n\}=C / n^{2}$ for $n=1,2, \ldots$.

Suppose that $M(s)$ is defined throughout an interval ( $-s_{0}, s_{0}$ ), where $s_{0}>0$. Since $e^{|s x|} \leq e^{s x}+e^{-s x}$ and the latter function is integrable $\mu$ for $|s|<s_{0}$, so is $\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}|s x|^{k} / k!=e^{|s x|}$. By the corollary to Theorem 16.7, $\mu$ has finite moments of all orders and

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(s)=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{s^{k}}{k!} E\left[X^{k}\right]=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{s^{k}}{k!} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x^{k} \mu(d x), \quad|s|<s_{0} . \tag{21.22}
\end{equation*}
$$

Thus $M(s)$ has a Taylor expansion about 0 with positive radius of convergence if it is defined in some $\left(-s_{0}, s_{0}\right), s_{0}>0$. If $M(s)$ can somehow be calculated and expanded in a series $\sum_{k} a_{k} s^{k}$, and if the coefficients $a_{k}$ can be identified, then, since $a_{k}$ must coincide with $E\left[X^{k}\right] / k!$, the moments of $X$ can be computed: $E\left[X^{k}\right]=a_{k} k!$ It also follows from the theory of Taylor expansions [A29] that $a_{k} k!$ is the $k$ th derivative $M^{(k)}(s)$ evaluated at $s=0$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
M^{(k)}(0)=E\left[X^{k}\right]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x^{k} \mu(d x) \tag{21.23}
\end{equation*}
$$

This holds if $M(s)$ exists in some neighborhood of 0 .
Suppose now that $M$ is defined in some neighborhood of $s$. If $\nu$ has density $e^{s x} / M(s)$ with respect to $\mu$ (see (16.11)), then $\nu$ has moment generating function $N(u)=M(s+u) / M(s)$ for $u$ in some neighborhood of 0 .

But then by $(21.23), N^{(k)}(0)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x^{k} \nu(d x)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x^{k} e^{s x} \mu(d x) / M(s)$, and since $N^{(k)}(0)=M^{(k)}(s) / M(s)$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
M^{(k)}(s)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x^{k} e^{s x} \mu(d x) \tag{21.24}
\end{equation*}
$$

This holds as long as the moment generating function exists in some neighborhood of $s$. If $s=0$, this gives (21,23) again. Taking $k=2$ shows that $M(s)$ is convex in its interval of definition.

Example 21.2. For the standard normal density,

$$
M(s)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{s x} e^{-x^{2} / 2} d x=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} e^{s^{2} / 2} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-(x-s)^{2} / 2} d x
$$

and a change of variable gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(s)=e^{s^{2} / 2} \tag{21.25}
\end{equation*}
$$

The moment generating function in this case defined for all $s$. Since $e^{s^{2} / 2}$ has the expansion

$$
e^{s^{2} / 2}=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{k!}\left(\frac{s^{2}}{2}\right)^{k}=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{1 \times 3 \times \cdots \times(2 k-1)}{(2 k)!} s^{2 k},
$$

the moments can be read off from (21.22), which proves (21.7) once more.
Example 21.3. In the exponential case (20.10), the moment generating function

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(s)=\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{s x} \alpha e^{-\alpha x} d x=\frac{\alpha}{\alpha-s}=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{s^{k}}{\alpha^{k}} \tag{21.26}
\end{equation*}
$$

is defined for $s<\alpha$. By (21.22) the $k$ th moment is $k!\alpha^{-k}$. The mean and variance are thus $\alpha^{-1}$ and $\alpha^{-2}$.

Example 21.4. For the Poisson distribution (20.7),

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(s)=\sum_{r=0}^{\infty} e^{r s} e^{-\lambda} \frac{\lambda^{r}}{r!}=e^{\lambda\left(e^{s}-1\right)} \tag{21.27}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $M^{\prime}(s)=\lambda e^{s} M(s)$ and $M^{\prime \prime}(s)=\left(\lambda^{2} e^{2 s}+\lambda e^{s}\right) M(s)$, the first two moments are $M^{\prime}(0)=\lambda$ and $M^{\prime \prime}(0)=\lambda^{2}+\lambda$; the mean and variance are both $\lambda$.

Let $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ be independent random variables, and suppose that each $X_{i}$ has a moment generating function $M_{i}(s)=E\left[e^{s X_{i}}\right]$ in ( $-s_{0}, s_{0}$ ). For $|s|<s_{0}$, each $\exp \left(s X_{i}\right)$ is integrable, and, since they are independent, their product $\exp \left(s \sum_{i=1}^{k} X_{i}\right)$ is also integrable (see (21.18)). The moment generating function of $X_{1}+\cdots+X_{k}$ is therefore

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(s)=M_{1}(s) \cdots M_{k}(s) \tag{2128}
\end{equation*}
$$

in ( $-s_{0}, s_{0}$ ). This relation for simple random variables was essential to the arguments in Section 9.

For simple random variables it was shown in Section 9 that the moment generating function determines the distribution. This will later be proved for general random variables; see Theorem 22.2 for the nonnegative case and Section 30 for the general case.

## PROBLEMS

21.1. Prove

$$
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-i x^{2} / 2} d x=t^{-1 / 2}
$$

differentiate $k$ times with respect to $t$ inside the integral (justify), and derive (21.7) again.
21.2. Show that, if $X$ has the standard normal distribution, then $E\left[|X|^{2 n+1}\right]= 2^{n} n!\sqrt{2 / \pi}$.
21.3. $20.9 \uparrow$ Records. Consider the sequence of records in the sense of Problem 20.9. Show that the expected waiting time to the next record is infinite.
21.4. 20.14 ↑ Show that the Cauchy distribution has no mean.
21.5. Prove the first Borel-Cantelli lemma by applying Theorem 16.6 to indicator random variables. Why is Theorem 16.6 not enough for the second Borel-Cantelli lemma?
21.6. Prove (21.9) by Fubini's theorem.
21.7. Prove for integrable $X$ that

$$
E[X]=\int_{0}^{\infty} P[X>t] d t-\int_{-\infty}^{0} P[X<t] d t
$$

21.8. (a) Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ have first moments, and prove

$$
E[Y]-E[X]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}(P[X<t \leq Y]-P[Y<t \leq X]) d t
$$

(b) Let ( $X, Y$ ] be a nondegenerate random interval Show that its expected length is the integral with respect to $t$ of the probability that it covers $t$.
21.9. Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ are random variables with distribution functions $F$ and $G$.
(a) Show that if $F$ and $G$ have no common jumps, then $E[F(Y)]+E[G(X)] =1$.
(b) If $F$ is continuous, then $E[F(X)]=\frac{1}{2}$.
(c) Even if $F$ and $G$ have common jumps, if $X$ and $Y$ are taken to be independent, then $E[F(Y)]+E[G(X)]=1+P[X=Y]$.
(d) Even if $F$ has jumps, $E[F(X)]=\frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2} \sum_{x} P^{2}[X=x]$
21.10. (a) Show that uncorrelated variables need not be independent.
(b) Show that $\operatorname{Var}\left[\sum_{i=1}^{n} X_{i}\right]=\sum_{i_{j}=1}^{n} \operatorname{Cov}\left[X_{i}, X_{j}\right]=\sum_{i=1}^{n} \operatorname{Var}\left[X_{i}\right]+ 2 \sum_{1 \leq i<j \leq n} \operatorname{Cov}\left[X_{i}, X_{j}\right]$. The cross terms drop out if the $X_{i}$ are uncorrelated, and hence drop out if they are independent.
21.11. $\uparrow$ Let $X, Y$, and $Z$ be independent random variables such that $X$ and $Y$ assume the values $0,1,2$ with probability $\frac{1}{3}$ each and $Z$ assumes the values 0 and 1 with probabilities $\frac{1}{3}$ and $\frac{2}{3}$. Let $X^{\prime}=X$ and $Y^{\prime}=X+Z(\bmod 3)$.
(a) Show that $X^{\prime}, Y^{\prime}$, and $X^{\prime}+Y^{\prime}$ have the same one-dimensional distributions as $X, Y$, and $X+Y$, respectively, even though ( $X^{\prime}, Y^{\prime}$ ) and ( $X, Y$ ) have different distributions.
(b) Show that $X^{\prime}$ and $Y^{\prime}$ are dependent but uncorrelated
(c) Show that, despite dependence, the moment generating function of $X^{\prime}+Y^{\prime}$ is the product of the moment generating functions of $X^{\prime}$ and $Y^{\prime}$.
21.12. Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ are independent, nonnegative random variables and that $E[X]=\infty$ and $E[Y]=0$. What is the value common to $E[X Y]$ and $E[X] E[Y]$ ? Use the conventions (15.2) for both the product of the random variables and the product of their expected values. What if $E[X]=\infty$ and $0<E[Y]<\infty$ ?
21.13. Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ are independent and that $f(x, y)$ is nonnegative. Put $g(x)=E[f(x, Y)]$ and show that $E[g(X)]=E[f(X, Y)]$. Show more generally that $\int_{X \in A} g(X) d P=\int_{X \in A} f(X, Y) d P$. Extend to $f$ that may be negative.
21.14. $\uparrow$ The integrability of $X+Y$ does not imply that of $X$ and $Y$ separately. Show that it does if $X$ and $Y$ are independent.
21.15. $20.25 \uparrow$ Write $d_{1}(X, Y)=E[|X-Y| /(1+|X-Y|)]$. Show that this is a metric equivalent to the one in Problem 20.25.
21.16. For the density $C \exp \left(-|x|^{1 / 2}\right),-\infty<x<\infty$, show that moments of all orders exist but that the moment generating function exists only at $s=0$.
21.17. 16.6 ↑ Show that a moment generating function $M(s)$ defined in ( $-s_{0}, s_{0}$ ), $s_{0}>0$, can be extended to a function analytic in the strip $\left[z:-s_{0}<\operatorname{Re} z<s_{0}\right]$. If $M(s)$ is defined in $\left[0, s_{0}\right), s_{0}>0$, show that it can be extended to a function continuous in $\left[z: 0 \leq \operatorname{Re} z<s_{0}\right]$ and analytic in $\left[z \cdot 0<\operatorname{Re} z<s_{0}\right]$.
21.18. Use (21.28) to find the generating function of (20.39).
21.19. For independent random variables having moment generating functions, show by (21.28) that the variances add.
21.20. 20.17 Show that the gamma density (20.47) has moment generating function $(1-s / \alpha)^{-u}$ for $s<\alpha$. Show that the $k$ th moment is $u(u+1) \cdots(u+k-$ 1) $/ \alpha^{k}$ Show that the chi-squared distribution with $n$ degrees of freedom has mean $n$ and variance $2 n$.
21.21. Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be identically distributed random variables with finite second moment. Show that $n P\left[\left|X_{1}\right| \geq \epsilon \sqrt{n}\right] \rightarrow 0$ and $n^{-1 / 2} \max _{k \leq n}\left|X_{k}\right| \rightarrow_{p} 0$.

## SECTION 22. SUMS OF INDEPENDENT RANDOM VARIABLES

Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be a sequence of independent random variables on some probability space. It is natural to ask whether the infinite series $\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} X_{n}$ converges with probability 1 , or as in Section 6 whether $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}$ converges to some limit with probability 1 . It is to questions of this sort that the present section is devoted.

Throughout the section, $S_{n}$ will denote the partial sum $\sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}\left(S_{0}=0\right)$.

## The Strong Law of Large Numbers

The central result is a general version of Theorem 6.1.

Theorem 22.1. If $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and identically distributed and have finite mean, then $S_{n} / n \rightarrow E\left[X_{1}\right]$ with probability 1 .

Formerly this theorem stood at the end of a chain of results. The following argument, due to Etemadi, proceeds from first principles.

Proof. If the theorem holds for nonnegative random variables, then $n^{-1} S_{n}=n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}^{+}-n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}^{-} \rightarrow E\left[X_{1}^{+}\right]-E\left[X_{1}^{-}\right]=E\left[X_{1}\right]$ with probability 1 . Assume then that $X_{k} \geq 0$.

Consider the truncated random variables $Y_{k}=X_{k} I_{\left[X_{k} \leq k\right]}$ and their partial sums $S_{n}^{*}=\sum_{k=1}^{n} Y_{k}$. For $\alpha>1$, temporarily fixed, let $u_{n}=\left\lfloor\alpha^{n}\right\rfloor$. The first step is to prove

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} P\left[\left|\frac{S_{u_{n}}^{*}-E\left[S_{u_{n}}^{*}\right]}{u_{n}}\right|>\epsilon\right]<\infty . \tag{22.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since the $X_{n}$ are independent and identically distributed,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\operatorname{Var}\left[S_{n}^{*}\right] & =\sum_{k=1}^{n} \operatorname{Var}\left[Y_{k}\right] \leq \sum_{k=1}^{n} E\left[Y_{k}^{2}\right] \\
& =\sum_{k=1}^{n} E\left[X_{1}^{2} I_{\left[X_{1} \leq k\right]}\right] \leq n E\left[X_{1}^{2} I_{\left[X_{1} \leq n\right]}\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

It follows by Chebyshev's inequality that the sum in (22.1) is at most

$$
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{\operatorname{Var}\left[S_{u_{n}}^{*}\right]}{\epsilon^{2} u_{n}^{2}} \leq \frac{1}{\epsilon^{2}} E\left[X_{1}^{2} \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{u_{n}} I_{\left[X_{1} \leq u_{n}\right]}\right] .
$$

Let $K=2 \alpha /(\alpha-1)$, and suppose $x>0$. If $N$ is the smallest $n$ such that $u_{n} \geq x$, then $\alpha^{N} \geq x$, and since $y \leq 2\lfloor y\rfloor$ for $y \geq 1$,

$$
\sum_{u_{n} \geq x} u_{n}^{-1} \leq 2 \sum_{n \geq N} \alpha^{-n}=K \alpha^{-N} \leq K x^{-1} .
$$

Therefore, $\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} u_{n}^{-1} I_{\left[X_{1} \leq u_{n}\right]} \leq K X_{1}^{-1}$ for $X_{1}>0$, and the sum in (22.1) is at most $K \epsilon^{-2} E\left[X_{1}\right]<\infty$.

From (22.1) it follows by the first Borel-Cantelli lemma (take a union over positive, rational $\epsilon$ ) that $\left(S_{u_{n}}^{*}-E\left[S_{u_{n}}^{*}\right]\right) / u_{n} \rightarrow 0$ with probability 1 . But by the consistency of Cesàro summation [A30], $n^{-1} E\left[S_{n}^{*}\right]=n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} E\left[Y_{k}\right]$ has the same limit as $E\left[Y_{n}\right]$, namely. $E\left[X_{1}\right]$. Therefore $S_{u_{n}}^{*} / u_{n} \rightarrow E\left[X_{1}\right]$ with probability 1 . Since

$$
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} P\left[X_{n} \neq Y_{n}\right]=\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} P\left[X_{1}>n\right] \leq \int_{0}^{\infty} P\left[X_{1}>t\right] d t=E\left[X_{1}\right]<\infty
$$

another application of the first Borel-Cantelli lemma shows that $\left(S_{n}^{*}-S_{n}\right) / n \rightarrow 0$ and hence

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{S_{u_{n}}}{u_{n}} \rightarrow E\left[X_{1}\right] \tag{22.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

with probability 1 .
If $u_{n} \leq k \leq u_{n+1}$, then since $X_{i} \geq 0$,

$$
\frac{u_{n}}{u_{n+1}} \frac{S_{u_{n}}}{u_{n}} \leq \frac{S_{k}}{k} \leq \frac{u_{n+1}}{u_{n}} \frac{S_{u_{n+1}}}{u_{n+1}} .
$$

But $u_{n+1} / u_{n} \rightarrow \alpha$, and so it follows by (22.2) that

$$
\frac{1}{\alpha} E\left[X_{1}\right] \leq \lim \inf _{k} \frac{S_{k}}{k} \leq \lim \sup _{k} \frac{S_{k}}{k} \leq \alpha E\left[X_{1}\right]
$$

with probability 1 . This is true for each $\alpha>1$. Intersecting the corresponding sets over rational $\alpha$ exceeding 1 gives $\lim _{k} S_{k} / k=E\left[X_{1}\right]$ with probability 1 . $\square$

Although the hypothesis that the $X_{n}$ all have the same distribution is used several times in this proof, independence is used only through the equation $\operatorname{Var}\left[S_{n}^{*}\right]=\sum_{k=1}^{n} \operatorname{Var}\left[Y_{k}\right]$, and for this it is enough that the $X_{n}$ be independent in pairs. The proof given for Theorem 6.1 of course extends beyond the case of simple random variables, but it requires $E\left[X_{1}^{4}\right]<\infty$.

Corollary. Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and identically distributed and $E\left[X_{1}^{-}\right]<\infty, E\left[X_{1}^{+}\right]=\infty$ (so that $E\left[X_{1}\right]=\infty$ ). Then $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k} \rightarrow \infty$ with probability 1 .

Proof. By the theorem, $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}^{-} \rightarrow E\left[X_{1}^{-}\right]$with probability 1 , and so it suffices to prove the corollary for the case $X_{1}=X_{1}^{+} \geq 0$. If

$$
X_{n}^{(u)}= \begin{cases}X_{n} & \text { if } 0 \leq X_{n} \leq u, \\ 0 & \text { if } X_{n}>u,\end{cases}
$$

then $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k} \geq n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}^{(u)} \rightarrow E\left[X_{1}^{(u)}\right]$ by the theorem. Let $u \rightarrow \infty$. $\square$

## The Weak Law and Moment Generating Functions

The weak law of large numbers (Section 6) carries over without change to the case of general random variables with second moments-only Chebyshev's inequality is required. The idea can be used to prove in a very simple way that a distribution concentrated on $[0, \infty)$ is uniquely determined by its moment generating function or Laplace transform.

For each $\lambda$, let $Y_{\lambda}$ be a random variable (on some probability space) having the Poisson distribution with parameter $\lambda$. Since $Y_{\lambda}$ has mean and variance $\lambda$ (Example 21.4), Chebyshev's inequality gives

$$
P\left[\left|\frac{Y_{\lambda}-\lambda}{\lambda}\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \leq \frac{\lambda}{\lambda^{2} \epsilon^{2}} \rightarrow 0, \quad \lambda \rightarrow \infty .
$$

Let $G_{\lambda}$ be the distribution function of $Y_{\lambda} / \lambda$, so that

$$
G_{\lambda}(t)=\sum_{k=0}^{\lfloor\lambda t\rfloor} e^{-\lambda} \frac{\lambda^{k}}{k!} .
$$

The result above can be restated as

$$
\lim _{\lambda \rightarrow \infty} G_{\lambda}(t)= \begin{cases}1 & \text { if } t>1,  \tag{22.3}\\ 0 & \text { if } t<1 .\end{cases}
$$

In the notation of Section 14, $G_{\lambda}(x) \Rightarrow \Delta(x-1)$ as $\lambda \rightarrow \infty$.
Now consider a probability distribution $\mu$ concentrated on $[0, \infty)$. Let $F$ be the corresponding distribution function. Define

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(s)=\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-s x} \mu(d x), \quad s \geq 0 \tag{22.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

here 0 is included in the range of integration. This is the moment generating function (21.21), but the argument has been reflected through the origin. It is a one-sided Laplace transform, defined for all nonnegative $s$.

For positive $s$, (21.24) gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
M^{(k)}(s)=(-1)^{k} \int_{0}^{\infty} y^{k} e^{-s y} \mu(d y) \tag{22.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

Therefore, for positive $x$ and $s$,

$$
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k=0}^{\lfloor s x\rfloor} \frac{(-1)^{k}}{k!} s^{k} M^{(k)}(s) & =\int_{0}^{\infty} \sum_{k=0}^{\lfloor s x\rfloor} e^{-s y} \frac{(s y)^{k}}{k!} \mu(d y)  \tag{22.6}\\
& =\int_{0}^{\infty} G_{s y}\left(\frac{x}{y}\right) \mu(d y)
\end{align*}
$$

Fix $x>0$. If ${ }^{\dagger} 0 \leq y<x$, then $G_{s y}(x / y) \rightarrow 1$ as $s \rightarrow \infty$ by (22.3); if $y>x$, the limit is 0 . If $\mu\{x\}=0$, the integrand on the right in (22.6) thus converges as $s \rightarrow \infty$ to $I_{[0, x]}(y)$ except on a set of $\mu$-measure 0 . The bounded convergence theorem then gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{s \rightarrow \infty} \sum_{k=0}^{\lfloor s x\rfloor} \frac{(-1)^{k}}{k!} s^{k} M^{(k)}(s)=\mu[0, x]=F(x) \tag{22.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

${ }^{\dagger}$ If $y=0$, the integrand in (22.5) is 1 for $k=0$ and 0 for $k \geq 1$, hence for $y=0$, the integrand in the middle term of (226) is 1 .

Thus $M(s)$ determines the value of $F$ at $x$ if $x>0$ and $\mu\{x\}=0$, which covers all but countably many values of $x$ in [ $0, \infty$ ). Since $F$ is right-continuous, $F$ itself and hence $\mu$ are determined through (22.7) by $M(s)$. In fact $\mu$ is by (22.7) determined by the values of $M(s)$ for $s$ beyond an arbitrary $s_{0}$ :

Theorem 22.2. Let $\mu$ and $\nu$ be probability measures on $[0, \infty)$. If

$$
\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-s x} \mu(d x)=\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-s x} v(d x), \quad s \geq s_{0}
$$

where $s_{0} \geq 0$, then $\mu=\nu$.
Corollary. Let $f_{1}$ and $f_{2}$ be real functions on $[0, \infty)$. If

$$
\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-s x} f_{\mathrm{I}}(x) d x=\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-s x} f_{2}(x) d x, \quad s \geq s_{0}
$$
