where $s_{0} \geq 0$, then $f_{1}=f_{2}$ outside a set of Lebesgue measure 0 .
The $f_{i}$ need not be nonnegative, and they need not be integrable, but $e^{-s x} f_{i}(x)$ must be integrable over $[0, \infty)$ for $s \geq s_{0}$.

Proof. For the nonnegative case, apply the theorem to the probability densities $g_{i}(x)=e^{-s_{0} x} f_{i}(x) / m$, where $m=\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-s_{0} x} f_{i}(x) d x, i=1,2$. For the general case, prove that $f_{1}^{+}+f_{2}^{-}=f_{2}^{+}+f_{1}^{-}$almost everywhere.

Example 22.1. If $\mu_{1} * \mu_{2}=\mu_{3}$, then the corresponding transforms (22.4) satisfy $M_{1}(s) M_{2}(s)=M_{3}(s)$ for $s \geq 0$. If $\mu_{i}$ is the Poisson distribution with mean $\lambda_{i}$, then (see (21.27)) $M_{i}(s)=\exp \left[\lambda_{i}\left(e^{-s}-1\right)\right]$. It follows by Theorem 22.2 that if two of the $\mu_{i}$ are Poisson, so is the third, and $\lambda_{1}+\lambda_{2}=\lambda_{3}$.

## Kolmogorov's Zero-One Law

Consider the set $A$ of $\omega$ for which $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}(\omega) \rightarrow 0$ as $n \rightarrow \infty$. For each $m$, the values of $X_{1}(\omega), \ldots, X_{m-1}(\omega)$ are irrelevant to the question of whether or not $\omega$ lies in $A$, and so $A$ ought to lie in the $\sigma$-field $\sigma\left(X_{m}, X_{m+1}, \ldots\right)$. In fact, $\lim _{n} n^{-1} \sum_{k=0}^{m-1} X_{k}(\omega)=0$ for fixed $m$, and hence $\omega$ lies in $A$ if and only if $\lim _{n} n^{-1} \sum_{k=m}^{n} X_{k}(\omega)=0$. Therefore,

$$
\begin{equation*}
A=\bigcap_{\epsilon} \bigcup_{N \geq m} \bigcap_{n \geq N}\left[\omega:\left|n^{-1} \sum_{k=m}^{n} X_{k}(\omega)\right|<\epsilon\right], \tag{22,8}
\end{equation*}
$$

the first intersection extending over positive rational $\epsilon$. The set on the inside
lies in $\sigma\left(X_{m}, X_{m+1}, \ldots\right)$, and hence so does $A$. Similarly, the $\omega$-set where the series $\sum_{n} X_{n}(\omega)$ converges lies in each $\sigma\left(X_{m}, X_{m+1}, \ldots\right)$.

The intersection $\mathscr{T}=\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} \sigma\left(X_{n}, X_{n+1}, \ldots\right)$ is the tail $\sigma$-field associated with the sequence $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$; its elements are tail events. In the case $X_{n}=I_{A_{n}}$, this is the $\sigma$-field (4.29) studied in Section 4. The following general form of Kolmogorov's zero-one law extends Theorem 4.5.

Theorem 22.3. Suppose that $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is independent and that $A \in \mathscr{T}= \cap_{n=1}^{\infty} \sigma\left(X_{n}, X_{n+1}, \ldots\right)$. Then either $P(A)=0$ or $P(A)=1$.

Proof. Let $\mathscr{F}_{0}=\bigcup_{k=1}^{\infty} \sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$. The first thing to establish is that $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ is a field generating the $\sigma$-field $\sigma\left(X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots\right)$. If $B$ and $C$ lie in $\mathscr{F}_{0}$, then $B \in \sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{j}\right)$ and $C \in \sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$ for some $j$ and $k$; if $m= \max \{j, k\}$, then $B$ and $C$ both lie in $\sigma\left(X_{\mathrm{i}}, \ldots, X_{m}\right)$, so that $B \cup C \in \sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{m}\right) \subset \mathscr{F}_{0}$. Thus $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ is closed under the formation of finite unions; since it is similarly closed under complementation, $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ is a field. For $H \in \mathscr{R}^{1},\left[X_{n} \in H\right] \in \mathscr{F}_{0} \subset \sigma\left(\mathscr{F}_{0}\right)$, and hence $X_{n}$ is measurable $\sigma\left(\mathscr{F}_{0}\right)$; thus $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ generates $\sigma\left(X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots\right)$ (which in general is much larger than $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ ).

Suppose that $A$ lies in $\mathscr{T}$. Then $A$ lies in $\sigma\left(X_{k+1}, X_{k+2}, \ldots\right)$ for each $k$. Therefore, if $B \in \sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$, then $A$ and $B$ are independent by Theorem 20.2. Therefore, $A$ is independent of $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ and hence by Theorem 4.2 is also independent of $\sigma\left(X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots\right)$. But then $A$ is independent of itself: $P(A \cap A)=P(A) P(A)$. Therefore, $P(A)=P^{2}(A)$, which implies that $P(A)$ is either 0 or 1 . $\square$

As noted above, the set where $\sum_{n} X_{n}(\omega)$ converges satisfies the hypothesis of Theorem 22.3, and so does the set where $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}(\omega) \rightarrow 0$. In many similar cases it is very easy to prove by this theorem that a set at hand must have probability either 0 or 1 . But to determine which of 0 and 1 is, in fact, the probability of the set may be extremely difficult.

## Maximal Inequalities

Essential to the study of random series are maximal inequalities-inequalities concerning the maxima of partial sums. The best known is that of Kolmogorov.

Theorem 22.4. Suppose that $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ are independent with mean 0 and finite variances. For $\alpha>0$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\max _{1 \leq k \leq n}\left|S_{k}\right| \geq \alpha\right] \leq \frac{1}{\alpha^{2}} \operatorname{Var}\left[S_{n}\right] . \tag{22.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Let $A_{k}$ be the set where $\left|S_{k}\right| \geq \alpha$ but $\left|S_{j}\right|<\alpha$ for $j<k$. Since the $A_{k}$ are disjoint,

$$
\begin{aligned}
E\left[S_{n}^{2}\right] & \geq \sum_{k=1}^{n} \int_{A_{k}} S_{n}^{2} d P \\
& =\sum_{k=1}^{n} \int_{A_{k}}\left[S_{k}^{2}+2 S_{k}\left(S_{n}-S_{k}\right)+\left(S_{n}-S_{k}\right)^{2}\right] d P \\
& \geq \sum_{k=1}^{n} \int_{A_{k}}\left[S_{k}^{2}+2 S_{k}\left(\dot{S}_{n}-S_{k}\right)\right] d P
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $A_{k}$ and $S_{k}$ are measurable $\sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$ and $S_{n}-S_{k}$ is measurable $\sigma\left(X_{k+1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right)$, and since the means are all 0 , it follows by (21.19) and independence that $\int_{A_{k}} S_{l}\left(S_{n}-S_{k}\right) d P=0$. Therefore,

$$
\begin{aligned}
E\left[S_{n}^{2}\right] & \geq \sum_{k=1}^{n} \int_{A_{k}} S_{k}^{2} d P \geq \sum_{k=1}^{n} \alpha^{2} P\left(A_{k}\right) \\
& =\alpha^{2} P\left[\max _{1 \leq k \leq n}\left|S_{k}\right| \geq \alpha\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

By Chebyshev's inequality, $P\left[\left|S_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right] \leq \alpha^{-2} \operatorname{Var}\left[S_{\eta}\right]$. That this can be strengthened to (22.9) is an instance of a general phenomenon: For sums of independent variables, if $\max _{k \leq n}\left|S_{k}\right|$ is large, then $\left|S_{n}\right|$ is probably large as well. Theorem 9.6 is an instance of this, and so is the following result, due to Etemadi.

Theorem 22.5. Suppose that $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ are independent. For $\alpha \geq 0$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\max _{1 \leq k \leq n}\left|S_{k}\right| \geq 3 \alpha\right] \leq 3 \max _{1 \leq k \leq n} P\left[\left|S_{k}\right| \geq \alpha\right] \tag{22.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Let $B_{k}$ be the set where $\left|S_{k}\right| \geq 3 \alpha$ but $\left|S_{j}\right|<3 \alpha$ for $j<k$. Since the $B_{k}$ are disjoint,

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left[\max _{1 \leq k \leq n}\left|S_{k}\right| \geq 3 \alpha\right] & \leq P\left[\left|S_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]+\sum_{k=1}^{n-1} P\left(B_{k} \cap\left[\left|S_{n}\right|<\alpha\right]\right) \\
& \leq P\left[\left|S_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]+\sum_{k=1}^{n-1} P\left(B_{k} \cap\left[\left|S_{n}-S_{k}\right|>2 \alpha\right]\right) \\
& =P\left[\left|S_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]+\sum_{k=1}^{n-1} P\left(B_{k}\right) P\left[\left|S_{n}-S_{k}\right|>2 \alpha\right] \\
& \leq P\left[\left|S_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]+\max _{1 \leq k \leq n} P\left[\left|S_{n}-S_{k}\right| \geq 2 \alpha\right] \\
& \leq P\left[\left|S_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]+\max _{1 \leq k \leq n}\left(P\left[\left|S_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]+P\left[\left|S_{k}\right| \geq \alpha\right]\right) \\
& \leq 3 \max _{1 \leq k \leq n} P\left[\left|S_{k}\right| \geq \alpha\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

If the $X_{k}$ have mean 0 and Chebyshev's inequality is applied to the right side of (22.10), and if $\alpha$ is replaced by $\alpha / 3$, the result is Kolmogorov's inequality (22.9) with an extra factor of 27 on the right side. For this reason, the two inequalities are equally useful for the applications in this section.

## Convergence of Random Series

For independent $X_{n}$, the probability that $\sum X_{n}$ converges is either 0 or 1 . It is natural to try and characterize the two cases in terms of the distributions of the individual $X_{n}$.

Theorem 22.6. Suppose that $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is an independent sequence and $E\left[X_{n}\right]=$ 0 . If $\sum \operatorname{Var}\left[X_{n}\right]<\infty$, then $\sum X_{n}$ converges with probability 1 .

Proof. By (22.9),

$$
P\left[\operatorname{inax}_{1 \leq k \leq r}\left|S_{n+k}-S_{n}\right|>\epsilon\right] \leq \frac{1}{\epsilon^{2}} \sum_{k=1}^{r} \operatorname{Var}\left[X_{n+k}\right] .
$$

Since the sets on the left are nondecreasing in $r$, letting $r \rightarrow \infty$ gives

$$
P\left[\sup _{k \geq 1}\left|S_{n+k}-S_{n}\right|>\epsilon\right] \leq \frac{1}{\epsilon^{2}} \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \operatorname{Var}\left[X_{n+k}\right] .
$$

Since $\sum \operatorname{Var}\left[X_{n}\right]$ converges,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} P\left[\sup _{k \geq 1}\left|S_{n+k}-S_{n}\right|>\epsilon\right]=0 \tag{22.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

for each $\epsilon$.
Let $E(n, \epsilon)$ be the set where $\sup _{j, k \geq n}\left|S_{j}-S_{k}\right|>2 \epsilon$, and put $E(\epsilon)= \cap_{n} E(n, \epsilon)$. Then $E(n, \epsilon) \downarrow E(\epsilon)$, and (22.11) implies $P(E(\epsilon))=0$. Now $\mathrm{U}_{\epsilon} E(\epsilon)$, where the union extends over positive rational $\epsilon$, contains the set where the sequence $\left\{S_{n}\right\}$ is not fundamental (does not have the Cauchy property), and this set therefore has probability 0 . $\square$

Example 22.2. Let $X_{n}(\omega)=r_{n}(\omega) a_{n}$, where the $r_{n}$ are the Rademacher functions on the unit interval-see (1.13). Then $X_{n}$ has variance $a_{n}^{2}$, and so $\sum a_{n}^{2}<\infty$ implies that $\sum r_{n}(\omega) a_{n}$ converges with probability 1 . An interesting special case is $a_{n}=n^{-1}$. If the signs in $\Sigma \pm n^{-1}$ are chosen on the toss of a coin, then the series converges with probability 1 . The alternating harmonic series $1-2^{-1}+3^{-1}+\cdots$ is thus typical in this respect. $\square$

If $\sum X_{n}$ converges with probability 1 , then $S_{n}$ converges with probability 1 to some finite random variable $S$. By Theorem 20.5, this implies that
$S_{n} \rightarrow_{p} S$. The reverse implication of course does not hold in general, but it does if the summands are independent.

Theorem 22.7. For an independent sequence $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$, the $S_{n}$ converge with probability 1 if and only if they converge in probability.

Proof. It is enough to show that if $S_{n} \rightarrow_{P} S$, then $\left\{S_{n}\right\}$ is fundamental with probability 1 . Since

$$
P\left[\left|S_{n+j}-S_{n}\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \leq P\left[\left|S_{n+j}-S\right| \geq \frac{\epsilon}{2}\right]+P\left[\left|S_{n}-S\right| \geq \frac{\epsilon}{2}\right],
$$

$S_{n} \rightarrow_{p} S$ implies

$$
\begin{equation*}
\limsup _{n} P\left[\left|S_{n+j}-S_{n}\right| \geq \epsilon\right]=0 \tag{22.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

But by (22.10),

$$
P\left[\max _{1 \leq j \leq k}\left|S_{n+j}-S_{n}\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \leq 3 \max _{1 \leq j \leq k} P\left[\left|S_{n+j}-S_{n}\right| \geq \frac{\epsilon}{3}\right],
$$

and therefore

$$
P\left[\sup _{k \geq 1}\left|S_{n+k}-S_{n}\right|>\epsilon\right] \leq 3 \sup _{k \geq 1} P\left[\left|S_{n+k}-S_{n}\right| \geq \frac{\epsilon}{3}\right]
$$

It now follows by (22.12) that (22.11) holds, and the proof is completed as before.

The final result in this direction, the three-series theorem, provides necessary and sufficient conditions for the convergence of $\sum X_{n}$ in terms of the individual distributions of the $X_{n}$. Let $X_{n}^{(c)}$ be $X_{n}$ truncated at $c: X_{n}^{(c)}= X_{n} I_{\left[\left|X_{n}\right| \leq c\right]}$.

Theorem 22.8. Suppose that $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is independent, and consider the three series

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum P\left[\left|X_{n}\right|>c\right], \quad \sum E\left[X_{n}^{(c)}\right], \quad \sum \operatorname{Var}\left[X_{n}^{(c)}\right] \tag{22.13}
\end{equation*}
$$

In order that $\sum X_{n}$ converge with probability 1 it is necessary that the three series converge for all positive $c$ and sufficient that they converge for some positive $c$.

Proof of Sufficiency. Suppose that the series (22.13) converge, and put $m_{n}^{(c)}=E\left[X_{n}^{(c)}\right]$. By Theorem 22.6, $\Sigma\left(X_{n}^{(c)}-m_{n}^{(c)}\right)$ converges with probability 1 , and since $\sum m_{n}^{(c)}$ converges, so does $\sum X_{n}^{(c)}$. Since $P\left[X_{n} \neq X_{n}^{(c)}\right.$ i.o. $]=0$ by the first Borel-Cantelli lemma, it follows finally that $\sum X_{n}$ converges with probability 1 . $\square$

Although it is possible to prove necessity in the three-series theorem by the methods of the present section, the simplest and clearest argument uses the central limit theorem as treated in Section 27. This involves no circularity of reasoning, since the three-series theorem is nowhere used in what follows.

Proof of Necessity. Suppose that $\sum X_{n}$ converges with probability 1 , and fix $c>0$. Since $X_{n} \rightarrow 0$ with probability 1 , it follows that $\sum X_{n}^{(c)}$ converges with probability 1 and, by the second Borel-Cantelli lemma, that $\sum P\left[\left|X_{n}\right|>c\right]<\infty$.

Let $M_{n}^{(c)}$ and $s_{n}^{(c)}$ be the mean and standard deviation of $S_{n}^{(c)}=\sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}^{(c)}$. If $s_{n}^{(c)} \rightarrow \infty$, then since the $X_{n}^{(c)}-m_{n}^{(c)}$ are uniformly bounded, it follows by the central limit theorem (see Example 27.4) that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} P\left[x<\frac{S_{n}^{(c)}-M_{n}^{(c)}}{s_{n}^{(c)}} \leq y\right]=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{x}^{y} e^{-t^{2} / 2} d t . \tag{22.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

And since $\sum X_{n}^{(c)}$ converges with probability $1, s_{n}^{(c)} \rightarrow \infty$ also implies $S_{n}^{(c)} / s_{n}^{(c)} \rightarrow 0$ with probability 1 , so that (Theorem 20.5)

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} P\left[\left|S_{n}^{(c)} / s_{n}^{(c)}\right| \geq \epsilon\right]=0 . \tag{22.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

But (22.14) and (22.15) stand in contradiction: Since

$$
P\left[x<\frac{S_{n}^{(c)}-M_{n}^{(c)}}{s_{n}^{(c)}} \leq y,\left|\frac{S_{n}^{(c)}}{s_{n}^{(c)}}\right|<\epsilon\right]
$$

is greater than or equal to the probability in (22.14) minus that in (22.15), it is positive for all sufficiently large $n$ (if $x<y$ ). But then

$$
x-\epsilon<-M_{n}^{(c)} / s_{n}^{(c)}<y+\epsilon,
$$

and this cannot hold simultaneously for, say, $(x-\epsilon, y+\epsilon)=(-1,0)$ and $(x-\epsilon, y+\epsilon)=(0,1)$. Thus $s_{n}^{(c)}$ cannot go to $\infty$, and the third series in (22.13) converges.

And now it follows by Theorem 22.6 that $\sum\left(X_{n}^{(c)}-m_{n}^{(c)}\right)$ converges with probability 1 , so that the middle series in (22.13) converges as well. $\square$

Example 22.3. If $X_{n}=r_{n} a_{n}$, where $r_{n}$ are the Rademacher functions, then $\sum a_{n}^{2}<\infty$ implies that $\sum X_{n}$ converges with probability 1 . If $\sum X_{n}$ converges, then $a_{n}$ is bounded, and for large $c$ the convergence of the third
series in (22.13) implies $\sum a_{n}^{2}<\infty$ : If the signs in $\sum \pm a_{n}$ are chosen on the toss of a coin, then the series converges with probability 1 or 0 according as $\sum a_{n}^{2}$ converges or diverges. If $\sum a_{n}^{2}$ converges but $\sum\left|a_{n}\right|$ diverges, then $\sum \pm a_{n}$ is with probability 1 conditionally but not absolutely convergent.

Example 22.4. If $a_{n} \downarrow 0$ but $\sum a_{n}^{2}=\infty$, then $\sum \pm a_{n}$ converges if the signs are strictly alternating, but diverges with probability 1 if they are chosen on the toss of a coin.

Theorems 22.6, 22.7, and 22.8 concern conditional convergence, and in the most interesting cases, $\sum X_{n}$ converges not because the $X_{n}$ go to 0 at a high rate but because they tend to cancel each other out. In Example 22.4, the terms cancel well enough for convergence if the signs are strictly alternating, but not if they are chosen on the toss of a coin.

## Random Taylor Series*

Consider a power series $\sum \pm z^{n}$, where the signs are chosen on the toss of a coin. The radius of convergence being 1 , the series represents an analytic function in the open unit disk $D_{0}=[z:|z|<1]$ in the complex plane. The question arises whether this function can be extended analytically beyond $D_{0}$. The answer is no: With probability 1 the unit circle is the natural boundary.

Theorem 22.9. Let $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ be an independent sequence such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[X_{n}=1\right]=P\left[X_{n}=-1\right]=\frac{1}{2}, \quad n=0,1, \ldots . \tag{22.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

There is probability 0 that

$$
\begin{equation*}
F(\omega, z)=\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} X_{n}(\omega) z^{n} \tag{22.17}
\end{equation*}
$$

coincides in $D_{0}$ with a function analytic in an open set properly containing $D_{0}$.
It will be seen in the course of the proof that the $\omega$-set in question lies in $\sigma\left(X_{0}, X_{1}, \ldots\right)$ and hence has a probability. It is intuitively clear that if the set is measurable at all, it must depend only on the $X_{n}$ for large $n$ and hence must have probability either 0 or 1 .

Proof. Since

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left|X_{n}(\omega)\right|=1, \quad n=0,1, \ldots \tag{22.18}
\end{equation*}
$$

[^8]with probability 1 , the series in (22.17) has radius of convergence 1 outside a set of measure 0 .

Consider an open disk $D=[z:|z-\zeta|<r]$, where $\zeta \in D_{0}$ and $r>0$. Now (22.17) coincides in $D_{0}$ with a function analytic in $D_{0} \cup D$ if and only if its expansion

$$
F(\omega, z)=\sum_{m=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{m!} F^{(m)}(\omega, \zeta)(z-\zeta)^{m}
$$

about $\zeta$ converges at least for $|z-\zeta|<r$. Let $A_{D}$ be the set of $\omega$ for which this holds. The coefficient

$$
a_{m}(\omega)=\frac{1}{m!} F^{(m)}(\omega, \zeta)=\sum_{n=m}^{\infty}\binom{n}{m} X_{n}(\omega) \zeta^{n-m}
$$

is a complex-valued raudom variable measurable $\sigma\left(X_{m}, X_{m+1}, \ldots\right)$. By the root test, $\omega \in A_{D}$ if and only if $\limsup \sup _{m}\left|a_{m}(\omega)\right|^{1 / m} \leq r^{-1}$. For each $m_{0}$, the condition for $\omega \in A_{D}$ can thus be expressed in terms of $a_{m_{0}}(\omega), a_{m_{0}+1}(\omega), \ldots$ alone, and so $A_{D} \in \sigma\left(X_{m_{10}}, X_{m_{0}+1}, \ldots\right)$. Thus $A_{D}$ has a probability, and in fact $P\left(A_{D}\right)$ is 0 or 1 by the zero-one law.

Of course, $P\left(A_{D}\right)=1$ if $D \subset D_{0}$. The central step in the proof is to show that $P\left(A_{D}\right)=0$ if $D$ contains points not in $D_{0}$. Assume on the contrary that $P\left(A_{D}\right)=1$ for such a $D$. Consider that part of the circumference of the unit circle that lies in $D$, and let $k$ be an integer large enough that this arc has length exceeding $2 \pi / k$. Define

$$
Y_{n}(\omega)=\left\{\begin{aligned}
X_{n}(\omega) & \text { if } n \neq 0(\bmod k), \\
-X_{n}(\omega) & \text { if } n \equiv 0(\bmod k) .
\end{aligned}\right.
$$

Let $B_{D}$ be the $\omega$-set where the function

$$
\begin{equation*}
G(\omega, z)=\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} Y_{n}(\omega) z^{n} \tag{22.19}
\end{equation*}
$$

coincides in $D_{0}$ with a function analytic in $D_{0} \cup D$.
The sequence $\left\{Y_{0}, Y_{1}, \ldots\right\}$ has the same structure as the original sequence: the $Y_{n}$ are independent and assume the values $\pm 1$ with probability $\frac{1}{2}$ each. Since $B_{D}$ is defined in terms of the $Y_{n}$ in the same way as $A_{D}$ is defined in terms of the $X_{n}$, it is intuitively clear that $P\left(B_{D}\right)$ and $P\left(A_{D}\right)$ must be the same. Assume for the moment the truth of this statement, which is somewhat more obvious than its proof.

If for a particular $\omega$ each of (22.17) and (22.19) coincides in $D_{0}$ with a function analytic in $D_{0} \cup D$, the same must be true of

$$
\begin{equation*}
F(\omega, z)-G(\omega, z)=2 \sum_{m=0}^{\infty} X_{m k}(\omega) z^{m k} \tag{22.20}
\end{equation*}
$$

Let $D_{l}=\left[z e^{2 \pi i l / k}: z \in D\right]$. Since replacing $z$ by $z e^{2 \pi i / k}$ leaves the function (22.20) unchanged, it can be extended analytically to each $D_{0} \cup D_{l}, l= 1,2, \ldots$. Because of the choice of $k$, it can therefore be extended analytically to $[z:|z|<1+\epsilon]$ for some positive $\epsilon$; but this is impossible if (22.18) holds, since the radius of convergence must then be 1 .

Therefore, $A_{D} \cap B_{D}$ cannot contain a point $\omega$ satisfying (22.18). Since (22.18) holds with probability 1 , this rules out the possibility $P\left(A_{D}\right)=P\left(B_{D}\right) =1$ and by the zero-one law leaves only the possibility $P\left(A_{D}\right)=P\left(B_{D}\right)=0$. Let $A$ be the $\omega$-set where (22.17) extends to a function analytic in some open set larger than $D_{0}$. Then $\omega \in A$ if and only if (22.17) extends to $D_{0} \cup D$ for some $D=[z:|z-\zeta|<r]$ for which $D-D_{0} \neq \varnothing, r$ is rational, and $\zeta$ has rational real and imaginary parts; in other words, $A$ is the countable union of $A_{D}$ for such $D$. Therefore, $A$ lies in $\sigma\left(X_{0}, X_{1}, \ldots\right)$ and has probability 0 .

It remains only to show that $P\left(A_{D}\right)=P\left(B_{D}\right)$, and this is most easily done by comparing $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ and $\left\{Y_{n}\right\}$ with a canonical sequence having the same structure. Put $Z_{n}(\omega)=\left(X_{n}(\omega)+1\right) / 2$, and let $T \omega$ be $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} Z_{n}(\omega) 2^{-n-1}$ on the $\omega$-set $A^{*}$ where this sum lies in ( 0,1 ]; on $\Omega-A^{*}$ let $T \omega$ be 1 , say. Because of (22.16) $P\left(A^{*}\right)=1$. Let $\mathscr{F}=\sigma\left(X_{0}, X_{1}, \ldots\right)$ and let $\mathscr{B}$ be the $\sigma$-field of Borel subsets of $(0,1]$; then $T: \Omega \rightarrow(0,1]$ is measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{B}$. Let $r_{n}(x)$ be the $n$th Rademacher function. If $M=\left[x: r_{i}(x)=u_{i}, i=1 \ldots, n\right]$, where $u_{i}= \pm 1$ for each $i$, then $P\left(T^{-1} M\right)=P\left[\omega: X_{i}(\omega)=u_{i}, i=0,1, \ldots, n-\right.$ 1] $=2^{-n}$, which is the Lebesgue measure $\lambda(M)$ of $M$. Since these sets form a $\pi$-system generating $\mathscr{B}, P\left(T^{-1} M\right)=\lambda(M)$ for all $M$ in $\mathscr{B}$ (Theorem 3.3).

Let $M_{D}$ be the set of $x$ for which $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} r_{n+1}(x) z^{n}$ extends analytically to $D_{0} \cup D$. Then $M_{D}$ lies in $\mathscr{B}$, this being a special case of the fact that $A_{D}$ lies in $\mathscr{F}$. Moreover, if $\omega \in A^{*}$, then $\omega \in A_{D}$ if and only if $T \omega \in M_{D}: A^{*} \cap A_{D}= A^{*} \cap T^{-1} M_{D}$. Since $P\left(A^{*}\right)=1$, it follows that $P\left(A_{D}\right)=\lambda\left(M_{D}\right)$.

This argument only uses (22.16), and therefore it applies to $\left\{Y_{n}\right\}$ and $B_{D}$ as weil. Therefore, $P\left(B_{D}\right)=\lambda\left(M_{D}\right)=P\left(A_{D}\right)$.

## PROBLEMS

22.1. Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ is an independent sequence and $Y$ is measurable $\sigma\left(X_{n}, X_{n+1}, \ldots\right)$ for each $n$. Show that there exists a constant $a$ such that $P[Y=a]=1$.
22.2. Assume $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ independent, and define $X_{n}^{(c)}$ as in Theorem 22.8. Prove that for $\sum\left|X_{n}\right|$ to converge with probability 1 it is necessary that $\sum P\left[\left|X_{n}\right|>c\right]$ and $\sum E\left[\mid X_{n}^{(c)}\right] \mid$ converge for all positive $c$ and sufficient that they converge for some positive $c$. If the three series (22.13) converge but $\Sigma E\left[\left|X_{n}^{(c)}\right|\right]=\infty$, then there is probability 1 that $\sum X_{n}$ converges conditionally but not absolutely.
22.3. $\uparrow$ (a) Generalize the Borel-Cantelli lemmas: Suppose $X_{n}$ are nonnegative If $\sum E\left[X_{n}\right]<\infty$, then $\sum X_{n}$ converges with probability 1 . If the $X_{n}$ are indepen dent and uniformly bounded, and if $\sum E\left[X_{n}\right]=\infty$, then $\sum X_{n}$ diverges witk probability 1.
(b) Construct independent, nonnegative $X_{n}$ such that $\sum X_{n}$ converges with probability 1 but $\sum E\left[X_{n}\right]$ diverges For an extreme example, arrange that $P\left[X_{n}>0\right.$ i.o. $]=0$ but $E\left[X_{n}\right] \equiv \infty$.
22.4. Show under the hypothesis of Theorem 22.6 that $\sum X_{n}$ has finite variance and extend Theorem 22.4 to infinite sequences.
22.5. $20.1422 .1 \uparrow$ Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent, each with the Cauchy distribution (20.45) for a common value of $u$.
(a) Show that $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}$ does not converge with probability 1 . Contrast with Theorem 22.1.
(b) Show that $P\left[n^{-1} \max _{k \leq n} X_{k} \leq x\right] \rightarrow e^{-u / \pi x}$ for $x>0$. Relate to Theorem 14.3.
22.6. If $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and identically distributed, and if $P\left[X_{1} \geq 0\right]=1$ and $P\left[X_{1}>0\right]>0$, then $\sum_{n} X_{n}=\infty$ with probability 1 . Deduce this from Theorem 22.1 and its corollary and also directly: find a positive $\epsilon$ such that $X_{r}>c$ infinitely often with probability 1 .
22.7. Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and identically distributed and $E\left[\left|X_{1}\right|\right]=\infty$. Use (21.9) to show that $\sum_{n} P\left[\left|X_{n}\right| \geq a n\right]=\infty$ for each $a$, and conclude that $\sup _{n} n^{-1}\left|X_{n}\right|=\infty$ with probability 1 . Now show that $\sup _{n} n^{-1}\left|S_{n}\right| =\infty$ with probability 1. Compare with the corollary to Theorem 22.1.
22.8. Wald's equation. Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be independent and identically distributed with finite mean, and put $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$. Suppose that $\tau$ is a stopping time: $\tau$ has positive integers as values and $[\tau=n] \in \sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right)$; see Section 7 for examples. Suppose also that $E[\tau]<\infty$.
(a) Prove that

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[S_{\tau}\right]=E\left[X_{1} 1 E[\tau]\right. \tag{22.21}
\end{equation*}
$$

(b) Suppose that $X_{n}$ is $\pm 1$ with probabilities $p$ and $q, p \neq q$, let $\tau$ be the first $n$ for which $S_{n}$ is $-a$ or $b$ ( $a$ and $b$ positive integers), and calculate $E[\tau]$. This gives the expected duration of the game in the gambler's ruin problem for unequal $p$ and $q$.
22.9. $20.9 \uparrow$ Let $Z_{n}$ be 1 or 0 according as at time $n$ there is or is not a record in the sense of Problem 20.9. Let $R_{n}=Z_{1}+\cdots+Z_{n}$ be the number of records up to time $n$. Show that $R_{n} / \log n \rightarrow_{p} 1$.
22.10. $22.1 \uparrow$ (a) Show that for an independent sequence $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ the radius of convergence of the random Taylor series $\sum_{n} X_{n} z^{n}$ is $r$ with probability 1 for some nonrandom $r$.
(b) Suppose that the $X_{n}$ have the same distribution and $P\left[X_{1} \neq 0\right]>0$. Show that $r$ is 1 or 0 according as $\log ^{+}\left|X_{1}\right|$ has finite mean or not.
22.11. Suppose that $X_{0}, X_{1}, \ldots$ are independent and each is uniformly distributed over $[0,2 \pi]$. Show that with probability 1 the series $\sum_{n} e^{i X_{n}} z^{n}$ has the unit circle as its natural boundary.
22.12. Prove (what is essentially Kolmogorov's zero-one law) that if $A$ is independent of a $\pi$-system $\mathscr{P}$ and $A \in \sigma(\mathscr{P})$, then $P(A)$ is either 0 or 1 .
22.13. Suppose that $\mathscr{A}$ is a semiring containing $\Omega$.
(a) Show that if $P(A \cap B) \leq b P(B)$ for all $B \in \mathscr{A}$, and if $b<1$ and $A \in \sigma(\mathscr{A})$, then $P(A)=0$.
(b) Show that if $P(A \cap B) \leq P(A) P(B)$ for all $B \in \mathscr{A}$, and if $A \in \sigma(\mathscr{A})$, then $P(A)$ is 0 or 1 .
(c) Show that if $a P(B) \leq P(A \cap B)$ for all $B \in \mathscr{A}$, and if $a>0$ and $A \in \sigma(\mathscr{A})$, then $P(A)=1$.
(d) Show that if $P(A) P(B) \leq P(A \cap B)$ for all $B \in \mathscr{A}$, and if $A \in \sigma(\mathscr{A})$, then $P(A)$ is 0 or 1 .
(e) Reconsider Problem 3.20
22.14. $22.12 \uparrow$ Burstin's theorem. Let $f$ be a Borel function on $[0,1]$ with arbitrarily small periods: For each $\epsilon$ there is a $p$ such that $0<p<\epsilon$ and $f(x)=f(x+p)$ for $0 \leq x \leq 1-p$. Show that such an $f$ is constant almost everywhere:
(a) Show that it is enough to prove that $P\left(f^{-1} B\right)$ is 0 or 1 for every Borel set $B$, where $P$ is Lebesgue measure on the unit interval.
(b) Show that $f^{-1} B$ is independent of each interval $[0, x]$, and conclude that $P\left(f^{-1} B\right)$ is 0 or 1 .
(c) Show by example that $f$ need not be constant.
22.15. Assume that $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ are independent and $s, t, \alpha$ are nonnegative Let

$$
\begin{aligned}
L(s) & =\max _{k \leq n} P\left[\left|S_{k}\right| \geq s\right], R(s)=\max _{k \leq n} P\left[\left|S_{n}-S_{k}\right|>s\right] \\
M(s) & =P\left[\max _{k \leq n}\left|S_{k}\right| \geq s\right], T(s)=P\left[\left|S_{n}\right| \geq s\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

(a) Following the first part of the proof of (22.10), show that

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(s+t) \leq T(t)+M(s+t) R(s) \tag{22.22}
\end{equation*}
$$

(b) Take $s=2 \alpha$ and $t=\alpha$; use (22.22), together with the inequalities $T(s) \leq L(s)$ and $R(2 s) \leq 2 L(s)$, to prove Etemadi's inequality (22.10) in the form

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(3 \alpha) \leq B_{E}(\alpha)==1 \wedge 3 L(\alpha) . \tag{22.23}
\end{equation*}
$$

(c) Carry the rightmost term in (22.22) to the left side, take $s=t=\alpha$, and prove Ottaviani's inequality:

$$
\begin{equation*}
M(2 \alpha) \leq B_{O}(\alpha)=1 \wedge \frac{T(\alpha)}{1-R(\alpha)} \tag{22.24}
\end{equation*}
$$

(d) Prove

$$
B_{E}(\alpha) \leq 3 B_{O}(\alpha / 2), \quad B_{O}(\alpha) \leq 3 B_{E}(\alpha / 6)
$$

This shows that the Etemadi and Ottaviani inequalities are of the same power for most purposes (as, for example, for the proofs of Theorem 22.7 and (37.9)). Etemadi's inequality seems the more natural of the two. Neither inequality can replace (9.39) in the proof of the law of the iterated logarithm.

## SECTION 23. THE POISSON PROCESS

## Characterization of the Exponential Distribution

Suppose that $X$ has the exponential distribution with parameter $\alpha$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[X>x]=e^{-\alpha x}, \quad x \geq 0 . \tag{23.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

The definition (4.1) of conditional probability then gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
P[X>x+y \mid X>x]=P[X>y], \quad x, y \geq 0 . \tag{23.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

Image $X$ as the waiting time for the occurrence of some event such as the arrival of the next customer at a queue or telephone call at an exchange. As observed in Section 14 (see (14.6)), (23.2) attributes to the waiting-time mechanism a lack of memory or aftereffect. And as shown in Section 14, the condition (23.2) implies that $X$ has the distribution (23.1) for some positive $\alpha$. Thus if in the sense of (23.2) there is no aftereffect in the waiting-time mechanism, then the waiting time itself necessarily follows the exponential law.

## The Poisson Process

Consider next a stream or sequence of events, say arrivals of calls at an exchange. Let $X_{1}$ be the waiting time to the first event, let $X_{2}$ be the waiting time between the first and second events, and so on. The formal model consists of an infinite sequence $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ of random variables on some probability space, and $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$ represents the time of occurrence of the $n$th event; it is convenient to write $S_{0}=0$. The stream of events itself remains intuitive and unformalized, and the mathematical definitions and arguments are framed in terms of the $X_{n}$.

If no two of the events are to occur simultaneously, the $S_{n}$ must be strictly increasing, and if only finitely many of the events are to occur in each finite interval of time, $S_{n}$ must go to infinity:

$$
\begin{equation*}
0=S_{0}(\omega)<S_{1}(\omega)<S_{2}(\omega)<\cdots, \quad \sup _{n} S_{n}(\omega)=\infty . \tag{23.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

This condition is the same thing as

$$
\begin{equation*}
X_{1}(\omega)>0, \quad X_{2}(\omega)>0, \ldots, \quad \sum_{n} X_{n}(\omega)=\infty . \tag{23.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

Throughout the section it will be assumed that these conditions hold every-where-for every $\omega$. If they hold only on a set $A$ of probability 1 , and if $X_{n}(\omega)$ is redefined as $X_{n}(\omega)=1$, say, for $\omega \notin A$, then the conditions hold everywhere and the joint distributions of the $X_{n}$ and $S_{n}$ are unaffected.

Condition $0^{\circ}$. For each $\omega$, (23.3) and (23.4) hold.

The arguments go through under the weaker condition that (23.3) and (23.4) hold with probability 1 , but they then involve some fussy and uninteresting details. There are at the outset no further restrictions on the $X_{i}$; they are not assumed independent, for example, or identically distributed.

The number $N_{t}$ of events that occur in the time interval $[0, t]$ is the largest integer $n$ such that $S_{n} \leq t$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
N_{t}=\max \left[n: S_{n} \leq t\right] . \tag{23.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

Note that $N_{t}=0$ if $t<S_{1}=X_{1}$; in particular, $N_{0}=0$. The number of events in ( $s, t$ ] is the increment $N_{t}-N_{s}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/6289d1e6-4ff9-4bb6-b3da-068bafdb2b1b-070.jpg?height=560&width=1240&top_left_y=1058&top_left_x=278)

From (23.5) follows the basic relation connecting the $N_{t}$ with the $S_{n}$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left[N_{t} \geq n\right]=\left[S_{n} \leq t\right] \tag{23.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

From this follows

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left[N_{t}=n\right]=\left[S_{n} \leq t<S_{n+1}\right] . \tag{23.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

Each $N_{t}$ is thus a random variable.
The collection $\left[N_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ is a stochastic process, that is, a collection of random variables indexed by a parameter regarded as time. Condition $0^{\circ}$ can be restated in terms of this process:

Condition $0^{\circ}$. For each $\omega, N_{t}(\omega)$ is a nonnegative integer for $t \geq 0, N_{0}(\omega)=0$, and $\lim _{t \rightarrow \infty} N_{t}(\omega)=\infty$; further, for each $\omega, N_{t}(\omega)$ as a function of $t$ is nondecreasing and right-continuous, and at the points of discontinuity the saltus $N_{t}(\omega)-\sup _{s<t} N_{s}(\omega)$ is exactly 1 .

It is easy to see that (23.3) and (23.4) and the definition (23.5) give random variables $N_{t}$ having these properties. On the other hand, if the stochastic
process $\left[N_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ is given and does have these properties, and if random variables are defined by $S_{n}(\omega)=\inf \left[t: N_{t}(\omega) \geq n\right]$ and $X_{n}(\omega)=S_{n}(\omega)- S_{n-1}(\omega)$, then (23.3) and (23.4) hold, and the definition (23.5) gives back the original $N_{r}$. Therefore, anything that can be said about the $X_{n}$ can be stated in terms of the $N_{t}$, and conversely. The points $S_{1}(\omega), S_{2}(\omega), \ldots$ of $(0, \infty)$ are exactly the discontinuities of $N_{t}(\omega)$ as a function of $t$; because of the queueing example, it is natural to call them arrival times.

The program is to study the joint distributions of the $N_{t}$ under conditions on the waiting times $X_{n}$ and vice versa. The most common model specifies the independence of the waiting times and the absence of aftereffect:

Condition $1^{\circ}$. The $X_{n}$ are independent, and each is exponentially distributed with parameter $\alpha$.

In this case $P\left[X_{n}>0\right]=1$ for each $n$ and $n^{-1} S_{n} \rightarrow \alpha^{-1}$ by the strong law of large numbers (Theorem 22.1), and so (23.3) and (23.4) hold with probability 1 ; to assume they hold everywhere (Condition $0^{\circ}$ ) is simply a convenient normalization.

Under Condition $1^{\circ}, S_{n}$ has the distribution function specified by (20.40), so that $P\left[N_{t} \geq n\right]=\sum_{i=n}^{\infty} e^{-\alpha t}(\alpha t)^{i} / i!$ by (23.6), and

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[N_{t}=n\right]=e^{-\alpha t} \frac{(\alpha t)^{n}}{n!}, \quad n=0,1, \ldots \tag{23.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

Thus $N_{t}$ has the Poisson distribution with mean $\alpha t$. More will be proved in a moment.

Condition $2^{\circ}$. (i) For $0<t_{1}<\cdots<t_{k}$ the increments $N_{t_{1}}, N_{t_{2}}-N_{t_{1}}, \ldots, N_{t_{k}} -N_{t_{k-1}}$ are independent.
(ii) The individual increments have the Poisson distribution:

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[N_{t}-N_{s}=n\right]=e^{-\alpha(t-s)} \frac{(\alpha(t-s))^{n}}{n!}, \quad n=0,1, \ldots, \quad 0 \leq s<t . \tag{23.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $N_{0}=0$, (23.8) is a special case of (23.9). A collection $\left[N_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ of random variables satisfying Condition $2^{\circ}$ is called a Poisson process, and $\alpha$ is the rate of the process. As the increments are independent by (i), if $r<s<t$, then the distributions of $N_{s}-N_{r}$ and $N_{t}-N_{s}$ must convolve to that of $N_{t}-N_{r}$. But the requirement is consistent with (ii) because Poisson distributions with parameters $u$ and $v$ convolve to a Poisson distribution with parameter $u+v$.

Theorem 23.1. Conditions $1^{\circ}$ and $2^{\circ}$ are equivalent in the presence of Condition $0^{\circ}$.

Proof of $1^{\circ} \rightarrow 2^{\circ}$. Fix $t$, and consider the events that happen after time $t$. By (23.5), $S_{N_{t}} \leq t<S_{N_{t}+1}$, and the waiting time from $t$ to the first event following $t$ is $S_{N_{t}+1}-t$; the waiting time between the first and second events following $t$ is $X_{N_{t}+2}$; and so on. Thus

$$
\begin{equation*}
X_{1}^{(t)}=S_{N_{t}+1}-t, \quad X_{2}^{(t)}=X_{N_{t}+2}, \quad X_{3}^{(t)}=X_{N_{t}+3}, \ldots \tag{23.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

define the waiting times following $t$. By (23.6), $N_{t+s}-N_{t} \geq m$, or $N_{t+s} \geq N_{t}+ m$, if and only if $S_{N_{f}+m} \leq t+s$, which is the same thing as $X_{1}^{(t)}+\cdots+X_{m}^{(t)} \leq s$. Thus

$$
\begin{equation*}
N_{t+s}-N_{t}=\max \left[m: X_{1}^{(t)}+\cdots+X_{m}^{(t)} \leq s\right] . \tag{23.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

Hence $\left[N_{t+s}-N_{t}=m\right]=\left[X_{1}^{(t)}+\cdots+X_{m}^{(t)} \leq s<X_{1}^{(t)}+\cdots+X_{m+1}^{(t)}\right]$. A comparison of (23.11) and (23.5) shows that for fixed $t$ the random variables $N_{t+s}-N_{t}$ for $s \geq 0$ are defined in terms of the sequence (23.10) in exactly the same way as the $N_{s}$ are defined in terms of the original sequence of waiting times.

The idea now is to show that conditionally on the event $\left[N_{t}=n\right]$ the random variables (23.10) are independent and exponentially distributed. Because of the independence of the $X_{k}$ and the basic property (23.2) of the exponential distribution, this seems intuitively clear. For a proof, apply (20.30). Suppose $y \geq 0$; if $G_{n}$ is the distribution function of $S_{n}$, then since $X_{n+1}$ has the exponential distribution,

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left[S_{n} \leq t<S_{n+1}, S_{n+1}-t>y\right] & =P\left[S_{n} \leq t, X_{n+1}>t+y-S_{n}\right] \\
& =\int_{x \leq t} P\left[X_{n+1}>t+y-x\right] d G_{n}(x) \\
& =e^{-\alpha y} \int_{x \leq t} P\left[X_{n+1}>t-x\right] d G_{n}(x) \\
& =e^{-\alpha y} P\left[S_{n} \leq t, X_{n+1}>t-S_{n}\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

By the assumed independence of the $X_{n}$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
& P\left[S_{n+1}-t>y_{1}, X_{n+2}>y_{2}, \ldots, X_{n+j}>y_{j}, S_{n} \leq t<S_{n+1}\right] \\
& \quad=P\left[S_{n+1}-t>y_{1}, S_{n} \leq t<S_{n+1}\right] e^{-\alpha y_{2} \cdots} e^{-\alpha y_{j}} \\
& \quad=P\left[S_{n} \leq t<S_{n+1}\right] e^{-\alpha y_{1} \cdots e^{-\alpha y_{j}}}
\end{aligned}
$$

If $H=\left(y_{1}, \infty\right) \times \cdots \times\left(y_{j}, \infty\right)$, this is

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[N_{t}=n,\left(X_{1}^{(t)}, \ldots, X_{j}^{(t)}\right) \in H\right]=P\left[N_{t}=n\right] P\left[\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{j}\right) \in H\right] . \tag{23.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

By Theorem 10.4, the equation extends from $H$ of the special form above to all $H$ in $\mathscr{R}^{j}$.

Now the event $\left[N_{s_{i}}=m_{i}, 1 \leq i \leq u\right]$ can be put in the form $\left[\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{j}\right) \in\right. H]$, where $j=m_{u}+1$ and $H$ is the set of $x$ in $R^{j}$ for which $x_{1}+\cdots+x_{m_{i}} \leq s_{i}<x_{1}+\cdots+x_{m_{i}+1}, 1 \leq i \leq u$. But then $\left[\left(X_{1}^{(t)}, \ldots, X_{j}^{(t)}\right) \in H\right]$ is by (23.11) the same as the event $\left[N_{t+s_{i}}-N_{t}=m_{i}, 1 \leq i \leq u\right]$. Thus (23.12) gives

$$
P\left[N_{t}=n, N_{t+s_{i}}-N_{t}=m_{i}, 1 \leq i \leq u\right]=P\left[N_{t}=n\right] P\left[N_{s_{i}}=m_{i}, 1 \leq i \leq u\right] .
$$

From this it follows by induction on $k$ that if $0=t_{0}<t_{1}<\cdots<t_{k}$, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[N_{t_{i}}-N_{t_{i-1}}=n_{i}, 1 \leq i \leq k\right]=\prod_{i=1}^{k} P\left[N_{t_{i}-t_{i-1}}=n_{i}\right] . \tag{23.13}
\end{equation*}
$$

Thus Condition $1^{\circ}$ implies (23.13) and, as already seen, (23.8). But from (23.13) and (23.8) follow the two parts of Condition $2^{\circ}$. $\square$

Proof of $2^{\circ} \rightarrow 1^{\circ}$. If $2^{\circ}$ holds, then by (23.6), $P\left[X_{1}>t\right]=P\left[N_{t}=0\right]= e^{-\alpha t}$, so that $X_{1}$ is exponentially distributed. To find the joint distribution of $X_{1}$ and $X_{2}$, suppose that $0 \leq s_{1}<t_{1}<s_{2}<t_{2}$ and perform the calculation

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left[s_{1}\right. & \left.<S_{1} \leq t_{1}, s_{2}<S_{2}<t_{2}\right] \\
& =P\left[N_{s_{1}}=0, N_{t_{1}}-N_{s_{1}}=1, N_{s_{2}}-N_{t_{1}}=0, N_{t_{2}}-N_{s_{s}} \geq 1\right] \\
& =e^{-\alpha s_{1}} \times \alpha\left(t_{1}-s_{1}\right) e^{-\alpha\left(t_{1}-s_{1}\right)} \times e^{-\alpha\left(s_{2}-t_{1}\right)} \times\left(1-e^{-\alpha\left(t_{2}-s_{2}\right)}\right) \\
& =\alpha\left(t_{1}-s_{1}\right)\left(e^{-\alpha s_{2}}-e^{-\alpha t_{2}}\right)=\iint_{\substack{s_{1}<y_{1} \leq t_{1} \\
s_{2}<y_{2} \leq t_{2}}} \alpha^{2} e^{-\alpha y_{2}} d y_{1} d y_{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus for a rectangle $A$ contained in the open set $G=\left[\left(y_{1}, y_{2}\right): 0<y_{1}<y_{2}\right]$.

$$
P\left[\left(S_{1}, S_{2}\right) \in A\right]=\int_{A} \alpha^{2} e^{-\alpha y_{2}} d y_{1} d y_{2} .
$$

By inclusion-exclusion, this holds for finite unions of such rectangles and hence, by a passage to the limit, for countable ones. Therefore, it holds for $A=G \cap G^{\prime}$ if $G^{\prime}$ is open. Since the open sets form a $\pi$-system generating the Borel sets, ( $S_{1}, S_{2}$ ) has density $\alpha^{2} e^{-\alpha y_{2}}$ on $G$ (of course, the density is ${ }^{4} 0$ outside $G$ ).

By a similar argument in $R^{k}$ (the notation only is more complicated), ( $S_{1}, \ldots, S_{k}$ ) has density $\alpha^{k} e^{-\alpha y_{k}}$ on $\left[y: 0<y_{1}<\cdots<y_{k}\right]$. If a linear transformation $g(y)=x$ is defined by $x_{i}=y_{i}-y_{i-1}$, then $\left(X_{1}, \ldots ; X_{k}\right)= g\left(S_{1}, \ldots, S_{k}\right)$ has by (20.20) the density $\prod_{i=1}^{k} \alpha e^{-\alpha x_{i}}$ (the Jacobian is identically 1 ). This proves Condition $1^{\circ}$. $\square$

## The Poisson Approximation

Other characterizations of the Poisson process depend on a generalization of the classical Poisson approximation to the binomial distribution.

Theorem 23.2. Suppose that for each $n, Z_{n 1}, \ldots, Z_{n r_{n}}$ are independent random variables and $Z_{n k}$ assumes the values 1 and 0 with probabilities $p_{n k}$ and $1-p_{n k}$. If

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum_{k=1}^{r_{n}} p_{n k} \rightarrow \lambda \geq 0, \quad \max _{1 \leq k \leq r_{n}} p_{n k} \rightarrow 0 \tag{23.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

then

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\sum_{k=1}^{r_{n}} Z_{n k}=i\right] \rightarrow e^{-\lambda} \frac{\lambda^{i}}{i!}, \quad i=0,1,2, \ldots \tag{23.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $\lambda=0$, the limit in (23.15) is interpreted as 1 for $i=0$ and 0 for $i \geq 1$. In the case where $r_{n}=n$ and $p_{n k}=\lambda / n$, (23.15) is the Poisson approximation to the binomial. Note that if $\lambda>0$, then (23.14) implies $r_{n} \rightarrow \infty$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/6289d1e6-4ff9-4bb6-b3da-068bafdb2b1b-074.jpg?height=130&width=1251&top_left_y=1561&top_left_x=290)

Proof. The argument depends on a construction like that in the proof of Theorem 20.4. Let $U_{1}, U_{2}, \ldots$ be independent random variables, each uniformly distributed over $[0,1)$. For each $p, 0 \leq p \leq 1$, split $[0,1)$ into the two intervals $I_{0}(p)=[0,1-p)$ and $I_{1}(p)=[1-p, 1)$, as well as into the sequence of intervals $J_{i}(p)=\left[\sum_{j=1}^{i-1} e^{-\rho} p^{j} / j!, \sum_{j=1}^{i} e^{-\rho} p^{j} / j!\right), i=0,1, \ldots$ Define $V_{n k}=1$ if $U_{k} \in I_{1}\left(p_{n k}\right)$ and $V_{n k}=0$ if $U_{k} \in I_{0}\left(p_{n k}\right)$. Then $V_{n 1}, \ldots, V_{n r_{n}}$ are independent, and $V_{n k}$ assumes the values 1 and 0 with probabilities $P\left[U_{k} \in I_{1}\left(p_{n k}\right)\right]= p_{n k}$ and $P\left[U_{k} \in I_{0}\left(p_{N k}\right)\right]=1-p_{n k}$. Since $V_{n 1}, \ldots, V_{n r_{n}}$ have the same joint distribution as $Z_{n 1}, \ldots, Z_{n r_{n}}$, (23.15) will follow if it is shown that $V_{n}=\sum_{k=1}^{r_{n}} V_{n k}$ satisfies

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[V_{n}=i\right] \rightarrow e^{-\lambda} \frac{\lambda^{i}}{i!} \tag{23.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

Now define $W_{n k}=i$ if $U_{k} \in J_{i}\left(p_{n k}\right), i=0,1, \ldots$. Then $P\left[W_{n k}=i\right]= e^{-p_{n k}} p_{n k}^{i} / i!-W_{n k}$ has the Poisson distribution with mean $p_{n k}$. Since the $W_{n k}$
are independent, $W_{n}=\sum_{k=1}^{r_{n}} W_{n \dot{k}}$ has the Poisson distribution with mean $\lambda_{n}=\sum_{k=1}^{r_{n}} p_{n k}$. Since $1-p \leq e^{-\rho}, J_{1}(p) \subset I_{1}(p)$ (see the diagram). Therefore,

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left[V_{n k} \neq W_{n k}\right] & =P\left[V_{n k}=1 \neq W_{n k}\right]=P\left[U_{k} \in I_{1}\left(p_{n k}\right)-J_{1}\left(p_{n k}\right)\right] \\
& =p_{n k}-e^{-\rho_{n k}} p_{n k} \leq p_{n k}^{2},
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
P\left[V_{n} \neq W_{n}\right] \leq \sum_{k=1}^{r_{n}} p_{n k}^{2} \leq \lambda_{n} \max _{1 \leq k \leq r_{n}} p_{n k} \rightarrow 0
$$

by (23.14). And now (23.16) and (23.15) follow because

$$
P\left[W_{n}=i\right]=e^{-\lambda_{n}} \lambda_{n}^{i} / i!\rightarrow e^{-\lambda} \lambda^{i} / i!
$$ $\square$

## Other Characterizations of the Poisson Process

The condition (23.2) is an interesting characterization of the exponential distribution because it is essentially qualitative. There are qualitative characterizations of the Poisson process as well.

For each $\omega$, the function $N_{t}(\omega)$ has a discontinuity at $t$ if and only if $S_{n}(\omega)=t$ for some $n \geq 1 ; t$ is a fixed discontinuity if the probability of this is positive. The condition that there be no fixed discontinuities is therefore

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[S_{n}=t\right]=0, \quad t \geq 0, \quad n \geq 1 ; \tag{23.17}
\end{equation*}
$$

that is, each of $S_{1}, S_{2}, \ldots$ has a continuous distribution function. Of course there is probability 1 (under Condition $0^{\circ}$ ) that $N_{t}(\omega)$ has a discontinuity somewhere (and indeed has infinitely many of them). But (23.17) ensures that a $t$ specified in advance has probability 0 of being a discontinuity, or time of an arrival. The Poisson process satisfies this natural condition.

Theorem 23.3. If Condition $0^{\circ}$ holds and $\left[N_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ has independent increments and no fixed discontinuities, then each increment has a Poisson distribution.

This is Prékopà's theorem. The conclusion is not that $\left[N_{r}: t \geq 0\right]$ is a Poisson process, because the mean of $N_{t}-N_{s}$ need not be proportional to $t-s$. If $\varphi$ is an arbitrary nondecreasing, continuous function on $[0, \infty)$ and $\varphi(0)=0$, and if $\left[N_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ is a Poisson process, then $N_{\varphi(t)}$ satisfies the conditions of the theorem. ${ }^{\dagger}$

Proof. The problem is to show for $t^{\prime}<t^{\prime \prime}$ that $N_{t^{\prime \prime}}-N_{t^{\prime}}$ has for some $\lambda \geq 0$ a Poisson distribution with mean $\lambda$, a unit mass at 0 being regarded as a Poisson distribution with mean 0 .

[^9]The procedure is to construct a sequence of partitions

$$
\begin{equation*}
t^{\prime}=t_{n 0}<t_{n 1}<\cdots<t_{n r_{n}}=t^{\prime \prime} \tag{23.18}
\end{equation*}
$$

of $\left[t^{\prime}, t^{\prime \prime}\right]$ with three properties. First, each decomposition refines the preceding one: each $t_{n k}$ is a $t_{n+1, j}$. Second,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum_{k=1}^{r_{n}} P\left[N_{t_{n k}}-N_{t_{n, k-1}} \geq 1\right] \uparrow \lambda \tag{23.19}
\end{equation*}
$$

for some finite $\lambda$ and

$$
\begin{equation*}
\max _{1 \leq k \leq r_{n}} P\left[N_{t_{n k}}-N_{t_{n, k-1}} \geq 1\right] \rightarrow 0 \tag{23.20}
\end{equation*}
$$

Third,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\max _{1 \leq k \leq r_{n}}\left(N_{t_{n k}}-N_{t_{n k-1}}\right) \geq 2\right] \rightarrow 0 . \tag{23.21}
\end{equation*}
$$

Once the partitions have been constructed, the rest of the proof is easy: Let $Z_{n k}$ be 1 or 0 according as $N_{t_{n k}}-N_{t_{n, k-1}}$ is positive or not. Since [ $N_{t}$ : $t \geq 0$ ] has independent increments, the $Z_{n k}$ are independent for each $n$. By Theorem 23.2, therefore, (23.19) and (23.20) imply that $Z_{n}=\sum_{k=1}^{r_{n}} Z_{n k}$ satisfies $P\left[Z_{n}=i\right] \rightarrow e^{-\lambda} \lambda^{i} / i!$ Now $N_{t^{\prime \prime}}-N_{t^{\prime}} \geq Z_{n}$, and there is strict inequality if and only if $N_{t_{n k}}-N_{t_{n, k-1}} \geq 2$ for some $k$. Thus (23.21) implies $P\left[N_{t^{\prime \prime}}-N_{t^{\prime}} \neq\right. \left.Z_{n}\right] \rightarrow 0$, and therefore $P\left[N_{t^{\prime \prime}}-N_{t^{\prime}}=i\right]=e^{-\lambda} \lambda^{i} / i!$

To construct the partitions, consider for each $t$ the distance $D_{t}=\inf _{m \geq 1} \left|t-S_{m}\right|$ from $t$ to the nearest arrival time. Since $S_{m} \rightarrow \infty$, the infimum is achieved. Further, $D_{t}=0$ if and only if $S_{m}=t$ for some $m$, and since by hypothesis there are no fixed discontinuities, the probability of this is 0 : $P\left[D_{t}=0\right]=0$. Choose $\delta_{t}$ so that $0<\delta_{t}<n^{-1}$ and $P\left[D_{t} \leq \delta_{t}\right]<n^{-1}$. The intervals $\left(t-\hat{o}_{t}, t+\delta_{t}\right)$ for $t^{\prime} \leq t \leq t^{\prime \prime}$ cover $\left[t^{\prime}, t^{\prime \prime}\right]$. Choose a finite subcover, and in (23.18) take the $t_{n k}$ for $0<k<r_{n}$ to be the endpoints (of intervals in the subcover) that are contained in ( $t^{\prime}, t^{\prime \prime}$ ). By the construction,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\max _{1 \leq k \leq r_{n}}\left(t_{n k}-t_{n, k-1}\right) \rightarrow 0, \tag{23.22}
\end{equation*}
$$

and the probability that $\left(t_{n, k-1}, t_{n k}\right]$ contains some $S_{m}$ is less than $n^{-1}$. This gives a sequence of partitions satisfying (23.20). Inserting more points in a partition cannot increase the maxima in (23.20) and (23.22), and so it can be arranged that each partition refines the preceding one.

To prove (23.21) it is enough (Theorem 4.1) to show that the limit superior of the sets involved has probability 0 . It is in fact empty: If for infinitely many $n, N_{t_{n k}}(\omega)-N_{t_{n, k-1}}(\omega) \geq 2$ holds for some $k \leq r_{n}$, then by (23.22), $N_{t}(\omega)$ as a
function of $t$ has in $\left[t^{\prime}, t^{\prime \prime}\right]$ discontinuity points (arrival times) arbitrarily close together, which requires $S_{m}(\omega) \in\left[t^{\prime}, t^{\prime \prime}\right]$ for infinitely many $m$, in violation of Condition $0^{\circ}$.

It remains to prove (23.19). If $Z_{n k}$ and $Z_{n}$ are defined as above and $p_{n k}=P\left[Z_{n k}=1\right]$, then the sum in (23.19) is $\Sigma_{k} p_{n k}=E\left[Z_{n}\right]$. Since $Z_{n+1} \geq Z_{n}$, $\sum_{k} p_{n k}$ is nondecreasing in $n$. Now

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left[N_{t^{\prime \prime}}-N_{t^{\prime}}=0\right] & =P\left[Z_{n k}=0, k \leq r_{n}\right] \\
& =\prod_{k=1}^{r_{n}}\left(1-p_{n k}\right) \leq \exp \left[-\sum_{k=1}^{r_{n}} p_{n k}\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

If the left-hand side here is positive, this puts an upper bound on $\sum_{k} p_{r k}$, and (23.19) follows. But suppose $P\left[N_{t^{\prime \prime}}-N_{t^{\prime}}=0\right]=0$. If $s$ is the midpoint of $t^{\prime}$ and $t^{\prime \prime}$, then since the increments are independent, one of $P\left[N_{s}-N_{t^{\prime}}=0\right]$ and $P\left[N_{t^{\prime \prime}}-N_{s}=0\right]$ must vanish. It is therefore possible to find a nested sequence of intervals $\left[u_{m}, v_{n}\right]$ such that $v_{m}-u_{m} \rightarrow 0$ and the event $A_{m}= \left[N_{t_{m}}-N_{u_{m}} \geq 1\right]$ has probability 1 . But then $P\left(\cap_{m} A_{m}\right)=1$, and if $t$ is the point common to the $\left[u_{m}, v_{m}\right]$, there is an arrival at $t$ with probability 1 , contrary to the assumption that there are no fixed discontinuities. $\square$

Theorem 23.3 in some cases makes the Poisson model quite plausible. The increments will be essentially independent if the arrivals to time $s$ cannot seriously deplete the population of potential arrivals, so that $N_{s}$ has for $t>s$ negligible effect on $N_{t}-N_{s}$. And the condition that there are no fixed discontinuities is entirely natural. These conditions hold for arrivals of calls at a telephone exchange if the rate of calls is small in comparison with the population of subscribers and calls are not placed at fixed, predetermined times. If the arrival rate is essentially constant, this leads to the following condition.

Condition $3^{\circ}$. (i) For $0<t_{1}<\cdots<t_{k}$ the increments $N_{t_{1}}, N_{t_{2}}-N_{t_{1}}, \ldots$, $N_{t_{k}}-N_{t_{k-1}}$ are independent.
(ii) The distribution of $N_{t}-N_{s}$ depends only on the difference $t-s$.

Theorem 23.4. Conditions $1^{\circ}, 2^{\circ}$, and $3^{\circ}$ are equivalent in the presence of Condition $0^{\circ}$.

Proof. Obviously Condition $2^{\circ}$ implies $3^{\circ}$. Suppose that Condition $3^{\circ}$ holds. If $J_{t}$ is the saltus at $t\left(J_{t}=N_{t}-\sup _{s<t} N_{s}\right)$, then $\left[N_{t}-N_{t-n^{-1}} \geq 1\right] \downarrow \left[J_{t} \geq 1\right]$, and it follows by (ii) of Condition $3^{\circ}$ that $P\left[J_{t} \geq 1\right]$ is the same for all $t$. But if the value common to $P\left[J_{t} \geq 1\right]$ is positive, then by the independence of the increments and the second Borel-Cantelli lemma there is probability 1 that $J_{t} \geq 1$ for infinitely many rational $t$ in ( 0,1 ), for example, which contradicts Condition $0^{\circ}$.

By Theorem 23.3, then, the increments have Poisson distributions. If $f(t)$ is the mean of $N_{t}$, then $N_{t}-N_{s}$ for $s<t$ must have mean $f(t)-f(s)$ and must by (ii) have mean $f(t-s)$; thus $f(t)=f(s)+f(t-s)$. Therefore, $f$ satisfies Cauchy's functional equation [A20] and, being nondecreasing, must have the form $f(t)=\alpha t$ for $\alpha \geq 0$. Condition $0^{\circ}$ makes $\alpha=0$ impossible.

One standard way of deriving the Poisson process is by differential equations.

Condition $4^{\circ}$. If $0<t_{1}<\cdots<t_{k}$ and if $n_{1}, \ldots, n_{k}$ are nonnegative integers, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[N_{t_{k}+h}-N_{t_{k}}=1 \mid N_{t_{j}}=n_{j}, j \leq k\right]=\alpha h+o(h) \tag{23.23}
\end{equation*}
$$

and

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[N_{t_{k}+h}-N_{t_{k}} \geq 2 \mid N_{t_{j}}=n_{j}, j \leq k\right]=o(h) \tag{23.24}
\end{equation*}
$$

as $h \downarrow 0$. Moreover, $\left[N_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ has no fixed discontinuities.
The occurrences of $o(h)$ in (23.23) and (23.24) denote functions, say $\phi_{1}(h)$, and $\phi_{2}(h)$, such that $h^{-1} \phi_{i}(h) \rightarrow 0$ as $h \downarrow 0$; the $\phi_{i}$ may depend a priori on $k, t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$, and $n_{1}, \ldots, n_{k}$ as well as on $h$. It is assumed in (23.23) and (23.24) that the conditioning events have positive probability, so that the conditional probabilities are well defined.

Theorem 23.5. Conditions $1^{\circ}$ through $4^{\circ}$ are all equivalent in the presence of Condition $0^{\circ}$.

Proof of $2^{\circ} \rightarrow 4^{\circ}$. For a Poisson process with rate $\alpha$, the left-hand sides of (23.23) and (23.24) are $e^{-\alpha h} \alpha h$ and $1-e^{-\alpha h}-e^{-\alpha h} \alpha h$, and these are $\alpha h+o(h)$ and $o(h)$, respectively, because $e^{-\alpha h}=1-\alpha h+o(h)$. And by the argument in the preceding proof, the process has no fixed discontinuities.

Proof of $4^{\circ} \rightarrow 2^{\circ}$. Fix $k$, the $t_{j}$, and the $n_{j}$; denote by $A$ the event $\left[N_{t_{j}}=n_{j}, j \leq k\right]$; and for $t \geq 0$ put $p_{n}(t)=P\left[N_{t_{k}+t}-N_{t_{k}}=n \mid A\right]$. It will be shown that

$$
\begin{equation*}
p_{n}(t)=e^{-\alpha t} \frac{(\alpha t)^{n}}{n!}, \quad n=0,1, \ldots \tag{23.25}
\end{equation*}
$$

This will also be proved for the case in which $p_{n}(t)=P\left[N_{t}=n\right]$. Condition $2^{\circ}$ will then follow by induction.

If $t>0$ and $|t-s|<n^{-1}$, then

$$
\left|P\left[N_{t}=n\right]-P\left[N_{s}=n\right]\right| \leq P\left[N_{t} \neq N_{s}\right] \leq P\left[N_{t+n^{-1}}-N_{t-n^{-1}} \geq 1\right] .
$$

As $n \rightarrow \infty$, the right side here decreases to the probability of a discontinuity at $t$, which is 0 by hypothesis. Thus $P\left[N_{t}=n\right]$ is continuous at $t$. The same kind of argument works for conditional probabilities and for $t=0$, and so $p_{n}(t)$ is continuous for $t \geq 0$.

To simplify the notation, put $D_{t}=N_{t_{k}+t}-N_{t_{k}}$. If $D_{t+h}=n$, then $D_{t}=m$ for some $m \leq n$. If $t>0$, then by the rules for conditional probabilities,

$$
\begin{aligned}
p_{n}(t+h)= & p_{n}(t) P\left[D_{t+h}-D_{t}=0 \mid A \cap\left[D_{t}=n\right]\right] \\
& +p_{n-1}(t) P\left[D_{t+h}-D_{t}=1 \mid A \cap\left[D_{t}=n-1\right]\right] \\
& +\sum_{m=0}^{n-2} p_{m}(t) P\left[D_{t+h}-D_{t}=n-m \mid A \cap\left[D_{t}=m\right]\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

For $n \leq 1$, the final sum is absent, and for $n=0$, the middle term is absent as well. This holds in the case $p_{n}(t)=P\left[N_{t}=n\right]$ if $D_{t}=N_{t}$ and $A=\Omega$. (If $t=0$, some of the conditioning events here are empty; hence the assumption $t>0$.) By (23.24), the final sum is $o(h)$ for each fixed $n$. Applying (23.23) and (23.24) now leads to

$$
p_{n}(t+h)=p_{n}(t)(1-\alpha h)+p_{n-1}(t) \alpha h+o(h)
$$

and letting $h \downarrow 0$ gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
p_{n}^{\prime}(t)=-\alpha p_{n}(t)+\alpha p_{n-1}(t) \tag{23.26}
\end{equation*}
$$

In the case $n=0$, take $p_{-1}(t)$ to be identically 0 . In (23.26), $t>0$ and $p_{n}^{\prime}(t)$ is a right-hand derivative. But since $p_{n}(t)$ and the right side of the equation are continuous on $[0, \infty)$, (23.26) holds also for $t=0$ and $p_{n}^{\prime}(t)$ can be taken as a two-sided derivative for $t>0$ [A22].

Now (23.26) gives [A23]

$$
p_{n}(t)=e^{-\alpha t}\left[p_{n}(0)+\alpha \int_{0}^{t} p_{n-1}(s) e^{\alpha s} d s\right]
$$

Since $p_{n}(0)$ is 1 or 0 as $n=0$ or $n>0$, (23.25) follows by induction on $n$.

## Stochastic Processes

The Poisson process $\left[N_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ is one example of a stochastic process-that is, a collection of random variables (on some probability space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ )) indexed by a parameter regarded as representing time. In the Poisson case, time is continuous. In some cases the time is discrete: Section 7 concerns the sequence $\left\{F_{n}\right\}$ of a gambler's fortunes; there $n$ represents time, but time that increases in jumps.

Part of the structure of a stochastic process is specified by its finite-dimensional distributions. For any finite sequence $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ of time points, the $k$-dimensional random vector ( $N_{t_{1}}, \ldots, N_{t_{k}}$ ) has a distribution $\mu_{t_{1}} r_{k}$ over $R^{k}$. These measures $\mu_{t_{1}} t_{k}$ are the finite-dimensional distributions of the process. Condition $2^{\circ}$ of this section in effect specifies them for the Poisson case:

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[N_{t_{j}}=n_{j}, j \leq k\right]=\prod_{j=1}^{k} e^{-\alpha\left(t_{j}-t_{j-1}\right)} \frac{\left(\alpha\left(t_{j}-t_{j-1}\right)\right)^{n_{j}-n_{j-1}}}{\left(n_{j}-n_{j-1}\right)!} \tag{23.27}
\end{equation*}
$$

if $0 \leq n_{1} \leq \cdots \leq n_{k}$ and $0 \leq t_{1}<\cdots<t_{k}\left(\right.$ take $\left.n_{0}=t_{0}=0\right)$.
The finite-dimensional distributions do not, however, contain all the mathematically interesting information about the process in the case of continuous time. Because of (23.3), (23.4), and the definition (23.5), for each fixed $\omega, N_{t}(\omega)$ as a function of $t$ has the regularity properties given in the second version of Condition $0^{\circ}$. These properties are used in an essential way in the proofs.

Suppose that $f(t)$ is $t$ or 0 according as $t$ is rational or irrational. Let $N_{t}$ be defined as before, and let

$$
\begin{equation*}
M_{t}(\omega)=N_{t}(\omega)+f\left(t+X_{1}(\omega)\right) . \tag{23.28}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $R$ is the set of rationals, then $P\left[\omega: f\left(t+X_{1}(\omega)\right) \neq 0\right]=P\left[\omega: X_{1}(\omega) \in\right. R-t]=0$ for each $t$ because $R-t$ is countable and $X_{1}$ has a density. Thus $P\left[M_{t}=N_{t}\right]=1$ for each $t$, and so the stochastic process $\left[M_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ has the same finite-dimensional distributions as $\left[N_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$. For $\omega$ fixed, however, $M_{t}(\omega)$ as a function of $t$ is everywhere discontinuous and is neither monotone nor exclusively integer-valued.

The functions obtained by fixing $\omega$ and letting $t$ vary are called the path functions or sample paths of the process. The example above shows that the finite-dimensional distributions do not suffice to determine the character of the path functions. In specifying a stochastic process as a model for some phenomenon, it is natural to place conditions on the character of the sample paths as well as on the finite-dimensional distributions. Condition $0^{\circ}$ was imposed throughout this section to ensure that the sample paths are nondecreasing, right-continuous, integer-valued step functions, a natural condition if $N_{t}$ is to represent the number of events in $[0, t]$. Stochastic processes in continuous time are studied further in Chapter 7.

## PROBLEMS

Assume the Poisson processes here satisfy Condition $0^{\circ}$ as well as Condition $1^{\circ}$.
23.1. Show that the minimum of independent exponential waiting times is again exponential and that the parameters add.
23.2. 20.17 Show that the time $S_{n}$ of the $n$th event in a Poisson stream has the gamma density $f(x ; \alpha, n)$ as defined by (20.47). This is sometimes called the Erlang density.
23.3. Let $A_{t}=t-S_{N_{1}}$ be the time back to the most recent event in the Poisson stream (or to 0 ), and let $B_{t}=S_{N_{t}+1}-t$ be the time forward to the next event. Show that $A_{t}$ and $B_{t}$ are independent, that $B_{t}$ is distributed as $X_{1}$ (exponentially with parameter $\alpha$ ), and that $A_{t}$ is distributed as $\min \left\{X_{1}, t\right\}: P\left[A_{t} \leq i\right]$ is $0,1-e^{-\alpha x}$, or 1 as $x<0,0 \leq x<t$, or $x \geq t$.
23.4. $\uparrow$ Let $L_{t}=A_{t}+B_{t}=S_{N_{t}+1}-S_{N_{t}}$ be the length of the interarrival interval covering $t$,
(a) Show that $L_{t}$ has density

$$
d_{t}(x)= \begin{cases}\alpha^{2} x e^{-\alpha x} & \text { if } 0<x<t, \\ \alpha(1+\alpha t) e^{-\alpha x} & \text { if } x \geq t .\end{cases}
$$

(b) Show that $E\left[L_{t}\right]$ converges to $2 E\left[X_{1}\right]$ as $t \rightarrow \infty$. This seems paradoxical because $L_{t}$ is one of the $X_{n}$. Give an intuitive resolution of the apparent paradox.
23.5. Merging Poisson streams. Define a process $\left\{N_{t}\right\}$ by (23.5) for a sequence $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ of random variables satisfying (23.4). Let ( $X_{n}^{t}$ ) be a second sequence of random variables, on the same probability space, satisfying (23.4), and define $\left\{N_{l}^{\prime}\right\}$ by $N_{t}^{t}=\max \left[n: X_{1}^{\prime}+\cdots+X_{n}^{\prime} \leq t\right]$. Define $\left(N_{t}^{\prime \prime}\right)$ by $N_{t}^{\prime \prime}=N_{t}+N_{t}^{\prime}$. Show that, if $\sigma\left(X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots\right)$ and $\sigma\left(X_{1}^{\prime}, X_{2}^{\prime}, \ldots\right)$ are independent and $\left\{N_{t}\right\}$ and $\left\{N_{t}^{\prime}\right\}$ are Poisson processes with respective rates $\alpha$ and $\beta$, then $\left\{N_{t}^{\prime \prime}\right\}$ is a Poisson process with rate $\alpha+\beta$.
23.6. $\uparrow$ The $n$th and $(n+1)$ st events in the process $\left\{N_{t}\right\}$ occur at times $S_{n}$ and $S_{n+1}$.
(a) Find the distribution of the number $N_{S_{n+1}}^{\prime}-N_{S_{n}}^{\prime}$ of events in the other process during this time interval.
(b) Generalize to $N_{S_{m}}^{r}-N_{S_{i n}}^{r}$.
23.7. Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and exponentially distributed with parameter $\alpha$, so that (23.5) defines a Poisson process $\left\{N_{t}\right\}$. Suppose that $Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and identically distributed and that $\sigma\left(X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots\right)$ and $\sigma\left(Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots\right)$ are independent. Put $Z_{1}=\Sigma_{k \leq N_{t}} Y_{k}$. This is the compound Poisson process. If, for example, the event at time $S_{n}$ in the original process
represents an insurance claim, and if $Y_{n}$ represents the amount of the claim, then $Z_{t}$ represents the total claims to time $t$,
(a) If $Y_{k}=1$ with probability 1 , then $\left\{Z_{t}\right\}$ is an ordinary Poisson process.
(b) Show that $\left(Z_{t}\right)$ has independent increments and that $Z_{s+t}-Z_{s}$ has the same distribution as $Z_{t}$.
(c) Show that, if $Y_{k}$ assumes the values 1 and 0 with probabilities $p$ and $1-p (0<p<1)$, then $\left\{Z_{1}\right\}$ is a Poisson process with rate $p \alpha$.
23.8. Suppose a process satisfies Condition $0^{\circ}$ and has independent, Poisson-distributed increments and no fixed discontinuities. Show that it has the form $\left\{N_{\varphi(1)}\right\}$, where $\left(N_{t}\right)$ is a standard Poisson process and $\varphi$ is a nondecreasing, continuous function on $[0, \infty)$ with $\varphi(0)=0$.
23.9. If the waiting times $X_{n}$ are independent and exponentially distributed with parameter $\alpha$, then $S_{n} / n \rightarrow \alpha^{-1}$ with probability 1 , by the strong law of large numbers. From $\lim _{t \rightarrow \infty} N_{t}=\infty$ and $S_{N_{t}} \leq t<S_{N_{t}+1}$ deduce that $\lim _{t \rightarrow \infty} N_{t} / t= \alpha$ with probability 1 .
23.10. $\uparrow$ (a) Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are positive, and assume directly that $S_{n} / n \rightarrow m$ with probability 1 , as happens if the $X_{n}$ are independent and identically distributed with mean $m$. Show that $\lim , N_{t} / t=1 / m$ with probability 1 .
(b) Suppose now that $S_{n} / n \rightarrow \infty$ with probability 1 , as happens if the $X_{n}$ are independent and identically distributed and have infinite mean. Show that $\lim , N_{t} / t=0$ with probability 1 .

The results in Problem 23.10 are theorems in renewal theory: A component of some mechanism is replaced each time it fails or wears out The $X_{n}$ are the lifetimes of the successive components, and $N_{i}$ is the number of replacements, or renewals, to time $t$.
23.11. 20.723 .10 ↑ Consider a persistent, irreducible Markov chain, and for a fixed state $j$ let $N_{n}$ be the number of passages through $j$ up to time $n$. Show that $N_{n} / n \rightarrow 1 / m$ with probability 1 , where $m=\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} k f_{j j}^{(k)}$ is the mean return time (replace $1 / m$ by 0 if this mean is infinite). See Lemma 3 in Section 8.
23.12. Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ have Poisson distributions with parameters $\alpha$ and $\beta$. Show that $|P[X=i]-P[Y=i]| \leq|\alpha-\beta|$. Hint: Suppose that $\alpha<\beta$, and represent $Y$ as $X+D$, where $X$ and $D$ are independent and have Poisson distributions with parameters $\alpha$ and $\beta-\alpha$.
23.13. ↑ Use the methods in the proof of Theorem 23.2 to show that the error in (23.15) is bounded uniformly in $i$ by $\left|\lambda-\lambda_{n}\right|+\lambda_{n} \max _{k} p_{n k}$.

## SECTION 24. THE ERGODIC THEOREM*

Even though chance necessarily involves the notion of change, the laws governing the change may themselves remain constant as time passes: If time

[^10]does not alter the roulette wheel, the gambler's fortunes fluctuate according to constant probability laws. The ergodic theorem is a version of the strong law of large numbers general enough to apply to any system governed by probability laws that are invariant in time.

## Measure-Preserving Transformations

Let $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ be a probability space. A mapping $T: \Omega \rightarrow \Omega$ is a measure-preserving transformation if it is measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{F}$ and $P\left(T^{-1} A\right)=P(A)$ for all $A$ in $\mathscr{F}$, from which it follows that $P\left(T^{-n} A\right)=P(A)$ for $n \geq 0$. If, further, $T$ is a one-to-one mapping onto $\Omega$ and the point mapping $T^{-1}$ is measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{F}(T A \in \mathscr{F}$ for $A \in \mathscr{F})$, then $T$ is invertible; in this case $T^{-1}$ automatically preserves measure: $P(A)=P\left(T^{-1} T A\right)=P(T A)$.

The first result is a simple consequence of the $\pi-\lambda$ theorem (or Theorems 13.1(i) and 3.3).

Lemma 1. If $\mathscr{P}$ is a $\pi$-system generating $\mathscr{F}$, and if $T^{-1} A \in \mathscr{F}$ and $P\left(T^{-1} A\right)=P(A)$ for $A \in \mathscr{P}$, then $T$ is a measure-preserving transformation.

Example 24.1. The Bernoulli shift. Let $S$ be a finite set, and consider the space $S^{\infty}$ of sequences (2.15) of elements of $S$. Define the shift $T$ by

$$
\begin{equation*}
T \omega=\left(z_{2}(\omega), z_{3}(\omega), \ldots\right) ; \tag{24.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

the first element of $\omega=\left(z_{1}(\omega), z_{2}(\omega), \ldots\right)$ is lost, and $T$ shifts the remaining elements one place to the left: $z_{k}(T \omega)=z_{k+1}(\omega)$ for $k \geq 1$. If $A$ is a cylinder (2.17), then

$$
\begin{align*}
T^{-1} A & =\left[\omega:\left(z_{2}(\omega), \ldots, z_{n+1}(\omega)\right) \in H\right]  \tag{24.2}\\
& =\left[\omega:\left(z_{1}(\omega), \ldots, z_{n+1}(\omega)\right) \in S \times H\right]
\end{align*}
$$

is another cylinder, and since the cylinders generate the basic $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{C}, T$ is measurable $\mathscr{C} / \mathscr{C}$.

For probabilities $p_{u}$ on $S$ (nonnegative and summing to 1 ), define product measure $P$ on the field $\mathscr{C}_{0}$ of cylinders by (2.21). Then $P$ is consistently defined and countably additive (Theorem 2.3) and hence extends to a probability measure on $\mathscr{b}=\sigma\left(\mathscr{C}_{0}\right)$. Since the thin cylinders (2.16) form a $\pi$-system that generates $\mathscr{C}, P$ is completely determined by the probabilities it assigns to them:

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\omega:\left(z_{1}(\omega), \ldots, z_{n}(\omega)\right)=\left(u_{1}, \ldots, u_{n}\right)\right]=p_{u_{1}} \cdots p_{u_{n}} . \tag{24.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $A$ is the thin cylinder on the left here, then by (24.2),

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(T^{-1} A\right)=\sum_{u \in S} p_{u} p_{u_{1}} \cdots p_{u_{n}}=p_{u_{1}} \cdots p_{u_{n}}=P(A) \tag{24.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

and it follows by the lemma that $T$ preserves $P$. This $T$ is the Bernoulli shift.
If $A=\left[\omega: z_{1}(\omega)=u\right](u \in S)$, then $I_{A}\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)$ is 1 or 0 according as $z_{k}(\omega)$ is $\underline{u}$ or not. Since by (24.3) the events [ $\omega: z_{k}(\omega)=u$ ] are independent, each with probability $p_{u}$, the random variables $I_{A}\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)$ are independent and take the value 1 with probability $p_{u}=P(A)$. By the strong law of large numbers, therefore,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} \frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} I_{A}\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)=P(A) \tag{24.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

with probability 1. $\square$

Example 24.1 gives a model for independent trials, and that $T$ preserves $P$ means the probability laws governing the trials are invariant in time. In the present section, it is this invariance of the probability laws that plays the fundamental role; independence is a side issue.

The orbit under $T$ of the point $\omega$ is the sequence ( $\omega, T \omega, T^{2} \omega, \ldots$ ), and (24.5) can be expressed by saying that the orbit enters the set $A$ with asymptotic relative frequency $P(A)$. For $A=\left[\omega:\left(z_{1}(\omega), z_{2}(\omega)\right)=\left(u_{1}, u_{2}\right)\right]$, the $I_{A}\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)$ are not independent, but (24.5) holds anyway. In fact, for the Bernoulli shift, (24.5) holds with probability 1 whatever $A$ may be ( $A \in \mathscr{F}$ ). This is one of the consequences of the ergodic theorem (Theorem 24.1). What is more, according to this theorem the limit in (24.5) exists with probability 1 (although it may not be constant in $\omega$ ) if $T$ is an arbitrary measure-preserving transformation on an arbitrary probability space, of which there are many examples.

Example 24.2. The Markov shift. Let $P=\left[p_{i j}\right]$ be a stochastic matrix with rows and columns indexed by the finite set $S$, and let $\pi_{i}$ be probabilities on $S$. Replace (2.21) by $P(A)=\sum_{H} \pi_{u_{1}} p_{u_{1} u_{2}} \cdots p_{u_{n-1} u_{n}}$. The argument in Section 2 showing that product measure is consistently defined and finitely additive carries over to this new measure: since the rows of the transition matrix add to 1 ,

$$
\sum_{u_{n+1}} p_{u_{m}} p_{u_{n} u_{n+1}} \cdots p_{u_{m-1} u_{m}}=1,
$$

and so the argument involving (2.23) goes through. The new measure is again
countably additive on $\mathscr{C}_{0}$ (Theorem 2.3) and so extends to $\mathscr{C}$. This probability measure $P$ on $\mathscr{C}$ is uniquely determined by the condition

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\omega:\left(z_{1}(\omega), \ldots, z_{n}(\omega)\right)=\left(u_{1}, \ldots, u_{n}\right)\right]=\pi_{u_{1}} p_{u_{1} u_{2}} \cdots p_{u_{n-1} u_{n}} . \tag{24.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

Thus the coordinate functions $z_{n}(\cdot)$ are a Markov chain with transition probabilities $p_{i j}$ and initial probabilities $\pi_{i}$.

Suppose that the $\pi_{i}$ (until now unspecified) are stationary: $\sum_{i} \pi_{i} p_{i j}=\pi_{j}$. Then

$$
\sum_{u \in S} \pi_{u} p_{u u_{1}} p_{u_{1} u_{2}} \cdots p_{u_{n-1} u_{n}}=\pi_{u_{1}} p_{u_{1} u_{2}} \cdots p_{u_{n-1} u_{n}},
$$

and it follows (see (24.4)) that $T$ preserves $P$. Under the measure $P$ specified by (24.6), $T$ is the Markov shift.

The shift $T$, qua point transformation on $S^{\infty}$, is the same in Examples 24.1 and 24.2. A measure-preserving transformation, however, is the point transformation together with the $\sigma$-field with respect to which it is measurable and the measure it preserves.

Example 24.3. Let $P$ be Lebesgue measure $\lambda$ on the unit interval, and take $T \omega=2 \omega(\bmod 1)$ :

$$
T \omega= \begin{cases}2 \omega & \text { if } 0<\omega \leq \frac{1}{2}, \\ 2 \omega-1 & \text { if } \frac{1}{2}<\omega \leq 1 .\end{cases}
$$

If $\omega$ has nonterminating dyadic expansion $\omega=. d_{1}(\omega) d_{2}(\omega) \ldots$, then $T \omega= . d_{2}(\omega) d_{3}(\omega) \ldots: T$ shifts the digits one place to the left-compare (24.1). Since $T^{-1}(0, x]=\left(0, \frac{1}{2} x\right] \cup\left(\frac{5}{2}, \frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2} x\right]$, it follows by Lemma 1 that $T$ preserves Lebesgue measure. This is the dyadic transformation.

Example 24.4. Let $\Omega$ be the unit circle in the complex plane, let $\mathscr{F}$ be the $\sigma$-field generated by the arcs, and let $P$ be normalized circular Lebesgue measure: map $[0,1)$ to the unit circle by $\phi(x)=e^{2 \pi i x}$ and define $P$ by $P(A)=\lambda\left(\phi^{-1} A\right)$. For a fixed $c$ in $\Omega$, let $T \omega=c \omega$. Since $T$ is effectively the rotation of the circle through the angle $\arg c, T$ preserves $P$. The rotation turns out to have radically different properties according as $c$ is a root of unity or not.

## Ergodicity

The $\mathscr{F}$-set $A$ is invariant under $T$ if $T^{-1} A=A$; it is a nontrivial invariant set if $0<P(A)<1$. And $T$ is by definition ergodic if there are in $\mathscr{F}$ no
nontrivial invariant sets. A measurable function $f$ is invariant if $f(T \omega)=f(\omega)$ for all $\omega ; A$ is invariant if and only if $I_{A}$ is.

The ergodic theorem:
Theorem 24.1. Suppose that $T$ is a measure-preserving transformation on $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ and that $f$ is measurable and integrable. Then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} \frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} f\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)=\hat{f}(\omega) \tag{24.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

with probability 1 , where $\hat{f}$ is invariant and integrable and $E[\hat{f}]=E[f]$. If $T$ is ergodic, then $\hat{f}=E[f]$ with probability 1 .

This will be proved later in the section. In Section 34, $\hat{f}$ will be identified as a conditional expected value (see Example 34.3).

If $f=I_{A}$, (24.7) becomes

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} \frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} I_{A}\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)=\hat{I}_{A}(\omega) \tag{24.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

and in the ergodic case,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\hat{I}_{A}(\omega)=P(A) \tag{24.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

with probability 1 . If $A$ is invariant, then $\hat{I}_{A}(\omega)$ is 1 on $A$ and 0 on $A^{c}$, and so the limit can certainly be nonconstant if $T$ is not ergodic.

Example 24.5. Take $\Omega=\{a, b, c, d, e\}$ and $\mathscr{F}=2^{\Omega}$. If $T$ is the cyclic permutation $T=(a, b, c, d, e)$ and $T$ preserves $P$, then $P$ assigns equal probabilities to the five points. Since $\varnothing$ and $\Omega$ are the only invariant sets, $T$ is ergodic. It is easy to check (24.8) and (24.9) directly.

The transformation $T=(a, b, c)(d, e)$, a product of two cycles, preserves $P$ if and only if $a, b, c$ have equal probabilities and $d, e$ have equal probabilities. If the probabilities are all positive, then since $\{a, b, c\}$ is invariant, $T$ is not ergodic. If, say, $A=\{a, d\}$, the limit in (24.8) is $\frac{1}{3}$ on $\{a, b, c\}$ and $\frac{1}{2}$ on $\{d, e\}$. This illustrates the essential role of ergodicity.

The coordinate functions $z_{n}(\cdot)$ in Example 24.1 are independent, and hence by Kolmogorov's zero-one law every set in the tail field $\mathscr{T}= \bigcap_{n} \sigma\left(z_{n}, z_{n+1}, \ldots\right)$ has probability 0 or 1 . (That the $z_{n}$ take values in the abstract set $S$ does not affect the arguments.) If $A \in \sigma\left(z_{1}, \ldots, z_{k}\right)$, then $T^{-n} A \in \sigma\left(z_{n+1}, \ldots, z_{n+k}\right) \subset \sigma\left(z_{n+1}, z_{n+2}, \ldots\right)$; since this is true for each $k$, $A \in \mathscr{F}=\sigma\left(z_{1}, z_{2}, \ldots\right)$ implies (Theorem 13.1(i)) $T^{-n} A \in \sigma\left(z_{n+1}, z_{n+2}, \ldots\right)$. For $A$ invariant, it follows that $A \in \mathscr{T}$ : The Bernoulli shift is ergodic. Thus
the ergodic theorem does imply that ( 24.5 ) holds with probability 1 , whatever $A$ may be.

The ergodicity of the Bernoulli shift can be proved in a different way.
