If $A=\left[\left(z_{1}, \ldots, z_{n}\right)=u\right]$ and $B=\left[\left(z_{1}, \ldots, z_{k}\right)=v\right]$ for an $n$-tuple $u$ and a $k$ tuple $v$, and if $P$ is given by (24.3), then $P\left(A \cap T^{-n} B\right)=P(A) P(B)$ because $T^{-n} B=\left[\left(z_{n+1}, \ldots, z_{n+k}\right)=v\right]$. Fix $n$ and $A$, and use the $\pi-\lambda$ theorem to show that this holds for all $B$ in $\mathscr{F}$. If $B$ is invariant, then $P(A \cap B)= P(A) P(B)$ holds for the $A$ above, and hence ( $\pi-\lambda$ again) holds for all $A$. Taking $B=A$ shows that $P(B)=(P(B))^{2}$ for invariant $B$, and $P(B)$ is 0 or 1. This argument is very close to the proof of the zero-one law, but a modification of it gives a criterion for ergodicity that applies to the Markov shift and other transformations.

Lemma 2. Suppose that $\mathscr{P} \subset \mathscr{F}_{0} \subset \mathscr{F}$, where $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ is a field, every set in $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ is a finite or countable disjoint union of $\mathscr{P}_{\text {sets }}$, and $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ generates $\mathscr{F}$. Suppose further that there exists a positive $c$ with this property: For each $A$ in $\mathscr{P}$ there is an integer $n_{A}$ such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(A \cap T^{-n_{A}} B\right) \geq c P(A) P(B) \tag{24.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all $B$ in $\mathscr{P}$. Then $T^{-1} C=C$ implies that $P(C)$ is 0 or 1 .
It is convenient not to require that $T$ preserve $P$; but if it does, then it is an ergodic measure-preserving transformation. In the argument just given, $\mathscr{P}$ consists of the thin cylinders, $n_{A}=n$ if $A=\left[\left(z_{1}, \ldots, z_{n}\right)=u\right], c=1$, and $\mathscr{F}_{0}=\mathscr{C}_{0}$ is the class of cylinders.

Proof. Since every $\mathscr{F}_{0}$-set is a disjoint union of $\mathscr{P}$-sets, (24.10) holds for $B \in \mathscr{F}_{0}$ (and $A \in \mathscr{P}$ ). Since for fixed $A$ the class of $B$ satisfying (24.10) is monotone, it contains $\mathscr{F}$ (Theorem 3.4). If $B$ is invariant, it follows that $P(A \cap B) \geq \mathrm{c} P(A) P(B)$ for $A$ in $\mathscr{P}$. But then, by the same argument, the inequality holds for all $A$ in $\mathscr{F}$. Take $A=B^{c}$ : If $B$ is invariant, then $P\left(B^{c}\right) P(B)=0$ and hence $P(B)$ is 0 or 1 . $\square$

To treat the Markov shift, take $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ to consist of the cylinders and $\mathscr{P}$ to consist of the thin ones. If $A=\left[\left(z_{1}, \ldots, z_{n}\right)=\left(u_{1}, \ldots, u_{n}\right)\right], n_{A}=n+m-1$, and $B=\left[\left(z_{1}, \ldots, z_{k}\right)=\left(v_{1}, \ldots, v_{k}\right)\right]$, then

$$
\begin{gather*}
P(A) P(B)=\pi_{u_{1}} p_{u_{1} u_{2}} \cdots p_{u_{n-1} u_{n}} \pi_{l_{1}} p_{l_{1} l_{2}} \cdots p_{l_{k-1} l_{k}} \\
P\left(A \cap T^{-n_{A}} B\right)=\pi_{u_{1}} p_{u_{1} u_{2}} \cdots p_{u_{n-1} u_{n}} p_{u_{n} l_{1}}^{(m)} p_{l_{1} l_{2}} \cdots p_{l_{k-1} l_{k}} \tag{24.11}
\end{gather*}
$$

The lemma will apply if there exist an integer $m$ and a positive $c$ such that $p_{i j}^{(m)} \geq \mathrm{c} \pi_{j}$ for all $i$ and $j$. By Theorem 8.9 (or Lemma 2, p. 125), there is in the irreducible, aperiodic case an $m$ such that all $p_{i j}^{(m)}$ are positive; take $c$
less than the minimum. By Lemma 2, the corresponding Markov shift is ergodic.

Example 24.6. Maps preserve ergodicity. Suppose that $\psi: \Omega \rightarrow \Omega$ is measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{F}$ and commutes with $T$ in the sense that $\psi T \omega=T \psi \omega$. If $T$ preserves $P$, it also preserves $P \psi^{-1}: P \psi^{-1}\left(T^{-1} A\right)=P\left(T^{-1} \psi^{-1} A\right)=P \psi^{-1}(A)$. And if $T$ is ergodic under $P$, it is also ergodic under $P \psi^{-1}$ : if $A$ is invariant, so is $\psi^{-1} A$, and hence $P \psi^{-1}(A)$ is 0 or 1 . These simple observations are useful in studying the ergodicity of stochastic processes (Theorem 36.4).

## Ergodicity of Rotations

The dyadic transformation, Example 24.3, is essentially the same as the Bernoulli shift. In any case, it is easy to use the zero-one law or Lemma 2 to show that it is ergodic. From this and the ergodic theorem, the normal number theorem follows once again.

Consider the rotations of Example 24.4. If the complex number $c$ defining the rotation ( $T \omega=c \omega$ ) is -1 , then the set consisting of the first and third quadrants is a nontrivial invariant set, and hence $T$ is not ergodic. A similar construction shows that $T$ is nonergodic whenever $c$ is a root of unity.

In the opposite case, $c$ is ergodic. In the first place, it is an old number-theoretic fact due to Kronecker that if $c$ is not a root of unity then the orbit ( $\omega, c \omega, c^{2} \omega, \ldots$ ) of every $\omega$ is dense. Since the orbits are rotations of one another, it suffices to prove that the orbit ( $1, c, c^{2}, \ldots$ ) of 1 is dense. But if $c$ is not a root of unity, then the elements of this orbit are all distinct and hence by compactness have a limit point $\omega_{0}$. For arbitrary $\epsilon$, there are distinct points $c^{n}$ and $c^{n+k}$ within $\epsilon / 2$ of $\omega_{0}$ and hence within $\epsilon$ of each other (distance measured along the arc). But then, since the distance from $c^{n+j k}$ to $c^{n+(j+1) k}$ is the same as that from $c^{n}$ to $c^{n+k}$, it is clear that for some $m$ the points $c^{n}, c^{n+k}, \ldots, c^{n+m k}$ form a chain which extends all the way around the circle and in which the distance from one point to the next is less than $\epsilon$. Thus every point on the circle is within $\epsilon$ of some point of the orbit ( $1, c, c^{2}, \ldots$ ), which is indeed dense.

To use this result to prove ergodicity, suppose that $A$ is invariant and $P(A)>0$. To show that $P(A)$ must then be 1 , observe first that for arbitrary $\epsilon$ there is an arc $I$, of length at most $\epsilon$, satisfying $P(A \cap I)>(1-\epsilon) P(A)$. Indeed, $A$ can be covered by a sequence $I_{1}, I_{2}, \ldots$ of arcs for which $P(A) /(1-\epsilon)>\sum_{n} P\left(I_{n}\right)$; the arcs can be taken disjoint and of length less than $\epsilon$. Since $\sum_{n} P\left(A \cap I_{n}\right)=P(A)>(1-\epsilon) \sum_{n} P\left(I_{n}\right)$, there is an $n$ for which $P\left(A \cap I_{n}\right)>(1-\epsilon) P\left(I_{n}\right)$ : take $I=I_{n}$. Let $I$ have length $\alpha ; \alpha \leq \epsilon$.

Since $A$ is invariant and $T$ is invertible and preserves $P$, it follows that $P(A \cap \left.T^{n} I\right) \geq(1-\epsilon) P\left(T^{n} I\right)$. Suppose the arc $I$ runs from $a$ to $b$. Let $n_{1}$ be arbitrary and, using the fact that ( $T^{n} a$ ) is dense, choose $n_{2}$ so that $T^{n_{1}} I$ and $T^{n_{2}} I$ are disjoint and the distance from $T^{n_{1}} b$ to $T^{n_{2}} a$ is less than $\epsilon \alpha$. Then choose $n_{3}$ so that $T^{n_{1}} I, T^{n_{2}} I, T^{n_{3}} I$ are disjoint and the distance from $T^{n_{2}} b$ to $T^{n_{7}} a$ is less than $\epsilon \alpha$. Continue until $T^{n_{k}} b$ is within $\alpha$ of $T^{n_{i}} a$ and a further step is impossible. Since the $T^{n_{i}} I$ are disjoint, $k \alpha \leq 1$; and by the construction, the $T^{n_{i}} I$ cover the circle to within a set of measure $k \epsilon \alpha+\alpha$, which is at most $2 \epsilon$. And now by disjointness,

$$
P(A) \geq \sum_{i=1}^{k} P\left(A \cap T^{n_{i}} I\right) \geq(1-\epsilon) \sum_{i=1}^{k} P\left(T^{n_{i}} I\right) \geq(1-\epsilon)(1-2 \epsilon)
$$

Since $\epsilon$ was arbitrary, $P(A)$ must be $1: T$ is ergodic if $c$ is not a root of unity. ${ }^{\dagger}$
${ }^{\dagger}$ For a simple Fourier-series proof, see Problem 26.30.

## Proof of the Ergodic Theorem

The argument depends on a preliminary result the statement and proof of which are most clearly expressed in terms of functional operators. For a real function $f$ on $\Omega$, let $U f$ be the real function with value $(U f)(\omega)=f(T \omega)$ at $\omega$. If $f$ is integrable, then by change of variable (Theorem 16.13),

$$
\begin{equation*}
E[U f]=\int_{\Omega} f(T \omega) P(d \omega)=\int_{\Omega} f(\omega) P T^{-1}(d \omega)=E[f] \tag{24.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

And the operator $U$ is nonnegative in the sense that it carries nonnegative functions to nonnegative functions; hence $f \leq g$ (pointwise) implies $U f \leq U g$.

Make these pointwise definitions: $S_{0} f=0, S_{n} f=f+U f+\cdots+U^{n-1} f$, $M_{n} f=\max _{0 \leq k \leq n} S_{k} f$, and $M_{\infty} f=\sup _{n \geq 0} S_{n} f=\sup _{n \geq 0} M_{n} f$. The maximal ergodic theorem:

Theorem 24.2. If $f$ is integrable, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{M_{x} f>0} f d P \geq 0 \tag{24.13}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Since $B_{n}=\left[M_{n} f>0\right] \uparrow\left[M_{\infty} f>0\right]$, it is enough, by the dominated convergence theorem, to show that $\int_{B_{n}} f d P \geq 0$. On $B_{n}, M_{n} f= \max _{1 \leq k \leq n} S_{k} f$. Since the operator $U$ is nonnegative, $S_{k} f=f+U S_{k-1} f \leq f+U M_{n} f$ for $1 \leq k \leq n$, and therefore $M_{n} f \leq f+U M_{n} f$ on $B_{n}$. This and the fact that the function $U M_{n} f$ is nonnegative imply

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{\Omega} M_{n} f d P & =\int_{B_{n}} M_{n} f d P \leq \int_{B_{n}}\left(f+U M_{n} f\right) d P \\
& \leq \int_{B_{n}} f d P+\int_{\Omega} U M_{n} f d P=\int_{B_{n}} f d P+\int_{\Omega} M_{n} f d P
\end{aligned}
$$

where the last equality follows from (24.12). Hence $\int_{B_{n}} f d P \geq 0$. $\square$

Replace $f$ by $f I_{A}$. If $A$ is invariant, then $S_{n}\left(f I_{A}\right)=\left(S_{n} f\right) I_{A}$, and $M_{\infty}\left(f I_{A}\right) =\left(M_{\infty} f\right) I_{A}$, and therefore (24.13) gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{A \cap\left[M_{x} f>0\right]} f d P \geq 0 \quad \text { if } T^{-1} A=A \tag{24.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

Now replace $f$ here by $f-\lambda, \lambda$ a constant. Clearly $\left[M_{\infty}(f-\lambda)>0\right]$ is the set
where for some $n \geq 1, S_{n}(f-\lambda)>0$. or $n^{-1} S_{n} f>\lambda$. Let

$$
\begin{equation*}
F_{\lambda}=\left[\omega: \sup _{n \geq 1} \frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} f\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)>\lambda\right] \tag{24.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

it follows by (24.14) that $\int_{A \cap F_{\lambda}}(f-\lambda) d P \geq 0$, or

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lambda P\left(A \cap F_{\lambda}\right) \leq \int_{A \cap F_{\lambda}} f d P \quad \text { if } T^{-1} A=A \tag{24.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

The $\lambda$ here can have either sign.
Proof of Theorem 24.1. To prove that the averages $a_{n}(\omega)= n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} f\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)$ converge, consider the set

$$
A_{\alpha, \beta}=\left[\omega: \lim \inf _{n} a_{n}(\omega)<\alpha<\beta<\lim \sup _{n} a_{n}(\omega)\right]
$$

for $\alpha<\beta$. Since $A_{\alpha, \beta}=A_{\alpha, \beta} \cap F_{\beta}$ and $A_{\alpha, \beta}$ is invariant, (24.16) gives $\beta P\left(A_{\alpha, \beta}\right) \leq \int_{A_{\alpha, \beta}} f d P$. The same result with $-f,-\beta,-\alpha$ in place of $f, \alpha, \beta$ is $\int_{A_{\alpha, \beta}} f d P \leq \alpha P\left(A_{\alpha, \beta}\right)$. Since $\alpha<\beta$, the two inequalities together lead to $P\left(A_{\alpha, \beta}\right)=0$. Take the union over rational $\alpha$ and $\beta$ : The averages $a_{n}(\omega)$ converge, that is,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} a_{n}(\omega)=\hat{f}(\omega) \tag{24.17}
\end{equation*}
$$

with probability 1 , where $\hat{f}$ may take the values $\pm \infty$ at certain values of $\omega$.
Because of (24.12), $E\left[a_{n}\right]=E[f]$; if it is shown that the $a_{n}(\omega)$ are uniformly integrable, then it will follow (Theorem 16.14) that $\hat{f}$ is integrable and $E[\hat{f}]=E[f]$.

By (24.16), $\lambda P\left(F_{\lambda}\right) \leq E[|f|]$. Combine this with the same inequality for $-f$ : If $G_{\lambda}=\left[\omega: \sup _{n}\left|a_{n}(\omega)\right|>\lambda\right]$, then $\lambda P\left(G_{\lambda}\right) \leq 2 E[|f|]$ (trivial if $\lambda \leq 0$ ). Therefore, for positive $\alpha$ and $\lambda$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{\left[\left|a_{n}\right|>\lambda\right]}\left|a_{n}\right| d P & \leq \frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} \int_{G_{\lambda}}\left|f\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)\right| P(d \omega) \\
& \leq \frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n}\left(\int_{\left|f\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)\right|>\alpha}\left|f\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)\right| P(d \omega)+\alpha P\left(G_{\lambda}\right)\right) \\
& =\int_{|f(\omega)|>\alpha}|f(\omega)| P(d \omega)+\alpha P\left(G_{\lambda}\right) \\
& \leq \int_{|f(\omega)|>\alpha}|f(\omega)| P(d \omega)+2 \frac{\alpha}{\lambda} E[|f|]
\end{aligned}
$$

Take $\alpha=\lambda^{1 / 2}$; since $f$ is integrable, the final expression here goes to 0 as
$\lambda \rightarrow \infty$. The $a_{n}(\omega)$ are therefore uniformly integrable, and $E[\hat{f}]=E[f]$. The uniform integrability also implies $E\left[\left|a_{n}-\hat{f}\right|\right] \rightarrow 0$.

Set $\hat{f}(\omega)=0$ outside the set where the $a_{n}(\omega)$ have a finite limit. Then (24.17) still holds with probability 1 , and $\hat{f}(T \omega)=\hat{f}(\omega)$. Since $[\omega: \hat{f}(\omega) \leq x]$ is invariant, in the ergodic case its measure is either 0 or 1 ; if $x_{0}$ is the infimum of the $x$ for which it is 1 , then $\hat{f}(\omega)=x_{0}$ with probability 1 , and from $x_{0}=E[\hat{f}]=E[f]$ it follows that $\hat{f}(\omega)=E[f]$ with probability 1. $\square$

## The Continued-Fraction Transformation

Let $\Omega$ consist of the irrationals in the unit interval, and for $x$ in $\Omega$ let $T x=\{1 / x\}$ and $a_{1}(x)=\lfloor 1 / x\rfloor$ be the fractional and integral parts of $1 / x$. This defines a mapping

$$
\begin{equation*}
T x=\left\{\frac{1}{x}\right\}=\frac{1}{x}-\left\lfloor\frac{1}{x}\right\rfloor=\frac{1}{x}-a_{1}(x) \tag{24.18}
\end{equation*}
$$

of $\Omega$ into itself, a mapping associated with the continued-fraction expansion of $x$ [A36]. Concentrating on irrational $x$ avoids some triviai details connected with the rational case, where the expansion is finite; it is an inessential restriction because the interest here centers on results of the almost-everywhere kind.

For $x \in \Omega$ and $n \geq 1$ let $a_{n}(x)=a_{1}\left(T^{n-1} x\right)$ be the $n$th partial quotient, and define integer-valued functions $p_{n}(x)$ and $q_{n}(x)$ by the recursions

$$
\begin{array}{llll}
p_{-1}(x)=1, & p_{0}(x)=0, & p_{n}(x)=a_{n}(x) p_{n-1}(x)+p_{n-2}(x), & n \geq 1,  \tag{24.19}\\
q_{-1}(x)=0, & q_{0}(x)=1, & q_{n}(x)=a_{n}(x) q_{n-1}(x)+q_{n-2}(x), & n \geq 1 .
\end{array}
$$

Simple induction arguments show that

$$
\begin{equation*}
p_{n-1}(x) q_{n}(x)-p_{n}(x) q_{n-1}(x)=(-1)^{n}, \quad n \geq 0, \tag{24.20}
\end{equation*}
$$

and [A37: (27)]

$$
\begin{equation*}
x=1 \sqrt{a_{1}(x)}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{a_{n-1}(x)}+1 \sqrt{a_{n}(x)+T^{n} x}, \quad n \geq 1 . \tag{24.21}
\end{equation*}
$$

It also follows inductively [A36: (26)] that

$$
\begin{align*}
& \sqrt[1]{a_{1}(x)}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{a_{n-1}(x)}+1 \sqrt{a_{n}(x)+t}  \tag{24.22}\\
& \quad=\frac{p_{n}(x)+t p_{n-1}(x)}{q_{n}(x)+t q_{n-1}(x)}, \quad n \geq 1,0 \leq t \leq 1 .
\end{align*}
$$

Taking $t=0$ here gives the formula for the $n$th convergent:

$$
\begin{equation*}
1 \sqrt{a_{1}(x)}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{a_{n}(x)}=\frac{p_{n}(x)}{q_{n}(x)}, \quad n \geq 1, \tag{24.23}
\end{equation*}
$$

where, as follows from (24.20), $p_{n}(x)$ and $q_{n}(x)$ are relatively prime. By (24.21) and (24.22),

$$
\begin{equation*}
x=\frac{p_{n}(x)+\left(T^{n} x\right) p_{n-1}(x)}{q_{n}(x)+\left(T^{n} x\right) q_{n-1}(x)}, \quad n \geq 0, \tag{24.24}
\end{equation*}
$$

which, together with (24.20), implies ${ }^{\dagger}$

$$
\begin{equation*}
x-\frac{p_{n}(x)}{q_{n}(x)}=\frac{(-1)^{n}}{q_{n}(x)\left(\left(T^{n} x\right)^{-1} q_{n}(x)+q_{n-1}(x)\right)}, \quad n \geq 0 . \tag{24.25}
\end{equation*}
$$

Thus the convergents for even $n$ fall to the left of $x$, and those for odd $n$ fall to the right. And since (24.19) obviously implies that $q_{n}(x)$ goes to infinity with $n$, the convergents $p_{n}(x) / q_{n}(x)$ do converge to $x$ : Each irrational $x$ in $(0,1)$ has the infinite simple continued-fraction representation

$$
\begin{equation*}
x=1 \sqrt{a_{1}(x)}+1 \sqrt{a_{2}(x)}+\cdots . \tag{24.26}
\end{equation*}
$$

The representation is unique [A36: (35)], and $T x=1 \sqrt{a_{2}(x)}+1 \sqrt{a_{3}(x)}+\cdots$ : $T$ shifts the partial quotients in the same way the dyadic transformation (Example 24.3) shifts the digits of the dyadic expansion. Since the continuedfraction transformation turns out to be ergodic, it can be used to study the continued-fraction algorithm.

Suppose now that $a_{1}, a_{2}, \ldots$ are positive integers and define $p_{n}$ and $q_{n}$ by the recursions (24.19) without the argument $x$. Then (24.20) again holds (without the $x$ ), and so $p_{n} / q_{n}-p_{n-1} / q_{n-1}=(-1)^{n+1} / q_{n-1} q_{n}, n \geq 1$. Since $q_{n}$ increases to infinity, the right side here is the $n$th term of a convergent alternating series. And since $p_{0} / q_{0}=0$, the $n$th partial sum is $p_{n} / q_{n}$, which therefore converges to some limit: Every simple infinite continued fraction converges, and [A36: (36)] the limit is an irrational in ( 0,1 ).

Let $\Delta_{a_{1} . . a_{n}}$ be the set of $x$ in $\Omega$ such that $a_{k}(x)=a_{k}$ for $1 \leq k \leq n$; call it a fundamental set of rank $n$. These sets are analogous to the dyadic intervals and the thin cylinders. For an explicit description of $\Delta_{a_{1}} a_{n}$-necessary for the proof of ergodicity-consider the function

$$
\begin{equation*}
\psi_{a_{1} \ldots a_{n}}(t)=1 \sqrt{a_{1}}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{a_{n-1}}+1 \sqrt{a_{n}+t} . \tag{24.27}
\end{equation*}
$$

[^11]If $x \in \Delta_{a_{1}} a_{n}$, then $x=\psi_{a_{1}} . . a_{n}\left(T^{n} x\right)$ by (24.21); on the other hand, because of the uniqueness of the partial quotients [A36: (33)], if $t$ is an irrational in the unit interval, then (24.27) lies in $\Delta_{a_{1} . . a_{n}}$. Thus $\Delta_{a_{1} \quad a_{n}}$ is the image under (24.27) of $\Omega$ itself.

Just as (24.22) follows by induction, so does

$$
\begin{equation*}
\psi_{a_{1}} \quad a_{n}(t)=\frac{p_{n}+t p_{n-1}}{q_{n}+t q_{n-1}} . \tag{24.28}
\end{equation*}
$$

And $\psi_{a_{1}} \quad a_{n}(t)$ is increasing or decreasing in $t$ according as $n$ is even or odd, as is clear from the form of (24.27) (or differentiate in (24.28) and use (24.20)). It follows that

$$
\Delta_{a_{1}} \quad a_{n}= \begin{cases}\left(\frac{p_{n}}{q_{n}}, \frac{p_{n}+p_{n-1}}{q_{n}+q_{n-1}}\right) \cap \Omega & \text { if } n \text { is even, } \\ \left(\frac{p_{n}+p_{n-1}}{q_{n}+q_{n-1}}, \frac{p_{n}}{q_{n}}\right) \cap \Omega & \text { if } n \text { is odd. }\end{cases}
$$

By (24.20), this set has Lebesgue measure

$$
\lambda\left(\begin{array}{cc}
\Delta_{a_{1}} & a_{n} \tag{24.29}
\end{array}\right)=\frac{1}{q_{n}\left(q_{n}+q_{n-1}\right)} .
$$

The fundamental sets of rank $n$ form a partition of $\Omega$, and their unions form a field $\mathscr{F}_{n}$; let $\mathscr{F}_{0}=\bigcup_{n=1}^{\infty} \mathscr{F}_{n}$. Then $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ is the field generated by the class $\mathscr{P}^{\mathscr{P}}$ of all the fundamental sets, and since each set in $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ is in some $\mathscr{F}_{n}$, each is a finite or countable disjoint union of $\mathscr{P}_{\text {sets. }}$. Since $q_{n} \geq 2 q_{n-2}$ by (24.19), induction gives $q_{n} \geq 2^{(n-1) / 2}$ for $n \geq 0$. And now (24.29) implies that $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ generates the $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{F}$ of linear Borel sets that are subsets of $\Omega$ (use Theorem 10.1(ii)). Thus $\mathscr{P}, \mathscr{F}_{0}, \mathscr{F}$ are related as in the hypothesis of Lemma 2. Clearly $T$ is measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{F}$.

Although $T$ does not preserve $\lambda$, it does preserve Gauss's measure, defined by

$$
\begin{equation*}
P(A)=\frac{1}{\log 2} \int_{A} \frac{d x}{1+x}, \quad A \in \mathscr{F} \tag{24.30}
\end{equation*}
$$

In fact, since

$$
T^{-1}((0, t) \cap \Omega)=\bigcup_{k=1}^{\infty}\left(\left(\frac{1}{k+i}, \frac{1}{k}\right) \cap \Omega\right),
$$

it is enough to verify

$$
\int_{0}^{I} \frac{d x}{1+x}=\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \int_{I /(k+1)}^{I / k} \frac{d x}{1+x}=\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \int_{1 /(k+l)}^{1 / k} \frac{d x}{1+x}
$$

Gauss's measure is useful because it is preserved by $T$ and has the same sets of measure 0 as Lebesgue measure does.

Proof that $T$ is ergodic. Fix $a_{1}, \ldots, a_{n}$, and write $\psi_{n}$ for $\psi_{a_{1} \quad a_{n}}$ and $\Delta_{n}$ for $\Delta_{a_{1}} a_{n}$. Suppose that $n$ is even, so that $\psi_{n}$ is increasing. If $x \in \Delta_{n}$, then (since $\left.x=\psi_{n}\left(T^{n} x\right)\right) s<T^{n} x<t$ if and only if $\psi_{n}(s)<x<\psi_{n}(t)$; and this last condition implies $x \in \Delta_{n}$. Combined with (24.28) and (24.20), this shows that

$$
\lambda\left(\Delta_{n} \cap\left[x: s<T^{n} x<t\right]\right)=\psi_{n}(t)-\psi_{n}(s)=\frac{t-s}{\left(q_{n}+s q_{n-1}\right)\left(q_{n}+t q_{n-1}\right)}
$$

If $B$ is an interval with endpoints $s$ and $t$, then by (24.29),

$$
\lambda\left(\Delta_{n} \cap T^{-n} B\right)=\lambda\left(\Delta_{n}\right) \lambda(B) \frac{q_{n}\left(q_{n}+q_{n-1}\right)}{\left(q_{n}+s q_{n-1}\right)\left(q_{n}+t q_{n-1}\right)}
$$

A similar argument establishes this for $n$ odd. Since the ratio on the right lies between $\frac{1}{2}$ and 2,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{1}{2} \lambda\left(\Delta_{n}\right) \lambda(B) \leq \lambda\left(\Delta_{n} \cap T^{-n} B\right) \leq 2 \lambda\left(\Delta_{n}\right) \lambda(B) \tag{24.31}
\end{equation*}
$$

Therefore, (24.10) holds for $\mathscr{P}, \mathscr{F}_{0}, \mathscr{F}$ as defined above, $A=\Delta_{n}, n_{A}=n$, $c=\frac{1}{2}$, and $\lambda$ in the role of $P$. Thus $T^{-1} C=C$ implies that $\lambda(C)$ is 0 or 1 , and since Gauss's measure (24.30) comes from a density, $P(C)$ is 0 or 1 as well. Therefore, $T$ is an ergodic measure-preserving transformation on $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$.

It follows by the ergodic theorem that if $f$ is integrable, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} \frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} f\left(T^{k-1} x\right)=\frac{1}{\log 2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{f(x)}{1+x} d x \tag{24.32}
\end{equation*}
$$

holds almost everywhere. Since the density in (24.30) is bounded away from 0 and $\infty$, the "integrable" and "almost everywhere" here can refer to $P$ or to $\lambda$ indifferently.

Taking $f$ to be the indicator of the $x$-set where $a_{1}(x)=k$ shows that the asymptotic relative frequency of $k$ annong the partial quotients is almost everywhere equal to

$$
\frac{1}{\log 2} \int_{1 /(k+1)}^{1 / k} \frac{d x}{1+x}=\frac{1}{\log 2} \log \frac{(k+1)^{2}}{k(k+2)}
$$

In particular, the partial quotients are unbounded almost everywhere.

For understanding the accuracy of the continued-fraction algorithm, the magnitude of $a_{n}(x)$ is less important than that of $q_{n}(x)$. The key relationship is

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{1}{q_{n}(x)\left(q_{n}(x)+q_{n+1}(x)\right)}<\left|x-\frac{p_{n}(x)}{q_{n}(x)}\right|<\frac{1}{q_{n}(x) q_{n+1}(x)}, \tag{24.33}
\end{equation*}
$$

which follows from (24.25) and (24.19). Suppose it is shown that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} \frac{1}{n} \log q_{n}(x)=\frac{\pi^{2}}{12 \log 2} \tag{24.34}
\end{equation*}
$$

almost everywhere; (24.33) will then imply that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} \frac{1}{n} \log \left|x-\frac{p_{n}(x)}{q_{n}(x)}\right|=-\frac{\pi^{2}}{6 \log 2}: \tag{24.35}
\end{equation*}
$$

The discrepancy between $x$ and its $n$th convergent is almost everywhere of the order $e^{-n \pi^{2} /(6 \log 2)}$.

To prove (24.34), note first that since $\dot{p}_{j+1}(x)=q_{j}(T x)$ by (24.19), the product $\prod_{k=1}^{n} p_{n-k+1}\left(T^{k-1} x\right) / q_{n-k+1}\left(T^{k-1} x\right)$ telescopes to $1 / q_{n}(x)$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
\log \frac{1}{q_{n}(x)}=\sum_{k=1}^{n} \log \frac{p_{n-k+1}\left(T^{k-1} x\right)}{q_{n-k+1}\left(T^{k-1} x\right)} . \tag{24.36}
\end{equation*}
$$

As observed earlier, $q_{n}(x) \geq 2^{(n-1) / 2}$ for $n \geq 1$. Therefore, by (24.33),

$$
\left|\frac{x}{p_{n}(x) / q_{n}(x)}-1\right| \leq \frac{1}{q_{n+1}(x)} \leq \frac{1}{2^{n / 2}}, \quad n \geq 1 .
$$

Since $|\log (1+s)| \leq 4|s|$ if $|s| \leq 1 / \sqrt{2}$,

$$
\left|\log x-\log \frac{p_{n}(x)}{q_{n}(x)}\right| \leq \frac{4}{2^{n / 2}}
$$

Therefore, by (24.36),

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left|\sum_{k=1}^{n} \log T^{k-1} x-\log \frac{1}{q_{n}(x)}\right| & \leq \sum_{k=1}^{n}\left|\log T^{k-1} x-\log \frac{p_{n-k+1}\left(T^{k-1} x\right)}{q_{n-k+1}\left(T^{k-1} x\right)}\right| \\
& \leq \sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \frac{4}{2^{i / 2}}<\infty .
\end{aligned}
$$

By the ergodic theorem, then, ${ }^{\dagger}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
\lim _{n} \frac{1}{n} \log \frac{1}{q_{n}(x)} & =\frac{1}{\log 2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{\log x}{1+x} d x=\frac{-1}{\log 2} \int_{0}^{1} \log (1+x) \frac{d x}{x} \\
& =\frac{-1}{\log 2} \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{(-1)^{k}}{(k+1)^{2}}=\frac{-\pi^{2}}{12 \log 2} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence (24.34).

## Diophantine Approximation

The fundamental theorem of the measure theory of Diophantine approximation, due to Khinchine, is Theorem 1.5 together with Theorem 1.6. As in Section 1, let $\varphi(q)$ be a positive function of integers and let $A_{\varphi}$ be the set of $x$ in ( 0,1 ) such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left|x-\frac{p}{q}\right|<\frac{1}{q^{2} \varphi(q)} \tag{24,37}
\end{equation*}
$$

has infinitely many irreducible solutions $p / q$. If $\Sigma 1 / q \varphi(q)$ converges, then $A_{\varphi}$ has Lebesgue measure 0, as was proved in Section 1 (Theorem 1.6). It remains to prove that if $\varphi$ is nondecreasing and $\Sigma 1 / q \varphi(q)$ diveiges, then $A_{\varphi}$ has Lebesgue measure 1 (Theorem 1.5). It is enough to consider irrational $x$.

Lemma 3. For positive $\alpha_{n}$, the probability ( $P$ or $\lambda$ ) of $\left[x: a_{n}(x)>\alpha_{n}\right.$ i.o. $]$ is 0 or 1 as $\Sigma 1 / \alpha_{n}$ converges or diverges.

Proof. Let $E_{n}=\left[x: a_{n}(x)>\alpha_{n}\right]$. Since $P\left(E_{n}\right)=P\left[x: a_{1}(x)>\alpha_{n}\right]$ is of the order $1 / \alpha_{n}$, the first Borel-Cantelli lemma settles the convergent case (not needed in the proof of Theorem 1.5).

By (24.31),

$$
\lambda\left(\Delta_{n} \cap E_{n+1}\right) \geq \frac{1}{2} \lambda\left(\Delta_{n}\right) \lambda\left[x: a_{1}(x)>\alpha_{n+1}\right] \geq \frac{1}{2} \lambda\left(\Delta_{n}\right) \frac{1}{\alpha_{n+1}+1} .
$$

Taking a union over certain of the $\Delta_{n}$ shows that for $m<n$,

$$
\lambda\left(E_{m}^{c} \cap \cdots \cap E_{n}^{c} \cap E_{n+1}\right) \geq \lambda\left(E_{m}^{c} \cap \cdots \cap E_{n}^{c}\right) \frac{1}{2\left(\alpha_{n+1}+1\right)} .
$$

By induction on n,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\lambda\left(E_{m}^{c} \cap \cdots \cap E_{n}^{c}\right) & \leq \prod_{k=m}^{n}\left(1-\frac{1}{2\left(\alpha_{k+1}+1\right)}\right) \\
& \leq \exp \left[-\sum_{k=m}^{n} \frac{1}{2\left(\alpha_{k+1}+1\right)}\right],
\end{aligned}
$$

as in the proof of the second Borel-Cantelli lemma. $\square$
${ }^{\dagger}$ Integrate by parts over ( $\alpha, 1$ ) and then let $\alpha \downarrow 0$. For the series, see Problem 26.28. The specific value of the limit in (24.34) is not needed for the application that follows.

Proof of Theorem 1.5. Fix an integer $N$ such that $\log N$ exceeds the limit in (24.34). Then, except on a set of measure 0 ,

$$
\begin{equation*}
q_{n}(x)<N^{n} \tag{24.38}
\end{equation*}
$$

holds for all but finitely many $n$. Since $\mathscr{4}$ is nondecreasing,

$$
\sum_{N^{n} \leq q<N^{n+1}} \cdot \frac{1}{q \varphi(q)} \leq \frac{N}{\varphi\left(N^{n}\right)},
$$

and $\Sigma 1 / \varphi\left(N^{n}\right)$ diverges if $\Sigma 1 / q \varphi(q)$ does. By the lemma, outside a set of measure 0 , $a_{n+1}(x) \geq \varphi\left(N^{n}\right)$ holds for infinitely many $n$. If this inequality and (24.38) both hold, then by (24.33) and the assumption that $\varphi$ is nondecreasing,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left|x-\frac{p_{n}(x)}{q_{n}(x)}\right| & <\frac{1}{q_{n}(x) q_{n+1}(x)} \leq \frac{1}{a_{n+1}(x) q_{n}^{2}(x)} \\
& \leq \frac{1}{\varphi\left(N^{n}\right) q_{n}^{2}(x)} \leq \frac{1}{\varphi\left(q_{n}(x)\right) q_{n}^{2}(x)} .
\end{aligned}
$$

But $p_{n}(x) / q_{n}(x)$ is irreducible by (24.20).

## PROBLEMS

24.1. Fix ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}$ ) and a $T$ measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{F}$. The probability measures on ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}$ ) preserved by $T$ form a convex set $C$. Show that $T$ is ergodic under $P$ if and only if $P$ is an extreme point of $C$-cannot be represented as a proper convex combination of distinct elements of $C$.
24.2. Show that $T$ is ergodic if and only if $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n-1} P\left(A \cap T^{-k} B\right) \rightarrow P(A) P(B)$ for all $A$ and $B$ (or all $A$ and $B$ in a $\pi$-system generating $\mathscr{F}$ ).
24.3. ↑ The transformation $T$ is mixing if

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(A \cap T^{-n} B\right) \rightarrow P(A) P(B) \tag{24.39}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all $A$ and $B$.
(a) Show that mixing implies ergodicity.
(b) Show that $T$ is mixing if (24.39) holds for all $A$ and $B$ in a $\pi$-system generating $\mathscr{F}$.
(c) Show that the Bernoulli shift is mixing.
(d) Show that a cyclic permutation is ergodic but not mixing.
(e) Show that if $c$ is not a root of unity, then the rotation (Example 24.4) is ergodic but not mixing.
24.4. $\uparrow$ Write $T^{-n} \mathscr{F}=\left[T^{-n} A: A \in \mathscr{F}\right]$, and call the $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}=\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} T^{-n} \mathscr{F}$ trivial if every set in it has probability either 0 or 1 . (If $T$ is invertible, $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}$ is $\mathscr{F}$ and hence is trivial only in uninteresting cases.)
(a) Show that if $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}$ is trivial, then $T$ is ergodic. (A cyclic permutation is ergodic even though $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}$ is not trivial.)
(b) Show that if the hypotheses of Lemma 2 are satisfied, then $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}$ is trivial.
(c) It can be shown by martingale theory that if $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}$ is trivial, then $T$ is mixing; see Problem 35.20. Reconsider Problem 24.3(c)
24.5. $8.3524 .4 \uparrow$ (a) Show that the shift corresponding to an irreducible, aperiodic Markov chain is mixing. Do this first by Problem 835, then by Problem 24.4(b), (c).
(b) Show that if the chain is irreducible but has period greater than 1 , then the shift is ergodic but not mixing.
(c) Suppose the state space splits into two closed, disjoint, nonempty subsets, and that the initial distribution (stationary) gives positive weight to each. Show that the corresponding shift is not ergodic.
24.6. Show that if $T$ is ergodic and if $j$ is nonnegative and $E[f]=\infty$, then $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} f\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right) \rightarrow \infty$ with probability 1.
24.7. $24.3 \hat{\imath}$ Suppose that $P_{0}(A)=\int_{A} \delta d P$ for all $A(\delta \geq 0)$ and that $T$ is mixing with respect to $P$ ( $T$ need not preserve $P_{0}$ ). Use (21.9) to prove

$$
P_{0}\left(T^{-n} A\right)=\int_{T^{-n} A} \delta d P \rightarrow P(A)
$$

24.8. $24.6 \uparrow$ (a) Show that

$$
\frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} a_{k}(x) \rightarrow \infty
$$

and

$$
\sqrt[n]{a_{1}(x) \cdots a_{n}(x)} \rightarrow \prod_{k=1}^{\infty}\left(1+\frac{1}{k^{2}+2 k}\right)^{(\log k) /(\log 2)}
$$

almost everywhere.
(b) Show that

$$
\frac{1}{n} \log \left(q_{n}(x)\left|x-\frac{p_{n}(x)}{q_{n}(x)}\right|\right) \rightarrow-\frac{\pi^{2}}{12 \log 2}
$$

24.9. 24.4 24.7 ↑ (a) Show that the continued-fraction transformation is mixing.
(b) Show that

$$
\lambda\left[x: T^{n} x \leq t\right] \rightarrow \frac{\log (1+t)}{\log 2}, \quad 0 \leq t \leq 1
$$

## CHAPTER5

## Convergence of Distributions

## SECTION 25. WEAK CONVERGENCE

Many of the best-known theorems in probability have to do with the asymptotic behavior of distributions. This chapter covers both general methods for deriving such theorems and specific applications. The present section concerns the general limit theory for distributions on the real line, and the methods of proof use in an essential way the order structure of the line. For the theory in $R^{k}$, see Section 29.

## Definitions

Distribution functions $F_{n}$ were defined in Section 14 to converge weakly to the distribution function $F$ if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} F_{n}(x)=F(x) \tag{25.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

for every continuity point $x$ of $F$; this is expressed by writing $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$. Examples 14.1, 14.2, and 14.3 illustrate this concept in connection with the asymptotic distribution of maxima. Example 14.4 shows the point of allowing (25.1) to fail if $F$ is discontinuous at $x$; see also Example 25.4. Theorem 25.8 and Example 25.9 show why this exemption is essential to a useful theory.

If $\mu_{n}$ and $\mu$ are the probability measures on ( $R^{1}, \mathscr{R}^{1}$ ) corresponding to $F_{n}$ and $F$, then $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$ if and only if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} \mu_{n}(A)=\mu(A) \tag{25.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

for every $A$ of the form $A=(-\infty, x]$ for which $\mu\{x\}=0$-see (20.5). In this case the distributions themselves are said to converge weakly, which is expressed by writing $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$. Thus $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$ and $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ are only different expressions of the same fact. From weak convergence it follows that (25.2) holds for many sets $A$ besides half-infinite intervals; see Theorem 25.8.

Example 25.1. Let $F_{n}$ be the distribution function corresponding to a unit mass at $n: F_{n}=I_{[n, \infty)}$. Then $\lim _{n} F_{n}(x)=0$ for every $x$, so that (25.1) is satisfied if $F(x) \equiv 0$. But $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$ does not hold, because $F$ is not a distribution function. Weak convergence is defined in this section only for functions $F_{n}$ and $F$ that rise from 0 at $-\infty$ to 1 at $+\infty$-that is, it is defined only for probability measures $\mu_{n}$ and $\mu .^{\dagger}$

Example 25.2 Poisson approximation to the binomial. Let $\mu_{n}$ be the binomial distribution (20.6) for $p=\lambda / n$ and let $\mu$ be the Poisson distribution (20.7). For nonnegative integers $k$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mu_{n}\{k\} & =\binom{n}{k}\left(\frac{\lambda}{n}\right)^{k}\left(1-\frac{\lambda}{n}\right)^{n-k} \\
& =\frac{\lambda^{k}(1-\lambda / n)^{n}}{k!} \times \frac{1}{(1-\lambda / n)^{k}} \prod_{i=0}^{k-1}\left(1-\frac{i}{n}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

if $n \geq k$. As $n \rightarrow \infty$ the second factor on the right goes to 1 for fixed $k$, and so $\mu_{n}(k) \rightarrow \mu\{k\}$; this is a special case of Theorem 23.2. By the series form of Scheffe's theorem (the corollary to Theorem 16.12), (25.2) holds for every set $A$ of nonnegative integers. Since the nonnegative integers support $\mu$ and the $\mu_{n}$, (25.2) even holds for every linear Borel set $A$. Certainly $\mu_{n}$ converges weakly to $\mu$ in this case.

Example 25.3. Let $\mu_{n}$ correspond to a mass of $n^{-1}$ at each point $k / n$, $k=0,1, \ldots, n-1$; let $\mu$ be Lebesgue measure confined to the unit interval. The corresponding distribution functions satisfy $F_{n}(x)=(\lfloor n x\rfloor+1) / n \rightarrow F(x)$ for $0 \leq x<1$, and so $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$. In this case (25.1) holds for every $x$, but (25.2) does not, as in the preceding example, hold for every Borel set $A$ : if $A$ is the set of rationals, then $\mu_{n}(A)=1$ does not converge to $\mu(A)=0$. Despite this, $\mu_{n}$ does converge weakly to $\mu$.

Example 25.4. If $\mu_{n}$ is a unit mass at $x_{n}$ and $\mu$ is a unit mass at $x$, then $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ if and only if $x_{n} \rightarrow x$. If $x_{n}>x$ for infinitely many $n$, then (25.1) fails at the discontinuity point of $F$.

## Uniform Distribution Modulo 1*

For a sequence $x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots$ of real numbers, consider the corresponding sequence of their fractional parts $\left\{x_{n}\right\}=x_{n}-\left\lfloor x_{n}\right\rfloor$. For each $n$, define a probability measure $\mu_{n}$ by

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{n}(A)=\frac{1}{n} \#\left[k: 1 \leq k \leq n,\left\{x_{k}\right\} \in A\right] ; \tag{25.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

[^12]$\mu_{n}$ has mass $n^{-1}$ at the points $\left\{x_{1}\right\}, \ldots,\left\{x_{n}\right\}$, and if several of these points coincide, the masses add. The problem is to find the weak limit of $\left\{\mu_{n}\right\}$ in number-theoretically interesting cases.

If the $\mu_{n}$ defined by (25.3) converge weakly to Lebesgue measure restricted to the unit interval, the sequence $x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots$ is said to be uniformly distributed modulo 1 . In this case every subinterval has asymptotically its proportional share of the points $\left\{x_{n}\right\}$; by Theorem 25.8 below, the same is then true of every subset whose boundary has Lebesgue measure 0 .

Theorem 25.1. For $\theta$ irrational, $\theta, 2 \theta, 3 \theta, \ldots$ is uniformly distributed modulo 1 .
Proof. Since $(n \theta)=\{n(\theta)\}, \theta$ can be assumed to lie in $[0,1]$. As in Example 24.4, map $[0,1)$ to the unit circle in the complex plane by $\phi(x)=e^{2 \pi i x}$. If $\theta$ is irrational, then $c=\phi(\theta)$ is not a root of unity, and so (p. 000) $T \omega=c \omega$ defines an ergodic transformation with respect to circular Lebesgue measure $P$. Let $\mathscr{I}$ be the class of open arcs with endpoints in some fixed countable, dense set. By the ergodic theorem, the orbit ( $T^{n} \omega$ ) of almost every $\omega$ enters every $I$ in $\mathscr{I}$ with asymptotic relative frequency $P(I)$. Fix such an $\omega$. If $I_{1} \subset J \subset I_{2}$, where $J$ is a closed arc and $I_{1}, I_{2}$ are in $\mathscr{I}$, then the upper and lower limits of $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} I_{J}\left(T^{k-1} \omega\right)$ are between $P\left(I_{1}\right)$ and $P\left(I_{2}\right)$, and therefore the limit exists and equals $P(J)$. Since the orbits and the arcs are rotations of one another, every orbit enters every closed arc $J$ with frequency $P(J)$. This is true in particular of the orbit ( $c^{n}$ ) of 1 .

Now carry all this back to $[0,1)$ by $\phi^{-1}$ : For every $x$ in $[0,1),\{n \theta\}=\phi^{-1}\left(c^{n}\right)$ lies in $[0, x]$ with asymptotic relative frequency $x$.

For a simple proof by Fourier series, see Example 26.3.

## Convergence in Distribution

Let $X_{n}$ and $X$ be random variables with respective distribution functions $F_{n}$ and $F$. If $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$, then $X_{n}$ is said to converge in distribution or in law to $X$, written $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$. This dual use of the double arrow will cause no confusion. Because of the defining conditions (25.1) and (25.2), $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ if and only if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} P\left[X_{n} \leq x\right]=P[X \leq x] \tag{25.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

for every $x$ such that $P[X=x]=0$.

Example 25.5. Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be independent random variables, each with the exponential distribution: $P\left[X_{n} \geq x\right]=e^{-\alpha x}, x \geq 0$. Put $M_{n}= \max \left\{X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right\}$ and $b_{n}=\alpha^{-1} \log n$. The relation (14.9), established in Example 14.1, can be restated as $P\left[M_{n}-b_{n} \leq x\right] \rightarrow e^{-e^{-\alpha x}}$. If $X$ is any random variable with distribution function $e^{-e^{-\alpha x}}$, this can be written $M_{n}-b_{n} \Rightarrow X$.

One is usually interested in proving weak convergence of the distributions of some given sequence of random variables, such as the $M_{n}-b_{n}$ in this example, and the result is often most clearly expressed in terms of the random variables themselves rather than in terms of their distributions or
distribution functions. Although the $M_{n}-b_{n}$ here arise naturally from the problem at hand, the random variable $X$ is simply constructed to make it possible to express the asymptotic relation compactly by $M_{n}-b_{n} \Rightarrow X$. Recall that by Theorem 14.1 there does exist a random variable for any prescribed distribution.

Example 25.6. For each $n$, let $\Omega_{n}$ be the space of $n$-tuples of 0 's and 1 's, let $\mathscr{F}_{n}$ consist of all subsets of $\Omega_{n}$, and let $P_{n}$ assign probability $(\lambda / n)^{k}(1- \lambda / n)^{n-k}$ to each $\omega$ consisting of $k$ 1's and $n-k$ 0's. Let $X_{n}(\omega)$ be the number of 1 's in $\omega$; then $X_{n}$, a random variable on ( $\Omega_{n}, \mathscr{F}_{n}, P_{n}$ ), represents the number of successes in $n$ Bernoulli trials having probability $\lambda / n$ of success at each.

Let $X$ be a random variable, on some ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ), having the Poisson distribution with parameter $\lambda$. According to Example 25.2, $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$.

As this example shows, the random variables $X_{n}$ may be defined on entirely different probability spaces. To allow for this possibility, the $P$ on the left in (25.4) really should be written $P_{n}$. Suppressing the $n$ causes no confusion if it is understood that $P$ refers to whatever probability space it is that $X_{n}$ is defined on; the underlying probability space enters into the definition only via the distribution $\mu_{n}$ it induces on the line. Any instance of $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$ or of $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ can be rewritten in terms of convergence in distribution: There exist random variables $X_{n}$ and $X$ (on some probability spaces) with distribution functions $F_{n}$ and $F$, and $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$ and $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ express the same fact.

## Convergence in Probability

Suppose that $X, X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are random variables all defined on the same probability space $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$. If $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ with probability 1 , then $P\left[\left|X_{n}-X\right| \geq \epsilon\right.$ i.o. $]=0$ for $\epsilon>0$, and hence

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} P\left[\left|X_{n}-X\right|>\epsilon\right]=0 \tag{25.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

by Theorem 4.1. Thus there is convergence in probability $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} X$; see Theorems 5.2 and 20.5.

Suppose that (25.5) holds for each positive $\epsilon$. Now $P[X \leq x-\epsilon]-P\left[\mid X_{n}-\right. X \mid \geq \epsilon] \leq P\left[X_{n} \leq x\right] \leq P[X \leq x+\epsilon]+P\left[\left|X_{n}-x\right| \geq \epsilon\right]$; letting $n$ tend to $\infty$ and then letting $\epsilon$ tend to 0 shows that $P[X<x] \leq \liminf _{n} P\left[X_{n} \leq x\right] \leq \limsup _{n} P\left[X_{n} \leq x\right] \leq P[X \leq x]$. Thus $P\left[X_{n} \leq x\right] \rightarrow P[X \leq x]$ if $P[X=x]=0$, and so $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ :

Theorem 25.2. Suppose that $X_{n}$ and $X$ are random variables on the same probability space. If $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ with probability 1 , then $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} X$. If $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} X$, then $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$.

Of the two implications in this theorem, neither converse holds. Because of Example 5.4, convergence in probability does not imply convergence with probability 1 . Neither does convergence in distribution imply convergence in probability: if $X$ and $Y$ are independent and assume the values 0 and 1 with probability $\frac{1}{2}$ each, and if $X_{n}=Y$, then $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$, but $X_{n} \rightarrow_{p} X$ cannot hold because $P[|X-Y|]=1=\frac{1}{2}$. What is more, (25.5) is impossible if $X$ and the $X_{n}$ are defined on different probability spaces, as may happen in the case of convergence in distribution.

Although (25.5) in general makes no sense unless $X$ and the $X_{n}$ are defined on the same probability space, suppose that $X$ is replaced by a constant real number $a$-that is, suppose that $X(\omega) \equiv a$. Then (25.5) becomes

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} P\left[\left|X_{n}-a\right| \geq \epsilon\right]=0 \tag{25.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

and this condition makes sense even if the space of $X_{n}$ does vary with $n$. Now $a$ can be regarded as a random variable (on any probability space at all), and it is easy to show that (25.6) implies that $X_{n} \Rightarrow a$ : Put $\epsilon=|x-a|$; if $x>a$, then $P\left[X_{n} \leq x\right] \geq P\left[\left|X_{n}-a\right|<\epsilon\right] \rightarrow 1$, and if $x<a$, then $P\left[X_{n} \leq x\right] \leq P\left[\left|X_{n}-a\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \rightarrow 0$. If $a$ is regarded as a random variable, its distribution function is 0 for $x<a$ and 1 for $x \geq a$. Thus (25.6) implies that the distribution function of $X_{n}$ converges weakly to that of $a$.

Suppose, on the other hand, that $X_{n} \Rightarrow a$. Then $P\left[\left|X_{n}-a\right|>\epsilon\right] \leq P\left[X_{n} \leq\right. a-\epsilon]+1-P\left[X_{n} \leq a+\epsilon\right] \rightarrow 0$, so that (25.6) holds:

Theorem 25.3. The condition (25.6) hoids for all positive $\epsilon$ if and only if $X_{n} \Rightarrow a$, that is, if and only if

$$
\lim _{n} P\left[X_{n} \leq x\right]= \begin{cases}0 & \text { if } x<a \\ 1 & \text { if } x>a\end{cases}
$$

If (25.6) holds for all positive $\epsilon, X_{n}$ may be said to converge to a in probability. As this does not require that the $X_{n}$ be defined on the same space, it is not really a special case of convergence in probability as defined by (25.5). Convergence in probability in this new sense will be denoted $X_{n} \Rightarrow a$, in accordance with the theorem just proved.

Example 14.4 restates the weak law of large numbers in terms of this concept. Indeed, if $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent, identically distributed random variables with finite mean $m$, and if $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$, the weak law of large numbers is the assertion $n^{-1} S_{n} \Rightarrow m$. Example 6.3 provides another illustration: If $S_{n}$ is the number of cycles in a random permutation on $n$ letters, then $S_{n} / \log n \Rightarrow 1$.

Example 25.7. Suppose that $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ and $\delta_{n} \rightarrow 0$. Given $\epsilon$ and $\eta$, choose $x$ so that $P[|X| \geq x]<\eta$ and $P[X= \pm x]=0$, and then choose $n_{0}$ so that $n \geq n_{0}$ implies that $\left|\delta_{n}\right|<\epsilon / x$ and $\left|P\left[X_{n} \leq y\right]-P[X \leq y]\right|<\eta$ for $y= \pm x$. Then $P\left[\left|\delta_{n} X_{n}\right| \geq \epsilon\right]<3 \eta$ for $n \geq n_{0}$. Thus $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ and $\delta_{n} \rightarrow 0$ imply that $\delta_{n} X_{n} \Rightarrow 0$, a restatement of Lemma 2 of Section 14 (p. 193).

The asymptotic properties of a random variable should remain unaffected if it is altered by the addition of a random variable that goes to 0 in probability. Let $\left(X_{n}, Y_{n}\right)$ be a two-dimensional random vector.

Theorem 25.4. If $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ and $X_{n}-Y_{n} \Rightarrow 0$, then $Y_{n} \Rightarrow X$.
Proof. Suppose that $y^{\prime}<x<y^{\prime \prime}$ and $P\left[X=y^{\prime}\right]=P\left[X=y^{\prime \prime}\right]=0$. If $y^{\prime}< x-\epsilon<x<x+\epsilon<y^{\prime \prime}$, then

$$
\begin{align*}
P\left[X_{n} \leq y^{\prime}\right]-P\left[\left|X_{n}-Y_{n}\right| \geq \epsilon\right] & \leq P\left[Y_{n} \leq x\right]  \tag{25.7}\\
& \leq P\left[X_{n} \leq y^{\prime \prime}\right]+P\left[\left|X_{n}-Y_{n}\right| \geq \epsilon\right]
\end{align*}
$$

Since $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$, letting $n \rightarrow \infty$ gives

$$
\begin{align*}
P\left[X \leq y^{\prime}\right] & \leq \lim \inf _{n} P\left[Y_{n} \leq x\right]  \tag{25.8}\\
& \leq \lim \sup _{n} P\left[Y_{n} \leq x\right] \leq P\left[X \leq y^{\prime \prime}\right]
\end{align*}
$$

Since $P[X=y]=0$ for all but countably many $y$, if $P[X=x]=0$, then $y^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime \prime}$ can further be chosen so that $P\left[X \leq y^{\prime}\right]$ and $P\left[X \leq y^{\prime \prime}\right]$ are arbitrarily near $P[X \leq x]$; hence $P\left[Y_{n} \leq x\right] \rightarrow P[X \leq x]$.

Theorem 25.4 has a useful extension. Suppose that ( $X_{n}^{(u)}, Y_{n}$ ) is a two-dimensional random vector.

Theorem 25.5. If, for each $u, X_{n}^{(u)} \Rightarrow X^{(u)}$ as $n \rightarrow \infty$, if $X^{(u)} \Rightarrow X$ as $u \rightarrow \infty$, and if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{u} \lim \sup _{n} P\left[\left|X_{n}^{(u)}-Y_{n}\right| \geq \epsilon\right]=0 \tag{25.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

for positive $\epsilon$, then $Y_{n} \Rightarrow X$.
Proof. Replace $X_{n}$ by $X_{n}^{(u)}$ in (25.7). If $P\left[X=y^{\prime}\right]=0 \equiv P\left[X^{(u)}=y^{\prime}\right]$ and $P[X \left.=y^{\prime \prime}\right]=0 \equiv P\left[X^{(u)}=y^{\prime \prime}\right]$, letting $n \rightarrow \infty$ and then $u \rightarrow \infty$ gives (25.8) once again. Since $P[X=y]=0 \equiv P\left[X^{(u)}=y\right]$ for all but countably many $y$, the proof can be completed as before.

## Fundamental Theorems

Some of the fundamental properties of weak convergence were established in Section 14. It was shown there that a sequence cannot have two distinct weak limits: If $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$ and $F_{n} \Rightarrow G$, then $F=G$. The proof is simple: The hypothesis implies that $F$ and $G$ agree at their common points of continuity, hence at all but countably many points, and hence by right continuity at all points. Another simple fact is this: If $\lim _{n} F_{n}(d)=F(d)$ for $d$ in a set $D$ dense in $R^{1}$, then $F_{n} \Rightarrow F$. Indeed, if $F$ is continuous at $x$, there are in $D$ points $d^{\prime}$ and $d^{\prime \prime}$ such that $d^{\prime}<x<d^{\prime \prime}$ and $F\left(d^{\prime \prime}\right)-F\left(d^{\prime}\right)<\epsilon$, and it follows that the limits superior and inferior of $F_{n}(x)$ are within $\epsilon$ of $F(x)$.

For any probability measure on $\left(R^{1}, \mathscr{R}^{1}\right)$ there is on some probability space a random variable having that measure as its distribution. Therefore, for probability measures satisfying $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$, there exist random variables $Y_{n}$ and $Y$ having these measures as distributions and satisfying $Y_{n} \Rightarrow Y$. According to the following theorem, the $Y_{n}$ and $Y$ can be constructed on the same probability space, and even in such a way that $Y_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow Y(\omega)$ for every $\omega$-a condition much stronger than $Y_{n} \rightarrow Y$. This result, Skorohod's theorem, makes possible very simple and transparent proofs of many important facts.

Theorem 25.6. Suppose that $\mu_{n}$ and $\mu$ are probability measures on ( $R^{1}, \mathscr{P}^{1}$ ) and $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$. There exist random variables $Y_{n}$ and $Y$ on a common probability space $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ such that $Y_{n}$ has distribution $\mu_{n}, Y$ has distribution $\mu$, and $Y_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow Y(\omega)$ for each $\omega$.

Proof. For the probability space $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$, take $\Omega=(0,1)$, let $\mathscr{F}$ consist of the Borel subsets of $(0,1)$, and for $P(A)$ take the Lebesgue measure of $A$.

The construction is related to that in the proofs of Theorems 14.1 and 20.4. Consider the distribution functions $F_{n}$ and $F$ corresponding to $\mu_{n}$ and $\mu$. For $0<\omega<1$, put $Y_{n}(\omega)=\inf \left[x: \omega \leq F_{n}(x)\right]$ and $Y(\omega)=\inf [x: \omega \leq F(x)]$. Since $\omega \leq F_{n}(x)$ if and only if $Y_{n}(\omega) \leq x$ (see the argument following (14.5)), $P\left[\omega: Y_{n}(\omega) \leq x\right]=P\left[\omega: \omega \leq F_{n}(x)\right]=F_{n}(x)$. Thus $Y_{n}$ has distribution function $F_{n}$; similarly, $Y$ has distribution function $F$.

It remains to show that $Y_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow Y(\omega)$. The idea is that $Y_{n}$ and $Y$ are essentially inverse functions to $F_{n}$ and $F$; if the direct functions converge, so must the inverses.

Suppose that $0<\omega<1$. Given $\epsilon$, choose $x$ so that $Y(\omega)-\epsilon<x<Y(\omega)$ and $\mu\{x\}=0$. Then $F(x)<\omega ; F_{n}(x) \rightarrow F(x)$ now implies that, for $n$ large enough, $F_{n}(x)<\omega$ and hence $Y(\omega)-\epsilon<x<Y_{n}(\omega)$. Thus $\liminf _{n} Y_{n}(\omega) \geq Y(\omega)$. If $\omega<\omega^{\prime}$ and $\epsilon$ is positive, choose a $y$ for which $Y\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)<y<Y\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)+\epsilon$ and $\mu\{y\}=0$. Now $\omega<\omega^{\prime} \leq F\left(Y\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)\right) \leq F(y)$, and so, for $n$ large enough, $\omega \leq F_{n}(y)$ and hence $Y_{n}(\omega) \leq y<Y\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)+\epsilon$. Thus $\limsup _{n} Y_{n}(\omega) \leq Y\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)$ if $\omega<\omega^{\prime}$. Therefore, $Y_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow Y(\omega)$ if $Y$ is continuous at $\omega$.

Since $Y$ is nondecreasing on $(0,1)$, it has at most countably many discontinuities. At discontinuity points $\omega$ of $Y$, redefine $Y_{n}(\omega)=Y(\omega)=0$. With this change, $Y_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow Y(\omega)$ for every $\omega$. Since $Y$ and the $Y_{n}$ have been altered only on a set of Lebesgue measure 0 , their distributions are still $\mu_{n}$ and $\mu$.

Note that this proof uses the order structure of the real line in an essential way. The proof of the corresponding result in $R^{k}$ is more complicated.

The following mapping theorem is of very frequent use.
Theorem 25.7. Suppose that $h: R^{1} \rightarrow R^{1}$ is measurable and that the set $D_{h}$ of its discontinuities is measurable. ${ }^{\dagger}$ If $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ and $\mu\left(D_{n}\right)=0$, then $\mu_{n} h^{-i} \Rightarrow \mu h^{-1}$.

Recall (see (13.7)) that $\mu h^{-1}$ has value $\mu\left(h^{-1} A\right)$ at $A$.
Proof. Consider the random variables $Y_{n}$ and $Y$ of Theorem 25.6. Since $Y_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow Y(\omega)$, if $Y(\omega) \notin D_{h}$ then $h\left(Y_{n}(\omega)\right) \rightarrow h(Y(\omega))$. Since $P[\omega: Y(\omega) \in \left.D_{h}\right]=\mu\left(D_{h}\right)=0$, it follows that $h\left(Y_{n}(\omega)\right) \rightarrow h(Y(\omega))$ with probability 1 . Hence $\dot{h}\left(Y_{n}\right) \Rightarrow h(Y)$ by Theorem 25.2. Since $P[h(Y) \in A]=P\left[Y \in h^{-1} A\right]=\mu\left(h^{-1} A\right)$, $h(Y)$ has distribution $\mu h^{-1}$; similarly, $h\left(Y_{n}\right)$ has distribution $\mu_{n} h^{-1}$. Thus $h\left(Y_{n}\right) \Rightarrow h(Y)$ is the same thing as $\mu_{n} h^{-1} \Rightarrow \mu h^{-1}$.

Because of the definition of convergence in distribution, this result has an equivalent statement in terms of random variables:

Corollary 1. If $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ and $P\left[X \in D_{h}\right]=0$, then $h\left(X_{n}\right) \Rightarrow h(X)$.
Take $X \equiv a$ :
Corollary 2. If $X_{n} \Rightarrow a$ and $h$ is continuous at $a$, then $h\left(X_{n}\right) \Rightarrow h(a)$.
Example 25.8. From $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ it follows directly by the theorem that $a X_{n}+b \Rightarrow a X+b$. Suppose also that $a_{n} \rightarrow a$ and $b_{n} \rightarrow b$. Then $\left(a_{n}-a\right) X_{n} \Rightarrow 0$ by Example 25.7, and so $\left(a_{n} X_{n}+b_{n}\right)-\left(a X_{n}+b\right) \Rightarrow 0$. And now $a_{n} X_{n}+b_{n} \Rightarrow a X+b$ follows by Theorem 25.4: If $X_{n} \Rightarrow X, a_{n} \rightarrow a$, and $b_{n} \rightarrow b$, then $a_{n} X_{n}+b_{n} \Rightarrow a X+b$. This fact was stated and proved differently in Section 14 -see Lemma 1 on p. 193.

By definition, $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ means that the corresponding distribution functions converge weakly. The following theorem characterizes weak convergence

[^13]without reference to distribution functions. The boundary $\partial A$ of $A$ consists of the points that are limits of sequences in $A$ and are also limits of sequences in $A^{c}$; alternatively, $\partial A$ is the closure of $A$ minus its interior. A set $A$ is a $\mu$-continuity set if it is a Borel set and $\mu(\partial A)=0$.

Theorem 25.8. The following three conditions are equivalent.
(i) $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$;
(ii) $\int f d \mu_{n} \rightarrow \int f d \mu$ for every bounded, continuous real function $f$;
(iii) $\mu_{n}(A) \rightarrow \mu(A)$ for every $\mu$-continuity set $A$.

Proof. Suppose that $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$, and consider the random variables $Y_{n}$ and $Y$ of Theorem 25.6. Suppose that $f$ is a bounded function such that $\mu\left(D_{f}\right)=0$, where $D_{f}$ is the set of points of discontinuity of $F$. From $P\left[Y \in D_{f}\right]=\mu\left(D_{f}\right)=0$ it follows that $f\left(Y_{n}\right) \rightarrow f(Y)$ with probabiiity 1 , and so by change of variable (see (21.1)) and the bounded convergence theorem, $\int f d \mu_{n}=E\left[f\left(Y_{n}\right)\right] \rightarrow E[f(Y)]=\int f d \mu$. Thus $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ and $\mu\left(D_{f}\right)=0$ together imply that $\int f d \mu_{n} \rightarrow \int f d \mu$ if $f$ is bounded. In particular, (i) implies (ii). Further, if $f=I_{A}$, then $D_{f}=\partial A$, and from $\mu(\partial A)=0$ and $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ follows $\mu_{n}(A)=\int f d \mu_{n} \rightarrow \int f d \mu=\mu(A)$. Thus (i) also implies (iii).

Since $\partial(-\infty, x]=\{x\}$, obviously (iii) implies (i). It therefore remains only to deduce $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ from (ii). Consider the corresponding distribution functions. Suppose that $x<y$, and let $f(t)$ be 1 for $t \leq x, 0$ for $t \geq y$, and interpolate linearly on $[x, y]: f(t)=(y-t) /(y-x)$ for $x \leq t \leq y$. Since $F_{n}(x) \leq \int f d \mu_{n}$ and $\int f d \mu \leq F(y)$, it follows from (ii) that $\limsup _{n} F_{n}(x) \leq F(y)$; letting $y \downarrow x$ shows that $\limsup _{n} F_{n}(x) \leq F(x)$. Similarly, $F(u) \leq \liminf _{n} F_{n}(x)$ for $u<x$ and hence $F(x-) \leq \liminf _{n} F_{n}(x)$. This implies convergence at continuity points. $\square$

The function $f$ in this last part of the proof is uniformly continuous. Hence $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ follows if $\int f d \mu_{n} \rightarrow \int f d \mu$ for every bounded and uniformly continuous $f$.

Example 25.9. The distributions in Example 25.3 satisfy $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$, but $\mu_{n}(A)$ does not converge to $\mu(A)$ if $A$ is the set of rationals. Hence this $A$ cannot be a $\mu$-continuity set; in fact, of course, $\partial A=R^{1}$. $\square$

The concept of weak convergence would be nearly useless if (25.2) were not allowed to fail when $\mu(\partial A)>0$. Since $F(x)-F(x-)=\mu\{x\}= \mu(\partial(-\infty, x])$, it is therefore natural in the original definition to allow (25.1) to fail when $x$ is not a continuity point of $F$.

## Helly's Theorem

One of the most frequently used results in analysis is the Helly selection theorem:

Theorem 25.9. For every sequence $\left\{F_{n}\right\}$ of distribution functions there exists a subsequence $\left\{F_{n_{k}}\right\}$ and a nondecreasing, right-continuous function $F$ such that $\lim _{k} F_{n_{k}}(x)=F(x)$ at continuity points $x$ of $F$.

Proof. An application of the diagonal method [A14] gives a sequence $\left\{n_{k}\right\}$ of integers along which the limit $G(r)=\lim _{k} F_{n_{k}}(r)$ exists for every rational $r$. Define $F(x)=\inf [G(r) ; x<r]$. Clearly $F$ is nondecreasing.

To each $x$ and $\epsilon$ there is an $r$ for which $x<r$ and $G(r)<F(x)+\epsilon$. If $x \leq y<r$, then $F(y) \leq G(r)<F(x)+\epsilon$. Hence $F$ is continuous from the right.

If $F$ is continuous at $x$, choose $y<x$ so that $F(x)-\epsilon<F(y)$; now choose rational $r$ and $s$ so that $y<r<x<s$ and $G(s)<F(x)+\epsilon$. From $F(x)-\epsilon< G(r) \leq G(s)<F(x)+\epsilon$ and $F_{n}(r) \leq F_{n}(x) \leq F_{n}(s)$ it follows that as $k$ goes to infinity $F_{n_{k}}(x)$ has limits superior and inferior within $\epsilon$ of $F(x)$.

The $F$ in this theorem necessarily satisfies $0 \leq F(x) \leq 1$. But $F$ need not be a distribution function: if $F_{n}$ has a unit jump at $n$, for example, $F(x) \equiv 0$ is the only possibility. It is important to have a condition which ensures that for some subsequence the limit $F$ is a distribution function.

A sequence of probability measures $\mu_{n}$ on $\left(R^{1}, \mathscr{R}^{1}\right)$ is said to be tight if for each $\epsilon$ there exists a finite interval $(a, b]$ such that $\mu_{n}(a, b]>1-\epsilon$ for all $n$. In terms of the corresponding distribution functions $F_{n}$, the condition is that for each $\epsilon$ there exist $x$ and $y$ such that $F_{n}(x)<\epsilon$ and $F_{n}(y)>1-\epsilon$ for all $n$. If $\mu_{n}$ is a unit mass at $n,\left\{\mu_{n}\right\}$ is not tight in this sense-the mass of $\mu_{n}$ "escapes to infinity." Tightness is a condition preventing this escape of mass.

Theorem 25.10. Tightness is a necessary and sufficient condition that for every subsequence $\left\{\mu_{n_{k}}\right\}$ there exist a further subsequence $\left(\mu_{n_{k(1)}}\right.$ ) and a probability measure $\mu$ such that $\mu_{n_{k(j)}} \Rightarrow \mu$ as $j \rightarrow \infty$.

Only the sufficiency of the condition in this theorem is used in what follows.

Proof. Sufficiency. Apply Helly's theorem to the subsequence $\left\{F_{n_{k}}\right\}$ of corresponding distribution functions. There exists a further subsequence $\left\{F_{n_{k(j)}}\right\}$ such that $\lim _{j} F_{n_{k(j)}}(x)=F(x)$ at continuity points of $F$, where $F$ is nondecreasing and right-continuous. There exists by Theorem 12.4 a measure $\mu$ on $\left(R^{1}, \mathscr{P}^{1}\right)$ such that $\mu(a, b]=F(b)-F(a)$. Given $\epsilon$, choose $a$ and $b$ so that $\mu_{n}(a, b]>1-\epsilon$ for all $n$, which is possible by tightness. By decreasing $a$
and increasing $b$, one can ensure that they are continuity points of $F$. But then $\mu(a, b] \geq 1-\epsilon$. Therefore, $\mu$ is a probability measure, and of course $\mu_{n_{k(j)}} \Rightarrow \mu$.

Necessity. If $\left\{\mu_{n}\right\}$ is not tight, there exists a positive $\epsilon$ such that for each finite interval $(a, b], \mu_{n}(a, b] \leq 1-\epsilon$ for some $n$. Choose $n_{k}$ so that $\mu_{n_{k}}(-k, k] \leq 1-\epsilon$. Suppose that some subsequence $\left\{\mu_{n_{k(j)}}\right\}$ of $\left\{\mu_{n_{k}}\right\}$ were to converge weakly to some probability measure $\mu$. Choose $(a, b]$ so that $\mu\{a\}=\mu\{b\}=0$ and $\mu(a, b]>1-\epsilon$. For large enough $j,(a, b] \subset (-k(j), k(j)]$, and so $1-\epsilon \geq \mu_{n_{k(j)}}(-k(j), k(j)] \geq \mu_{n_{k(j)}}(a, b] \rightarrow \mu(a, b]$. Thus $\mu(a, b] \leq 1-\epsilon$, a contradiction. $\square$

Corollary. If $\left\{\mu_{n}\right\}$ is a tight sequence of probability measures, and if each subsequence that converges weakly at all converges weakly to the probability measure $\mu$, then $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$.

Proof. By the theorem, each subsequence $\left\{\mu_{n_{k}}\right\}$ contains a further subsequence $\left\{\mu_{n_{k(j)}}\right\}$ converging weakly $(j \rightarrow \infty)$ to some limit, and that limit must by hypothesis be $\mu$. Thus every subsequence $\left\{\mu_{n_{k}}\right\}$ contains a further subsequence $\left\{\mu_{n_{k(t)}}\right\}$ converging weakly to $\mu$.

Suppose that $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ is false. Then there exists some $x$ such that $\mu\{x\}=0$ but $\mu_{n}(-\infty, x]$ does not converge to $\mu(-\infty, x]$. But then there exists a positive $\epsilon$ such that $\left|\mu_{n_{k}}(-\infty, x]-\mu(-\infty, x]\right| \geq \epsilon$ for an infinite sequence $\left\{n_{k}\right\}$ of integers, and no subsequence of $\left\{\mu_{n_{k}}\right\}$ can converge weakly to $\mu$. This contradiction shows that $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$. $\square$

If $\mu_{n}$ is a unit mass at $x_{n}$, then $\left\{\mu_{n}\right\}$ is tight if and only if $\left.x_{n}\right\}$ is bounded. The theorem above and its corollary reduce in this case to standard facts about real line; see Example 25.4 and A10: tightness of sequences of probability measures is analogous to boundedness of sequences of real numbers.

Example 25.10. Let $\mu_{n}$ be the normal distribution with mean $m_{n}$ and variance $\sigma_{n}^{2}$. If $m_{n}$ and $\sigma_{n}^{2}$ are bounded, then the second moment of $\mu_{n}$ is bounded, and it follows by Markov's inequality (21.12) that $\left\{\mu_{n}\right\}$ is tight. The conclusion of Theorem 25.10 can also be checked directly: If $\left\{n_{k(j)}\right\}$ is chosen so that $\lim _{j} m_{n_{k(j)}}=m$ and $\lim _{j} \sigma_{n_{k(j)}}^{2}=\sigma^{2}$, then $\mu_{n_{k(j)}} \Rightarrow \mu$, where $\mu$ is normal with mean $m$ and variance $\sigma^{2}$ (a unit mass at $m$ if $\sigma^{2}=0$ ).

If $m_{n}>b$, then $\mu_{n}(b, \infty) \geq \frac{1}{2}$; if $m_{n}<a$, then $\mu_{n}(-\infty, a] \geq \frac{1}{2}$. Hence $\left\{\mu_{n}\right\}$ cannot be tight if $m_{n}$ is unbounded. If $m_{n}$ is bounded, say by $K$, then $\mu_{n}(-\infty, a] \geq \nu\left(-\infty,(a-K) \sigma_{n}^{-1}\right]$, where $\nu$ is the standard normal distribution. If $\sigma_{n}$ is unbounded, then $\nu\left(-\infty,(a-K) \sigma_{n}^{-1}\right] \rightarrow \frac{1}{2}$ along some subsequence, and $\left\{\mu_{n}\right\}$ cannot be tight. Thus a sequence of normal distributions is tight if and only if the means and variances are bounded. $\square$

## Integration to the Limit

Theorem 25.11. If $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$, then $E[|X|] \leq \liminf _{n} E\left[\left|X_{n}\right|\right]$.
Proof, Apply Skorohod's Theorem 25.6 to the distributions of $X_{n}$ and $X$ : There exist on a common probability space random variables $Y_{n}$ and $Y$ such that $Y=\lim _{n} Y_{n}$ with probability $1, Y_{n}$ has the distribution of $X_{n}$, and $Y$ has the distribution of $X$. By Fatou's lemma, $E[|Y|] \leq \liminf _{n} E\left[\left|Y_{n}\right|\right]$. Since $|X|$ and $|Y|$ have the same distribution, they have the same expected value (see (21.6)), and similarly for $\left|X_{n}\right|$ and $\left|Y_{n}\right|$. $\square$

The random variables $X_{n}$ are said to be uniformly integrable if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{\alpha \rightarrow \infty} \sup _{n} \int_{\left[\left|X_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]}\left|X_{n}\right| d P=0 \tag{25.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

see (16.21). This implies (see (16.22)) that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sup _{n} E\left[\left|X_{n}\right|\right]<\infty . \tag{25.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

Theorem 25.12. If $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ and the $X_{n}$ are uniformly integrable, then $X$ is integrable and

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[X_{n}\right] \rightarrow E[X] . \tag{25.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Construct random variables $Y_{n}$ and $Y$ as in the preceding proof, Since $Y_{n} \rightarrow Y$ with probability 1 and the $Y_{n}$ are uniformly integrable in the sense of (16.21), $E\left[X_{n}\right]=E\left[Y_{n}\right] \rightarrow E[Y]=E[X]$ by Theorem 16.14. $\square$

If $\sup _{n} E\left[\left|X_{n}\right|^{1+\epsilon}\right]<\infty$ for some positive $\epsilon$, then the $X_{n}$ are uniformly integrable because

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{\left[\left|X_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]}\left|X_{n}\right| d P \leq \frac{1}{\alpha^{\epsilon}} E\left[\left|X_{n}\right|^{1+\epsilon}\right] . \tag{25.13}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ implies that $X_{n}^{r} \Rightarrow X^{r}$ by Theorem 25.7, there is the following consequence of the theorem.

Corollary. Let $r$ be a positive integer. If $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ and $\sup _{n} E\left[\left|X_{n}\right|^{r+\epsilon}\right]<\infty$, where $\epsilon>0$, then $E\left[|X|^{r}\right]<\infty$ and $E\left[X_{n}^{r}\right] \rightarrow E\left[X^{r}\right]$.

The $X_{n}$ are also uniformly integrable if there is an integrable random variable $Z$ such that $P\left[\left|X_{n}\right| \geq t\right] \leq P[|Z| \geq t]$ for $t>0$, because then (21.10)
gives

$$
\int_{\left[\left|X_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]}\left|X_{n}\right| d P \leq \int_{[|Z| \geq \alpha]}|Z| d P .
$$

From this the dominated convergence theorem follows again.

## PROBLEMS

25.1. (a) Show by example that distribution functions having densities can converge weakly even if the densities do not converge: Hint: Consider $f_{n}(x)=1+ \cos 2 \pi n x$ on $[0,1]$.
(b) Let $f_{n}$ be $2^{n}$ times the indicator of the set of $x$ in the unit interval for which $d_{n+1}(x)=\cdots=d_{2 n}(x)=0$, where $d_{k}(x)$ is the $k$ th dyadic digit. Show that $f_{n}(x) \rightarrow 0$ except on a set of Lebesgue measure 0 ; on this exceptional set, redefine $f_{n}(x)=0$ for all $n$, so that $f_{n}(x) \rightarrow 0$ everywhere. Show that the distributions corresponding to these densities converge weakly to Lebesgue measure confined to the unit interval.
(c) Show that distributions with densities can converge weakly to a limit that has no density (even to a unit mass).
(d) Show that discrete distributions can converge weakly to a distribution that has a density.
(e) Construct an example, like that of Example 25.3, in which $\mu_{n}(A) \rightarrow \mu(A)$ fails but in which all the measures come from continuous densities on $[0,1]$.
25.2. $14.8 \uparrow$ Give a simple proof of the Gilvenko-Cantelli theorem (Theorem 20.6) under the extra hypothesis that $F$ is continuous.
25.3. Initial digits. (a) Show that the first significant digit of a positive number $x$ is $d$ (in the scale of 10) if and only if $\left\{\log _{10} x\right\}$ lies between $\log _{10} d$ and $\log _{10}(d+1)$, $d=1, \ldots, 9$, where the braces denote fractional part.
(b) For positive numbers $x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots$, let $N_{r}(d)$ be the number among the first $n$ that have initial digit $d$. Show that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} \frac{1}{n} N_{n}(d)=\log _{10}(d+1)-\log _{10} d, \quad d=1, \ldots, 9 \tag{25.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

if the sequence $\log _{10} x_{n}, n=1,2, \ldots$, is uniformly distributed modulo 1 . This is true, for example, of $x_{n}=\vartheta^{n}$ if $\log _{10} \vartheta$ is irrational.
(c) Let $D_{n}$ be the first significant digit of a positive random variable $X_{n}$. Show that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} P\left[D_{n}=d\right]=\log _{10}(d+1)-\log _{10} d, \quad d=1, \ldots, 9, \tag{25.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

if $\left\{\log _{10} X_{n}\right\} \Rightarrow U$, where $U$ is uniformly distributed over the unit interval.
25.4. Show that for each probability measure $\mu$ on the line there exist probability measures $\mu_{n}$ with finite support such that $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$. Show further that $\mu_{n}\{x\}$ can
be taken rational and that each point in the support can be taken rational. Thus there exists a countable set of probability measures such that every $\mu$ is the weak limit of some sequence from the set. The space of distribution functions is thus separable in the Lévy metric (see Problem 14.5).
25.5. Show that (25.5) implies that $P\left([X \leq x] \Delta\left[X_{n} \leq x\right]\right) \rightarrow 0$ if $P[X=x]=0$.
25.6. For arbitrary random variables $X_{n}$ there exist positive constants $a_{n}$ such that $a_{n} X_{n} \Rightarrow 0$.
25.7. Generalize Example 25.8 by showing for three-dimensional random vectors ( $A_{n}, B_{n}, X_{n}$ ) and constants $a$ and $b, a \geq 0$, that, if $A_{n} \Rightarrow a, B_{n} \Rightarrow b$, and $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$, then $A_{n} X_{n}+B_{n} \Rightarrow a X+b$ Hint: First show that if $Y_{n} \Rightarrow Y$ and $D_{n} \Rightarrow 0$, then $D_{n} Y_{n} \Rightarrow 0$.
25.8. Suppose that $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$ and that $h_{n}$ and $h$ are Borel functions. Let $E$ be the set of $x$ for which $h_{n} x_{n} \rightarrow h x$ fails for some sequence $x_{n} \rightarrow x$. Suppose that $E \in \mathscr{R}^{1}$ and $P[X \in E]=0$. Show that $h_{n} X_{n} \Rightarrow h X$.
25.9. Suppose that the distributions of random variables $X_{n}$ and $X$ have densities $f_{n}$ and $f$. Show that if $f_{n}(x) \rightarrow f(x)$ for $x$ outside a set of Lebesgue measure 0 , then $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$.
25.10. $\uparrow$ Suppose that $X_{n}$ assumes as values $\gamma_{n}+k \delta_{n}, k=0, \pm 1, \ldots$, where $\delta_{n}>0$. Suppose that $\delta_{n} \rightarrow 0$ and that, if $k_{n}$ is an integer varying with $n$ in such a way that $\gamma_{n}+k_{n} \delta_{n} \rightarrow x$, then $P\left[X_{n}=\gamma_{n}+k_{n} \delta_{n}\right] \delta_{n}^{-1} \rightarrow f(x)$, where $f$ is the density of a random variable $X$. Show that $X_{n} \Rightarrow X$.
25.11. $\uparrow$ Let $S_{n}$ have the binemial distribution with parameters $n$ and $p$. Assume as known that

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[S_{n}=k_{n}\right](n p(1-p))^{1 / 2} \rightarrow \frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} e^{-x^{2} / 2} \tag{25.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

if $\left(k_{n}-n p\right)(n p(1-p))^{-1 / 2} \rightarrow x$. Deduce the DeMoivre-Laplace theorem: $\left(S_{n}\right. -n p)(n p(1-p))^{-1 / 2} \Rightarrow N$, where $N$ has the standard normal distribution. This is a special case of the central limit theorem; see Section 27.
25.12. Prove weak convergence in Example 25.3 by using Theorem 25.8 and the theory of the Riemann integral.
25.13. (a) Show that probability measures satisfy $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ if $\mu_{n}(a, b] \rightarrow \mu(a, b]$ whenever $\mu\{a\}=\mu\{b\}=0$.
(b) Show that, if $\int f d_{\mu_{n}} \rightarrow \int f d \mu$ for all continuous $f$ with bounded support, then $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$.
25.14. $\uparrow$ Let $\mu$ be Lebesgue measure confined to the unit interval; let $\mu_{n}$ correspond to a mass of $x_{n, i}-x_{n, i-1}$ at some point in ( $x_{n, i-1}, x_{n, i}$ ], where $0=x_{n 0} <x_{n 1}<\cdots<x_{n n}=1$. Show by considering the distribution functions that $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ if $\max _{i<n}\left(x_{n, i}-x_{n, i-1}\right) \rightarrow 0$. Deduce that a bounded Borel function continuous almost everywhere on the unit interval is Riemann integrable. See Problem 17.1.
25.15. $2.185 .19 \uparrow$ A function $f$ of positive integers has distribution function $F$ if $F$ is the weak limit of the distribution function $P_{n}[m: f(m) \leq x]$ of $f$ under the measure having probability $1 / n$ at each of $1, \ldots, n$ (see 2.34)). In this case $D[m: f(m) \leq x]=F(x)$ (see (2.35)) for continuity points $x$ of $F$. Show that $\varphi(m) / m$ (see (2.37)) has a distribution:
(a) Show by the mapping theorem that it suffices to prove that $f(m)= \log (\varphi(m) / m)=\sum_{p} \delta_{p}(m) \log (1-1 / p)$ has a distribution.
(b) Let $f_{u}(m)=\sum_{p \leq u} \delta_{p}(m) \log (1-1 / p)$, and show by (5.45) that $f_{u}$ has distribution function $F_{u}(x)=P\left[\sum_{p \leq u} X_{p} \log (1-1 / p) \leq x\right]$, where the $X_{p}$ are independent random variables (one for each prime $p$ ) such that $P\left[X_{p}=1\right]= 1 / p$ and $P\left[X_{p}=0\right]=1-1 / p$.
(c) Show that $\sum_{p} X_{p} \log (1-1 / p)$ converges with probability 1 . Hint: Use Theorem 22.6.
(d) Show that $\lim _{u \rightarrow \infty} \sup _{n} E_{n}\left[\left|f-f_{u}\right|\right]=0$ (see (5.46) for the notation).
(e) Conclude by Markov's inequality and Theorem 25.5 that $f$ has the distribution of the sum in (c).
25.16. For $A \in \mathscr{R}^{1}$ and $T>0$, put $\lambda_{T}(A)=\lambda([-T, T] \cap A) / 2 T$, where $\lambda$ is Lebesgue measure. The relative measure of $A$ is

$$
\begin{equation*}
\rho(A)=\lim _{T \rightarrow \infty} \lambda_{T}(A) \tag{25.17}
\end{equation*}
$$

provided that this limit exists. This is a continuous analogue of density (see (2.35)) for sets of integers. A Borel function $f$ has a distribution under $\lambda_{T}$; if this converges weakly to $F$, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\rho[x: f(x) \leq u]=F(u) \tag{25.18}
\end{equation*}
$$

for continuity points $u$ of $F$, and $F$ is called the distribution function of $f$. Show that all periodic functions have distributions.
25.17. Suppose that $\sup _{n} \int f d \mu_{n}<\infty$ for a nonnegative $f$ such that $f(x) \rightarrow \infty$ as $x \rightarrow \pm \infty$. Show that $\left(\mu_{n}\right)$ is tight.
25.18. $23.4 \uparrow$ Show that the random variables $A_{r}$ and $L_{r}$ in Problems 23.3 and 23.4 converge in distribution. Show that the moments converge.
25.19. In the applications of Theorem 9.2, only a weaker result is actually needed: For each $K$ there exists a positive $\alpha=\alpha(K)$ such that if $E[X]=0, E\left[X^{2}\right]=1$, and $E\left[X^{4}\right] \leq K$, then $P[X \geq 0] \geq \alpha$. Prove this by using tightness and the corollary to Theorem 25.12.
25.20. Find uniformly integrable random variables $X_{n}$ for which there is no integrable $Z$ satisfying $P\left[\left|X_{n}\right| \geq t\right] \leq P[|Z| \geq t]$ for $t>0$.

## SECTION 26. CHARACTERISTIC FUNCTIONS

## Definition

The characteristic function of a probability measure $\mu$ on the line is defined for real $t$ by

$$
\begin{aligned}
\varphi(t) & =\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{i t x} \mu(d x) \\
& =\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \cos t x \mu(d x)+i \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \sin t x \mu(d x)
\end{aligned}
$$

see the end of Section 16 for integrals of complex-valued functions. ${ }^{\dagger}$ A random variable $X$ with distribution $\mu$ has characteristic function

$$
\varphi(t)=E\left[e^{i t X}\right]=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{i t x} \mu(d x)
$$

The characteristic function is thus defined as the moment generating function but with the real argument $s$ replaced by it; it has the advantage that it always exists because $e^{i t x}$ is bounded. The characteristic function in nonprobabilistic contexts is called the Fourier transform.

The characteristic function has three fundamental properties to be established here:
(i) If $\mu_{1}$ and $\mu_{2}$ have respective characteristic functions $\varphi_{1}(t)$ and $\varphi_{2}(t)$, then $\mu_{1} * \mu_{2}$ has characteristic function $\varphi_{1}(t) \varphi_{2}(t)$. Although convolution is essential to the study of sums of independent random variables, it is a complicated operation, and it is often simpler to study the products of the corresponding characteristic functions.
(ii) The characteristic function uniquely determines the distribution. This shows that in studying the products in (i), no information is lost.
(iii) From the pointwise convergence of characteristic functions follows the weak convergence of the corresponding distributions. This makes it possible, for example, to investigate the asymptotic distributions of sums of independent random variables by means of their characteristic functions.

## Moments and Derivatives

It is convenient first to study the relation between a characteristic function and the moments of the distribution it comes from.

[^14]Of course, $\varphi(0)=1$, and by $(16.30),|\varphi(t)| \leq 1$ for all $t$. By Theorem 16.8(i), $\varphi(t)$ is continuous in $t$. In fact, $|\varphi(t+h)-\varphi(t)| \leq \int\left|e^{i h x}-1\right| \mu(d x)$, and so it follows by the bounded convergence theorem that $\varphi(t)$ is uniformly continuous.

In the following relations, versions of Taylor's formula with remainder, $x$ is assumed real. Integration by parts shows that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{0}^{x}(x-s)^{n} e^{i s} d s=\frac{x^{n+1}}{n+1}+\frac{i}{n+1} \int_{0}^{x}(x-s)^{n+1} e^{t s} d s \tag{26.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

and it follows by induction that

$$
\begin{equation*}
e^{i x}=\sum_{k=0}^{n} \frac{(i x)^{k}}{k!}+\frac{i^{n+1}}{n!} \int_{0}^{\lambda}(x-s)^{n} e^{i s} d s \tag{26.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

for $n \geq 0$. Replace $n$ by $n-1$ in (26.1), solve for the integral on the right, and substitute this for the integral in (26.2); this gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
e^{i x}=\sum_{k=0}^{n} \frac{(i x)^{k}}{k!}+\frac{i^{n}}{(n-1)!} \int_{0}^{x}(x-s)^{n-1}\left(e^{i s}-1\right) d s \tag{26.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

Estimating the integrals in (26.2) and (26.3) (consider separately the cases $x \geq 0$ and $x<0$ ) now leads to

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left|e^{i x}-\sum_{k=0}^{n} \frac{(i x)^{k}}{k!}\right| \leq \min \left\{\frac{|x|^{n+1}}{(n+1)!}, \frac{2|x|^{n}}{n!}\right\} \tag{26.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

for $n \geq 0$. The first term on the right gives a sharp estimate for $|x|$ small, the second a sharp estimate for $|x|$ large. For $n=0,1,2$, the inequality specializes to

$$
\begin{align*}
\left|e^{i x}-1\right| & \leq \min \{|x|, 2\}  \tag{0}\\
\left|e^{i x}-(1+i x)\right| & \leq \min \left\{\frac{1}{2} x^{2}, 2|x|\right\}  \tag{1}\\
\left|e^{i x}-\left(1+i x-\frac{1}{2} x^{2}\right)\right| & \leq \min \left\{\frac{1}{6}|x|^{3}, x^{2}\right\} \tag{2}
\end{align*}
$$

If $X$ has a moment of order $n$, it follows that

$$
\begin{equation*}
