\left|\varphi(t)-\sum_{k=0}^{n} \frac{(i t)^{k}}{k!} E\left[X^{k}\right]\right| \leq E\left[\min \left\{\frac{|t X|^{n+1}}{(n+1)!}, \frac{2|t X|^{n}}{n!}\right\}\right] . \tag{26.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

For any $t$ satisfying

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} \frac{|t|^{n} E\left[|X|^{n}\right]}{n!}=0, \tag{26.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

$\varphi(t)$ must therefore have the expansion

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi(t)=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{(i t)^{k}}{k!} E\left[X^{k}\right] \tag{26.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

compare (21.22). If

$$
\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{|t|^{k}}{k!} E\left[|X|^{k}\right]=E\left[\epsilon^{\mid t X_{i}}\right]<\infty,
$$

then (see (16.31)) (26.7) must hold. Thus (26.7) holds if $X$ has a moment generating function over the whole line.

Example 26.1. Since $E\left[e^{|1 X|}\right]<\infty$ if $X$ has the standard normal distribution, by (26.7) and (21.7) its characteristic function is

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi(t)=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{(i t)^{2 k}}{(2 k)!} 1 \times 3 \times \cdots \times(2 k-1)=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{k!}\left(-\frac{t^{2}}{2}\right)^{k}=e^{-t^{2} i^{2}} . \tag{26.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

This and (21.25) formally coincide if $s=i t$. $\square$

If the power-series expansion (26.7) holds, the moments of $X$ can be read off from it:

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi^{(k)}(0)=i^{k} E\left[X^{k}\right] . \tag{26.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

This is the analogue of (21.23). It holds, however, under the weakest possible assumption, namely that $E\left[\left|X^{k}\right|\right]<\infty$. Indeed,

$$
\frac{\varphi(t+h)-\varphi(t)}{h}-E\left[i X e^{i t X}\right]=E\left[e^{i t X} \frac{e^{i h X}-1-i h X}{h}\right] .
$$

By ( $26.4_{1}$ ), the integrand on the right is dominated by $2|X|$ and goes to 0 with $h$; hence the expected value goes to 0 by the dominated convergence theorem. Thus $\varphi^{\prime}(t)=E\left[i X e^{i t X}\right]$. Repeating this argument inductively gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi^{(k)}(t)=E\left[(i X)^{k} e^{i t X}\right] \tag{26.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

if $E\left[\left|X^{k}\right|\right]<\infty$. Hence (26.9) holds if $E\left[\left|X^{k}\right|\right]<\infty$. The proof of uniform continuity for $\varphi(t)$ works for $\varphi^{(k)}(t)$ as well.

If $E\left[X^{2}\right]$ is finite, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi(t)=1+i t E[X]-\frac{1}{2} t^{2} E\left[X^{2}\right]+o\left(t^{2}\right), \quad t \rightarrow 0 . \tag{26.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

Indeed, by ( $26.4_{2}$ ), the error is at most $t^{2} E\left[\min \left\{|t||X|^{3}, X^{2}\right\}\right]$, and as $t \rightarrow 0$ the integrand goes to 0 and is dominated by $X^{2}$. Estimates of this kind are essential for proving limit theorems.

The more moments $\mu$ has, the more derivatives $\varphi$ has. This is one sense in which lightness of the tails of $\mu$ is reflected by smoothness of $\varphi$. There are results which connect the behavior of $\varphi(t)$ as $|t| \rightarrow \infty$ with smoothness properties of $\mu$. The Riemann-Lebesgue theorem is the most important of these:

Theorem 26.1. If $\mu$ has a density, then $\varphi(t) \rightarrow 0$ as $|t| \rightarrow \infty$.
Proof. The problem is to prove for integrable $f$ that $f f(x) e^{i t x} d x \rightarrow 0$ as $|t| \rightarrow \infty$. There exists by Theorem 17.1 a step function $g=\sum_{k} \alpha_{k} I_{A_{k}}$, a finite linear combination of indicators of intervals $A_{k}=\left(a_{k}, b_{k}\right]$, for which $\int \mid f- g \mid d x<\epsilon$. Now $\int f(x) e^{i t x} d x$ differs by at most $\epsilon$ from $\int g(x) e^{i t x} d x= \sum_{k} \alpha_{k}\left(e^{i t b_{k}}-e^{i t a_{k}}\right) / i t$, and this goes to 0 as $|t| \rightarrow \infty$. $\square$

## Independence

The multiplicative property (21.28) of moment generating functions extends to characteristic functions. Suppose that $X_{1}$ and $X_{2}$ are independent random variables with characteristic functions $\varphi_{1}$ and $\varphi_{2}$. If $Y_{j}=\cos X_{j}$ and $Z_{j}= \sin t X_{j}$, then ( $Y_{1}, Z_{1}$ ) and ( $Y_{2}, Z_{2}$ ) are independent; by the rules for integrating complex-valued functions,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\varphi_{1}(t) \varphi_{2}(t)= & \left(E\left[Y_{1}\right]+i E\left[Z_{1}\right]\right)\left(E\left[Y_{2}\right]+i E\left[Z_{2}\right]\right) \\
= & E\left[Y_{1}\right] E\left[Y_{2}\right]-E\left[Z_{1}\right] E\left[Z_{2}\right] \\
& +i\left(E\left[Y_{1}\right] E\left[Z_{2}\right]+E\left[Z_{1}\right] E\left[Y_{2}\right]\right) \\
= & E\left[Y_{1} Y_{2}-Z_{1} Z_{2}+i\left(Y_{1} Z_{2}+Z_{1} Y_{2}\right)\right]=E\left[e^{i t\left(X_{1}+X_{2}\right)}\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

This extends to sums of three or more: If $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ are independent, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[e^{i t \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}}\right]=\prod_{k=1}^{n} E\left[e^{i t X_{k}}\right] . \tag{26.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $X$ has characteristic function $\varphi(t)$, then $a X+b$ has characteristic function

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[e^{l t(a X+b)}\right]=e^{i t b} \varphi(a t) \tag{26.13}
\end{equation*}
$$

In particular, $-X$ has characteristic function $\varphi(-t)$, which is the complex conjugate of $\varphi(t)$.

## Inversion and the Uniqueness Theorem

A characteristic function $\varphi$ uniquely determines the measure $\mu$ it comes from. This fundamental fact will be derived by means of an inversion formula through which $\mu$ can in principle be recovered from $\varphi$.

Define

$$
S(T)=\int_{0}^{T} \frac{\sin x}{x} d x, \quad T \geq 0
$$

In Example 18.4 it is shown that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{T \rightarrow \infty} S(T)=\frac{\pi}{2} \tag{26.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

$S(T)$ is therefore bounded. If $\operatorname{sgn} \theta$ is $+1,0$, or -1 as $\theta$ is positive, 0 , or negative, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{0}^{T} \frac{\sin t \theta}{t} d t=\operatorname{sgn} \theta \cdot S(T|\theta|), \quad T \geq 0 \tag{26.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

Theorem 26.2. If the probability measure $\mu$ has characteristic function $\varphi$, and if $\mu\{a\}=\mu\{b\}=0$, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu(a, b]=\lim _{T \rightarrow \infty} \frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{-T}^{T} \frac{e^{-i t a}-e^{-i t b}}{i t} \varphi(t) d t \tag{26.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

Distinct measures cannot have the same characteristic function.
Note: By ( 26.4 ) the integrand here converges as $t \rightarrow 0$ to $b-a$, which is to be taken as its value for $t=0$. For fixed $a$ and $b$ the integrand is thus continuous in $t$, and by ( $26.4_{0}$ ) it is bounded. If $\mu$ is a unit mass at 0 , then $\varphi(t) \equiv 1$ and the integral in (26.16) cannot be extended over the whole line.

Proof. The inversion formula will imply uniqueness: It will imply that if $\mu$ and $\nu$ have the same characteristic function, then $\mu(a, b]=\nu(a, b]$ if $\mu\{a\}=\nu\{a\}=\mu\{b\}=\nu\{b\}=0$; but such intervals ( $a, b$ ] form a $\pi$-system generating $\mathscr{P}^{1}$,

Denote by $I_{T}$ the quantity inside the limit in (26.16). By Fubini's theorem

$$
\begin{equation*}
I_{T}=\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\left[\int_{-T}^{T} \frac{e^{i t(x-a)}-e^{i t(x-b)}}{i t} d t\right] \mu(d x) \tag{26,17}
\end{equation*}
$$

This interchange is legitimate because the double integral extends over a set of finite product measure and by $\left(26.4_{0}\right)$ the integrand is bounded by $|b-a|$. Rewrite the integrand by DeMoivre's formula. Since $\sin s$ and $\cos s$ are odd and even, respectively, (26.15) gives

$$
I_{T}=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\left[\frac{\operatorname{sgn}(x-a)}{\pi} S(T \cdot|x-a|)-\frac{\operatorname{sgn}(x-b)}{\pi} S(T \cdot|x-b|)\right] \mu(d x) .
$$

The integrand here is bounded and converges as $T \rightarrow \infty$ to the function

$$
\psi_{a, b}(x)= \begin{cases}0 & \text { if } x<a  \tag{26.18}\\ \frac{1}{2} & \text { if } x=a \\ 1 & \text { if } a<x<b \\ \frac{1}{2} & \text { if } x=b \\ 0 & \text { if } b<x\end{cases}
$$

Thus $I_{T} \rightarrow \int \psi_{a, b} d \mu$, which implies that (26.16) holds if $\mu\{a\}=\mu\{b\}=0$. $\square$

The inversion formula contains further information. Suppose that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}|\varphi(t)| d t<\infty . \tag{26.19}
\end{equation*}
$$

In this case the integral in (26.16) can be extended over $R^{1}$. By (26.40),

$$
\left|\frac{e^{-i t b}-e^{-i t a}}{i t}\right|=\frac{\left|e^{i t(b-a)}-1\right|}{|t|} \leq|b-a| ;
$$

therefore, $\mu(a, b) \leq(b-a) \int_{-\infty}^{\infty}|\varphi(t)| d t$, and there can be no point masses. By (26.16), the corresponding distribution function satisfies

$$
\frac{F(x+h)-F(x)}{h}=\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{e^{-i t x}-e^{-i t(x+h)}}{i t h} \varphi(t) d t
$$

(whether $h$ is positive or negative). The integrand is by $\left.(26.4)_{0}\right)$ dominated by $|\varphi(t)|$ and goes to $e^{i t x} \varphi(t)$ as $h \rightarrow 0$. Therefore, $F$ has derivative

$$
\begin{equation*}
f(x)=\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-i t x} \varphi(t) d t \tag{26.20}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $f$ is continuous for the same reason $\varphi$ is, it integrates to $F$ by the fundamental theorem of the calcuius (see $(17,6)$ ). Thus (26.19) implies that $\mu$ has the continuous density (26.20). Moreover, this is the only continuous density. In this result, as in the Riemann-Lebesgue theorem, conditions on the size of $\varphi(t)$ for large $|t|$ are connected with smoothness properties of $\mu$.

The inversion formula (26.20) has many applications. In the first place, it can be used for a new derivation of (26.14). As pointed out in Example 17.3, the existence of the limit in (26.14) is easy to prove. Denote this limit temporarily by $\pi_{0} / 2$-without assuming that $\pi_{0}=\pi$. Then (26.16) and (26.20) follow as before if $\pi$ is replaced by $\pi_{0}$. Appiying the latter to the standard normal density (see (26.8)) gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} e^{-x^{2} / 2}=\frac{1}{2 \pi_{0}} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-i t x} e^{-t^{2} / 2} d t, \tag{26.21}
\end{equation*}
$$

where the $\pi$ on the left is that of analysis and geometry-it comes ultimately from the quadrature (18.10). An application of (26.8) with $x$ and $t$ interchanged reduces the right side of (26.21) to $\left(\sqrt{2 \pi} / 2 \pi_{0}\right) e^{-x^{2} / 2}$, and therefore $\pi_{0}$ does equal $\pi$.

Consider the densities in the table. The characteristic function for the normal distribution has already been calculated. For the uniform distribution over $(0,1)$, the computation is of course straightforward; note that in this case the density cannot be recovered from (26.20), because $\varphi(t)$ is not integrable; this is reflected in the fact that the density has discontinuities at 0 and 1 .

| Distribution | Density | Interval | Characteristic Function |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| 1. Normal | $\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} e^{-x^{2} / 2}$ | $-\infty<x<\infty$ | $e^{-t^{2} / 2}$ |
| 2. Uniform | 1 | $0<x<1$ | $\frac{e^{i t}-1}{i t}$ |
| 3. Exponential | $e^{-x}$ | $0<x<\infty$ | $\frac{1}{1-i t}$ |
| 4. Double exponential or Laplace | $\frac{1}{2} e^{-\|x\|}$ | $-\infty<x<\infty$ | $\frac{1}{1+t^{2}}$ |
| 5. Cauchy | $\frac{1}{\pi} \frac{1}{1+x^{2}}$ | $-\infty<x<\infty$ | $e^{-\|t\|}$ |
| 6. Triangular | $1-\|x\|$ | $-1<x<1$ | $2 \frac{1-\cos t}{t^{2}}$ |
| 7. | $\frac{1}{\pi} \frac{1-\cos x}{x^{2}}$ | $-\infty<x<\infty$ | $(1-\|t\|) I_{(-1,1)}(t)$ |

The characteristic function for the exponential distribution is easily calculated; compare Example 21.3. As for the double exponential or Laplace distribution, $e^{-|x|} e^{i t x}$ integrates over $(0, \infty)$ to $(1-i t)^{-1}$ and over $(-\infty, 0)$ to $(1+i t)^{-1}$, which gives the result. By (26.20), then,

$$
e^{-|x|}=\frac{1}{\pi} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-i t x} \frac{d t}{1+t^{2}}
$$

For $x=0$ this gives the standard integral $\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} d t /\left(1+t^{2}\right)=\pi$; see Example 17.5. Thus the Cauchy density in the table integrates to 1 and has characteristic function $e^{-|t|}$. This distribution has no first moment, and the characteristic function is not differentiable at the origin.

A straightforward integration shows that the triangular density has the characteristic function given in the table, and by (26.20),

$$
(1-|x|) I_{(-1,1)}(x)=\frac{1}{\pi} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-i t x} \frac{1-\cos t}{t^{2}} d t
$$

For $x=0$ this is $\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}(1-\cos t) t^{-2} d t=\pi$; hence the last line of the table.
Each density and characteristic function in the table can be transformed by (26.13), which gives a family of distributions.

## The Continuity Theorem

Because of (26.12), the characteristic function provides a powerful means of studying the distributions of sums of independent random variables. It is often easier to work with products of characteristic functions than with convolutions, and knowing the characteristic function of the sum is by Theorem 26.2 in principle the same thing as knowing the distribution itself. Because of the following continuity theorem, characteristic functions can be used to study limit distributions.

Theorem 26.3. Let $\mu_{n}, \mu$ be probability measures with characteristic functions $\varphi_{n}, \varphi$. A necessary and sufficient condition for $\mu_{n} \Rightarrow \mu$ is that $\varphi_{n}(t) \rightarrow \varphi(t)$ for each $t$.

Proof. Necessity. For each $t, e^{i t x}$ has bounded modulus and is continuous in $x$. The necessity therefore follows by an application of Theorem 25.8 (to the real and imaginary parts of $e^{i t x}$ ).

Then $A(\epsilon, \delta)$ is open and $D_{h}=\cup_{\epsilon} \cap_{\delta} A(\epsilon, \delta)$, where $\epsilon$ and $\delta$ range over the positive rationals.
